The author of this book says: When I have completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is a part of the science of medicine in its completeness, and have reached the utmost limit in its clarity and explanation, I saw fit to complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the practical work.
Because practical work is completely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that its knowledge is almost extinct and its influence has ceased. And only a few traces of it remain in the books of the ancients, which have been corrupted by hands and marred by mistakes and confusion, until its meanings have become obscure and its usefulness has been lost.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and to compose this treatise on it in a way that is explanatory, clear, and concise, and to provide pictures of cauterizing irons and other surgical instruments, since this is a further clarification and a great help to those who need it.
The reason why there is no skilled practitioner in our time is that the craft of medicine is long and its practitioner must beforehand be trained in the science of anatomy which Galen described. Until he understands the functions, forms, temperaments, connections, and separations of the organs, and the knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, their number, origins, the arteries and veins, their pulsations, and the locations of their origins.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of surgery, and we have mentioned some of this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable in what we have mentioned of anatomy will inevitably make mistakes that kill people, as I have often witnessed in those who have imagined themselves proficient in this science and claimed it without knowledge or training.
For I saw an ignorant physician who incised a porcine tumor in a woman's neck, and severed some of the neck's arteries, so the woman bled until she fell dead before him.
And I saw another physician who proceeded to remove a stone from a man who had been stabbed in the bladder; the stone was large, so he acted rashly and removed it with a piece of the bladder's substance. So the man died after about three days, and I had been called to remove it; and I saw from the size of the stone and the condition of the patient that I could have done so.
And I saw another physician who earned his living practicing medicine amongst some of the brothel keepers of our city.
Then a black boy came to him with a fracture in his leg near the heel, together with a wound.  The physician, in his ignorance, hastily bound the fracture over the wound with bandages and splints very tightly, and left no room for the wound to breathe.
Then he gave free rein to his desires, and left him for several days, ordering him not to untie the bandage until his leg and foot swelled and he was on the verge of death.
So I was called to him, and I quickly untied the bandage; then he found relief and was freed from his pains, except that the corruption had become firmly established in the limb, and I was unable to check it.
And the corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
And I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, which ulcerated after several days, until the suffering of its owner became great. This is because if a cancer is purely of a black humor, then it should never be treated with an iron instrument at all.
Unless it is in a limb that can be entirely excised.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual operations are divided into two parts: an operation accompanied by safety, and an operation accompanied by harm in most cases.
And I have warned in every place in this book where such dangerous and risky procedures occur, and you should avoid and reject them.
Lest the ignorant find a way to criticize and attack, take for yourselves caution and circumspection, and for your patients gentleness and deliberation.
And use the best method leading to safety and a favorable outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure diseases.
And purify yourselves from that which you fear might cast doubt on your religion and your worldly life, for this is more lasting for your reputation and higher in this world and the hereafter for your status.
For Galen said in some of his precepts: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become a bad physician.
This treatise is divided into three sections.
The first section.
Concerning cauterization with fire and cauterization with strong medicine, methodically ordered from head to foot, with images of the instruments and cautery irons and everything needed for the work by hand.
And the second section.
Concerning incision, puncture, bloodletting, cupping, wounds, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all methodically ordered with images of the instruments.
And the third section.
Concerning setting, reduction, and the treatment of dislocations and the like, methodically ordered from head to foot, with images of the instruments.
The first section.
On cauterization.
And before we mention its use, it is necessary that we mention the manner of its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
So I say that the discussion of the manner of the benefit of cauterization and its harms is a lengthy discussion and a precise science and a hidden secret, and a number of wise men have spoken about it.
And they differed in it, and I have shortened from their speech a little for fear of lengthening.
So I say that cauterization generally benefits every bad temperament that is with matter and without matter, except for two temperaments, which are the hot temperament without matter and the dry temperament without matter.
As for the dry temperament with matter, they differed in it.  Some of them said that cauterization is beneficial in it, and others said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable in a disease that is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
And it is impossible to be cured from a hot dry disease with a hot dry medicine. And he who says the opposite said that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot dry disease that occurs in the bodies of people. Because when you add the body of man and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the body of man cold.
And I say as he says, because experience has revealed this to me many times, except that one should not imagine that matter except one who has trained and practiced in the field of cauterization a thorough training. And he has understood the difference in the temperaments of people and the state of diseases in themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and their duration.
As for the other temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially cold and moist diseases. All physicians have agreed on them and have not differed in the benefit of cauterization in them.
And know, O my son, that one of the secrets of cauterization with fire and its superiority over cauterization with a burning drug is that fire is a simple element whose effect does not exceed the member that has been cauterized, and it does not harm another connected member except with a slight harm.
And cauterization with a burning drug may extend its effect to other members, and it may cause a disease in the member that is difficult to treat, and it may even kill. Fire, because of its nobility and the excellence of its essence, does not do that except if one overdoes it.
This has become clear to us through experience, long service, attention to the craft, and understanding the realities of the matter.
For this reason I have dispensed with lengthy discourse. Were it not unbecoming of this book of mine, I would have presented to you concerning fire a hidden secret and the manner of its action in bodies and its removal of diseases with philosophical, demonstrative discourse too subtle for your understanding.
And know, O my sons, that they have differed concerning the time suitable for cauterization, and they have considered the best time to be springtime.
And I say that cauterization is suitable at any time, because the harm caused by the time is consumed by the benefit which is brought about by cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for strong, necessary, urgent pains which do not tolerate delay, because of the fear that what follows may be a calamity greater than the slight harm caused by the time.
And let it not occur to you, O my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization which cures a disease will never recur, and that you should make it a prohibition.
And the matter is not as they imagine, because cauterization is only like a medicine which changes the temperament and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
Except that cauterization is preferable to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
And it is possible that the disease may return after a time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength of his disease, and the accumulation of humors in his body, and his negligence in acquiring them from foods and similar causes.
Except that the disease in which cauterization is used is a mild disease and in a member with few humors and moistures. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like, for it is possible that that pain will not return, and that is in the least.
And as for the common people's saying also that cauterization is the last resort of medicine, it is a correct saying, not to what they go to, for they believe that no treatment is beneficial by medicine or otherwise after cauterization has taken place, and the matter is contrary to that.
And the meaning of cauterization being the last resort of medicine is only that when we use various treatments for a disease and those medicines do not work, then we use cauterization as a last resort, and it works; from here it is said that cauterization is the last resort of medicine. Not in the sense that the common people and many ignorant physicians went to.
The ancients mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said that because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and they said that it does not suppurate the place of cauterization, and that is not absolutely the case.
Because I have tried it and found that it only does that in some bodies and not others, and cauterization with it is better and more excellent than with iron, as they said, except that if you heat the cautery iron in the fire made of gold, you cannot determine when it is heated to the degree you want because of the redness of the gold.
And because it quickly gets cold, and if you increase the heat, it melts in the fire and sticks, so the craftsman is busy with that; therefore, cauterization with iron is, for us, quicker and closer to the correct practice, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cauterization in sections that I have organized from head to foot, to make it easier for the student to find what he wants, God willing.
Chapter One.
Cauterization of the head, one cauterization.
This cauterization benefits from the predominance of moisture and coldness in the brain, which are the cause of headache, and frequent colds from the head to the area of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, and toothache and throat pain. And in short, for every illness that arises from coldness, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and catalepsy, and similar illnesses.
The image of this cauterization is that you first order the patient to vomit using a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, his age, and his habit.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him before you cross-legged, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then you place the base of your palm on the root of his nose between his eyes; where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery, which is of this form.
Expose the bone to the extent of the head of a clove or the size of a carob seed; then raise your hand, or else replace your hand with the same instrument or another if it has cooled. Until you see of the bone what I have mentioned to you; then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a cotton swab in it, place it on the spot, and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter to it and leave it there until the eschar from the cautery separates; then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they have said that the longer a wound remains open, the better and more useful it is for the pus to drain.
And some have mentioned cauterizing the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the thickness of the bone is burned, then removing what has been burned from the bone, and then treating it.
And others said that it is necessary to exaggerate with the cautery until it strongly affects the bone, until it falls from the bone like a carat or a small flake, and they claimed that this vents the vapors of the head from that place and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then treat it until it heals.  I do not see these two types of cautery at all except in some people and in a haphazard way, and leaving it alone is better for me and safer. If it is done, then the head weakens when its natural connection is broken, as we have seen in other members.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it, so act according to it and you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
Also on cauterizing the head.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and it persists for the patient, and poultices, collyria, snuffs, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if a single cauterization as we described has been performed and none of that has helped him, then look. If the patient's head is naturally strong and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize him again a little above the previous cauterization. Then cauterize each temple of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone we described is exposed. And cauterize him in the back of his head in the place known as the hatchet, and lighten your hand in this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will find this very painful, unlike the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head, and I will mention this cauterization in its place.
The cautery with which the temples and the back of the head are cauterized should be gentler than the cautery with which the middle of the head is cauterized.  This is its form.
Chapter Three.
On cauterizing non-chronic migraine.
If pain occurs in a fissure of the head along with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then let the patient vomit with purgative medicines for the head, and use all the treatment that I mentioned in the classifications of diseases. If that does not work, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, it is that you take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides, then incise the place of pain in the temple with a wide scalpel until there is a space under the skin that can accommodate the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears, then bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it for about fifteen hours, then unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Dress it with cotton soaked in butter until the place suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. And if you wish, do this with some of the burning medicines that I gave in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is in this manner: heat the cautery, whose image is this:
And it is called the nail cautery because its head is like the shape of a nail, with some perforation and a small projection in its middle.
Then you place it on the painful area and hold your hand while you rotate the iron little by little, and the amount you burn should be half the thickness of the skin. And lift your hand so that you do not burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding, then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area and leave it for three days. Then you dress the cotton with ointment, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this migraine with the knife-like end of the cautery, and this preserves from cutting the artery in this chronic migraine especially.
Chapter Four.
Cauterization of chronic migraine.
If you treat the migraine with what we have mentioned of the preceding treatment and what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and you see from the illness something that what we mentioned of the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire does not overcome. Then it is necessary that you heat the knife-like cautery until it whitens after you mark the place of the pain with a line the length of half a finger or so, and you leave your hand once while you pull it until you cut the artery and reach toward the bone.
Except that it is necessary for you to be cautious of the contact of the jaw which moves upon chewing, lest you burn the muscle or the nerve that moves it, and thus cause spasm.
And be cautious and watchful of bleeding from the artery which you cut, for in its cutting there is danger. Especially for one who is ignorant of what he does and has not been a trained and experienced person; and leaving the work is better.
And the treatment of bleeding which occurs from the artery will be mentioned in its place in the book, God willing; but if you see from the illness something which this cautery does not cure,
and you see the body of the patient able to bear it, cauterize him in the middle of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cautery which we mentioned in the chapter on the incision of the artery with the double-bladed cautery, for it is a better and more effective cautery than this.
Chapter Five.
In cauterizing the pains of the ears.
If a pain occurs in the ear due to cold and it is treated with laxatives and all the treatments that we mentioned in the classification and the pain does not go away, then heat the cautery which is called the point, whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, apply it with dots around the whole ear, or around both if the pain is in both. And keep the cauterization a little away from the root of the ear, after marking the place with ink. The cauterization should be about ten points in each ear, or thereabouts. Then treat the places until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
Cauterization of the tonsils.
The tonsils treated by cauterization are of the type caused by phlegm, as I mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
And avoid cauterizing the type that occurs from dryness and nerve spasms.
When you have treated this type of tonsil condition with applications, snuffs, and gargles, and your treatment has not been effective, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cautery on the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The image of the cauterization is that you cauterize with a cautery opposite the upper tip of the ear, slightly below the horn of the head, and another in the temple, its length being the length of the thumb.
Lower your hand with the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin.  This is the image of the cautery; it is a type of knife, the image of which has been presented earlier, but it is slightly gentler, as you see. The knife should be slightly thicker than necessary. Then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
If the apoplexy becomes chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned and your treatment is ineffective, and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize in four places. One cauterization on each temple, one in the middle of the head as previously mentioned, and one at the back of the head as before.
The description of the cauterizations is as previously mentioned.  It may also be cauterized on the stomach's opening, which will be more effective, then treat as previously mentioned.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterizing forgetfulness.
If it is from phlegm, the patient should first be given strong purgatives and pills that cleanse the brain.
Then his whole head should be shaved and a mustard plaster, described in the chapter on plasters, should be applied to the back of his head repeatedly, for it is a kind of cauterization.
Do this in exactly the same order as I described there. If he is cured by this, well and good; otherwise, perform three cauterizations on the back of his head, arranged in a row from the top of the head to the bottom of the neck.
Make the distance between each cauterization the width of a finger.  Then treat the cauterization as described above. If you wish to increase [the treatment] and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle again.
If you wish to increase [the treatment], cauterize the temples, then treat it until it heals. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as described previously.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterizing paralysis and general bodily relaxation.
It is necessary that you first purify the head with purgatives and as we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, then cauterize: one cauterization in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in the case of general bodily relaxation, you need more and the patient can tolerate it and the disease is strong and persistent, then cauterize four times on the vertebrae of his back. And continue cauterizing until you burn most of the skin, then lift your hand and then treat him as previously mentioned until he recovers, God willing. And let the cautery be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterizing epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is caused by phlegm should be cauterized.  It is necessary to first cleanse his brain with strong purgatives and with all the other treatments which we have mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and able to take medicines.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, let him use gargles and masticatories which cleanse the brain many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then his head is shaved, and one cauterization is applied in the middle of the head, as described above, and another cauterization at the back of his head. And one cauterization on each temple of his head. If the patient is strong and able to tolerate it, apply the cauterizations which were mentioned for the paralytic and for those with muscle relaxation, on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.  The cautery should be olive-shaped, as described above.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, apply the cauterizations which we mentioned for the paralytic.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess tending towards black bile and the patient's body is moist, purge him so as to cleanse his brain, as explained previously in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a firm cake of linen like a circle, then place it in the middle of his head, the patient sitting cross-legged holding it from each side, then take one pound of old sheep fat.
Then heat it over a fire with moderate heat, as much as the finger can bear when inserted, then pour it into the middle of his head in the circle and leave it until it cools.  Do this for the patient once a week along with all other good treatment until he recovers, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures, without holding your hand to the cautery, but rather by sprinkling; for this type of cautery moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton wool compress soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing a running eye.
If you ascertain the onset of a running eye by the signs mentioned in the classification, then act quickly; purge the patient, cleanse his head and protect it from all moisture, and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and apply a cautery to the middle of the head, then two cauteries to the temples if the running is in both eyes, or to one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries under the skin; let the cauteries be lengthwise across the temples, and guard against bleeding.
If you see any of it, cut it immediately with any treatment available to you.  We shall discuss the wisdom of cutting arteries and veins and guarding against bleeding.
And two strong cauteries may be applied to the nape of the neck below the bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
On cauterizing chronic weeping sores.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and are from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, outside the head, and you are certain that it is from a cold, thick, phlegmatic excess, then cauterize it with the cautery described at the beginning for the treatment of the descending water. One cautery in the middle of the head, and two cauteries on the temples, and two cauteries on the nape of the neck below the bones; and if you need more, then cauterize one on each side of the corner of the eye at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing the foul smell of the nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the classification and the treatment is not effective, then quickly make the patient fast on quince for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle with the olive-shaped cautery iron. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery iron two cauteries above the eyebrows, a little below the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it.  And this is the image of the nail-shaped [cautery iron].
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing the relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid relaxes from illness or dampness, cauterize the eyelid with one cauterization using this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize a little above the eyebrows, two cauterizations on each side, and keep them distant from the temples, and let the length of each cauterization be the length of the eyebrow.
And do not exaggerate your hand with the cauterization, but only to the extent that a third of the skin is burnt, and the shape of the cautery iron is like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its lids are inverted inwards so that they prick the eye, then cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medicine. As for cauterization with fire, you should order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelids alone, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and become even.
If it pricks him while they are growing, then tie his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow. When they have grown and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, then mark on the eyelid with ink a sign in the shape of an upside-down letter "S".
The beginning of the mark should be near the eyelids. Then place under the eyelid cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard. Then heat a cautery iron, this is its shape.
Then cauterize according to the mark you made, little by little, in many times, until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the upside-down letter "S", is completely burnt, especially its surface. A sign that your work is correct is to see the eyelid curl and the hair rise from the flesh of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days. Then apply cotton with butter to it until the scab falls off. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If some of the hair returns after a time and the eyelid relaxes,
repeat the cauterization in that place as you did before. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and the hair does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a burning drug, you should order the patient to leave the eyelids until they grow long and even.
Then make from paper the shape of an upside-down letter S. Then take from the usual soap and from unslaked lime, of each one a drachm weight or so. Crush them all well together, and do this quickly lest it dry. Then spread some of it on the paper you made in the shape of the upside-down letter S, and place it on one or both eyelids. Place cotton soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger over the medication and hold it down slightly, while moving it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medication, for he will feel a burning sensation. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medication and move it with your finger. When the burning subsides, remove the medication and wash the eye with water.
And observe: if you see that the eyelid has risen as it rises when cauterized with fire or by cutting, then do not repeat the medication on the area where the medication has not taken effect and not blackened until you complete your work and the eyelid is cauterized, then put cotton with butter on it until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm ointment or another until it heals.  It is necessary for you during the procedure to be extremely careful that nothing of the medication falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after days, and something like a hair is suspected in the eye,
then reapply the medication to that relaxed part of the eyelid specifically, as you did initially, then treat it until it heals. This is the image of the upside-down letter S.
Know that people's eyes differ in size, so let your cauterization be according to that. The correct method is not hidden from one who is experienced in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
Concerning the cauterization of the canthus which appears at the corner of the eye.
If you treat the fistula with what we mentioned in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is not effective, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap, and let a servant hold his head in your hands so that he does not move or shake his head.
Then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a frog on his eyes, then heat the cautery iron, whose image is this.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle, hollow at one end which will be used for cauterization; and if you wish, it may be perforated at the other end, or if you wish, it may be solid like an awl, but this hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then squeeze the fistula if it is open and remove its contents and dry it, or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus, then place the very hot cautery iron on it and hold it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
And move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose during cauterization, lest your hand should err or the patient should move, and the cautery iron should fall into the conjunctiva and damage it. If you reach the bone with the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization a second time if you need to.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton soaked in butter and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days have passed and it has not healed, apply a strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and remove it as will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a fistula, mentioned by some of the ancients:  you go to the site of the fistula, incise it, and then place a thin cone in the incision itself; this is its image.
And pour into it the weight of a dirham of molten lead, and hold your hand on the cone firmly and tightly, and the patient must not move at all, lest the molten lead flow into his eye; and you should place on the patient's eye cotton soaked in egg white or in water.
For the lead will burn the site of the fistula and heal it wonderfully. If the fistula heals with the cauterization and treatment that we have mentioned, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to use the perforation of the nose and return the fistula to the nasal passage, as will be explained in its special place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, called a harelip, especially in the lips of children; for it often occurs. If you treat this cleft with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective, then heat a small, knife-like cautery of this shape.
Its cavity should be as thin as a knife. Then apply it, hot, quickly into the cleft itself, until the cautery reaches the depth of the cleft; then treat it with the kyrioti, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a canker sore occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling appears at the root of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the molars, then it suppurates and bursts, and from the running of the pus a fistula results, and you treat it but the treatment is not effective, then you should heat a cautery to the extent that it fits into the fistula.
Then insert it, hot, into the hole of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and end. Do this once or twice; then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned of treatment, until it heals, God willing.
If the material is cut off and it heals, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to examine the place, and the decayed bone is removed according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Concerning the loosening of molars and gums.
If the gums are loosened due to moisture and the molars become mobile, and you have treated them with medication but it has not worked, then place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery iron, whose image comes after this, after you have placed the tube on the tooth.
And insert the hot cautery iron quickly, and hold your hand for a short time until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then lift your hand, then return the cautery iron repeatedly as you wish, then the patient fills his mouth with salt water.
And he holds it for an hour and then spits it out; for the mobile tooth becomes firm, and the loosened gum becomes strong, and the corrupt moisture dries.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In cauterizing the toothache.
If the toothache is from cold, or if there are worms in it and medication has not been effective, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with butter, or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with butter, you take cow's butter and boil it in an iron spoon or in a shell, then you take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, then you dip it in the boiled butter and quickly place it on the aching tooth and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat it several times until the strength of the fire reaches the root of the tooth. And if you wish, dip wool or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth and place a heated iron on top of it until the fire reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, you take a brass or iron tube, and it should have some thickness so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the cautery iron to the appropriate temperature and place it on the tooth itself, holding your hand until the cautery iron cools. Do this repeatedly.
Then the pain will go away, either that same day or the next day. After the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out. This is the image of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, as is convenient. This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If fistulas are due to phlegm and cold humors and do not yield to ripening by medication, and you desire a quick ripening, heat a hollow cautery iron, which has this image, perforated at both ends.
Let the smoke exit at the other end during cauterization.  Place it, protected, on the fistula itself once or twice, if necessary, until you reach the depth of the fistula. If the fistula is small, make the cautery iron the size of the fistula.
Then leave it for three days and apply a cotton swab soaked in butter until what was burned by fire is gone. Then treat it with ointment and tents until it heals, God willing.
Chapter twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors prevail in the trachea, especially if this is accompanied by coldness of temperament, then the patient should first be purged with purgative medicines, then cauterize with a cautery in the depression of the neck at the root of the throat in the lower location.
Beware of reaching the throat with the cautery and do not burn more than half of the skin. Then apply another cautery at the joint of the neck at the end of its last vertebra, and let the cautery be nail-shaped in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what has been mentioned until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter twenty-four.
On cauterizing lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, apply two cauteries above the clavicles in the low, soft, empty areas, and another cautery in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously described.  If you wish, let your cautery be by puncturing with the cautery called the "point," the image of which has been shown previously in puncturing earache, and let the punctures number around thirty.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he recovers. You may also make a cautery with three branches in this form, and it hastens the cauterization, because you cauterize with it three areas at once. This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated because of slippery humors, or if it does not remain fixed when it is reduced from its dislocation so that this becomes a habit, then it dislocates at the slightest movement, as we have seen, then it is necessary to reduce the joint first. Then the patient lies on his back or on his sound side, and then the skin inside the armpit is raised upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then heat the cautery iron with two heads, of which this is the image.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cauterization will be four cauterizations.  It may be cauterized with a cautery iron with three heads, and then the shape of the cauterization will be six cauterizations, and the heads will be as fine as a rose.
And this is the image of the cautery iron with three heads.
And this number may be increased by one, so that the cauterizations are eight. Then, apply to the cauterization balls pounded with salt, then keep the patient quiet and do not move the limb for a while until it strengthens. And if the dislocation is upwards, and that is rarely the case.
Then cauterize above the shoulder one good, nail-shaped cauterization, or many cauterizations in a dotted pattern, for the joint will stiffen, and the moisture will leave, and the patient will heal.
Chapter twenty-six.
On cauterizing the stomach.
If there occurs in the stomach coldness and much moisture, until it is put out of its temperament and the catarrhs to it increase, and it is treated with various treatments, but to no avail, then the patient should lie on his back, and extend his legs and arms, then cauterize three cauterizations. One cauterization under the breastbone, the size of a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauterizations below on the sides of the first cauterization, so that the shape of the cauterizations is triangular, and farther apart so that they do not come together if they suppurate.  The depth of the cauterization should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and this should be the shape of the cauterizations.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach in this manner.
And the cautery with which you cauterize this single cauterization should be in the shape of a circle, in this manner.
And the stomach may be cauterized by puncturing for whoever is afraid of this cauterization. This is done by marking points on the stomach with ink to the desired extent, then cauterizing them with a punctate cautery, then treating it with the treatment previously described until it heals.
Chapter twenty-seven.
On the cold cauterization of the liver.
If pain arises from the liver due to coldness and dampness, or due to a slight wind, to the extent that it has excessively departed from its natural temperament, and the patient is treated with what we mentioned in the division, and that does not work, then the patient should lie on his back, and three cauterizations should be marked with ink in this shape and this exact amount on the liver, below the diaphragm where the human elbow ends.
And after each cauterization, there should be a cauterization of the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization should be straight along the body, and do not press down firmly with the cautery.
Let the thickness of the skin burned be about half, no more; and let the patient stand on his feet; if he cannot stand, let him lie down with his legs stretched out and his arms raised.  This is the image of the cautery.
And you may cauterize these cauterizations with a knife-cautery if you are gentle and skillful in the craft, and take care lest you overdo the cauterization and burn all the skin, burning the abdomen and reaching the intestines, for the skin there is thin; so know this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cauterizing a swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know if that swelling is in the liver flesh or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the liver flesh, the patient will feel heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be intense sharpness with the pain, and you see that physicians have been unable to treat it, then the patient should lie on his back, and then you should mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire; it is the cautery that resembles a mill; and this is its image. And cauterize it with one cauterization until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the peritoneum, until all the pus comes out; then treat it with the treatment of abscesses until it heals.
And this type of cauterization should not be used except by one whose training in the art of medicine has been long, and who has repeatedly experienced these diseases; then he may undertake such work, but leaving it is better in my opinion.
Chapter twenty-nine.
In cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with the roots of rhubarb for a cold fistula in this manner: that you take one long, dry root of rhubarb, the longest you can find, and let it be about the thickness of a finger, then dip it in oil and heat it in the fire. Then apply one cauterization between the junction of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the jugular veins, inclined towards the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two cauterizations also between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backwards, and another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations on the back: one between the shoulder blades, and two on the sides of the spine, lower than the cauterization which is between the shoulder blades.
And you should not deepen your hand with the cautery, but it should be a superficial cauterization of the skin. Some of the ancients mentioned that some people used an iron cautery like a file, which they heated and inserted between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
And the pus is discharged as we mentioned in liver tumors.  In this method of cauterization there is great danger; either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula forms at the site which will not heal.
Chapter Thirty.
On the cauterization of the spleen.
If you treat the disease of the spleen with what we have mentioned in the classification of treatments, and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is done in three ways, all correct. One is to cauterize it with three or four cauterizations arranged lengthwise along the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations whose shape we have described previously, and between each cauterization and the next should be a distance equal to or slightly more than the thickness of a finger. The type of cautery should be as we described for the cauterization of the liver, and do not deepen your cautery, and the patient's position should be lying on his back.
And the other method is in the armpit, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left armpit ends, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left armpit ends, and your lifting of the skin should be across the body so that the cauterizations fall along the length of the body.
Then you insert two extremely hot cauteries until they penetrate the skin on the other side, then you remove the cautery, so that there are four cauterizations. And if you wish, you can cauterize with the other cautery with three points.
Then you treat the site of the cauterization after you leave it to suppurate for many days; this is more effective than all the treatments mentioned previously.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in ascitic dropsy specifically. If you treat the dropsical patient with the various treatments we mentioned in the classification, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize him with four cauterizations around the navel and one cauterization on the abdomen.
Another cauterization on the liver, one on the spleen, and two on his back between the vertebrae—one opposite his chest and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then leave the cauterization open to suppurate for a long time.
And do not neglect the patient after cauterization with what is necessary to combine the two aims, so that healing is hastened.  The shape of the cautery iron used to cauterize the back should be nail-shaped, as its shape was previously described, and that used to cauterize the abdomen should be olive-shaped.
Chapter thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the dorsum of the foot in the depression between the little toe and the ring finger.
And place your hand with the cautery iron and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for the yellow water will exude, and the cautery iron should be of this shape.
Then cauterize the legs, two cauterizations on each leg. The cauterization should be with the sharp end of the cautery iron, and the cauterizations should be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below that towards the middle of the leg, and on each thigh two cauterizations.
And leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, so that the water may exude, then treat it with what we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On cauterizing diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and dampness, so that it weakens the retaining and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and this has been treated with various treatments and it has not healed, and you see that the patient can tolerate cauterization and is strong, then cauterize him with a large cauterization on his stomach. As has been described in cauterizing the stomach with the circular cautery and four cauterizations around the navel gently with the gentle nail cautery, and a cauterization on the cotton above the coccyx, large or two.
If you see that the dampness is abundant and the patient can tolerate it, then cauterize him on the pubis and cauterize him on each flank, and perhaps you can add two small cauterizations to the stomach near the large one, for this is a successful treatment whose benefit is not missed, God willing.
Chapter thirty-four.
On cauterizing the hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are chronic, numerous, or single hemorrhoids in the anus, and they are from thick, cold humors or putrid dampness, and they have been treated with what we mentioned in the classification, and the treatment has not been effective, then cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the sacrum, a little below the coccyx, triangularly.
Cauterize below the navel by the width of two fingers.  And if you estimate that his stomach is cold and his food is not digested, and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cauterization as previously described, and cauterize on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery iron. Leave the cauterization open for a while, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery iron that resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the same cut wart and persevere until the cautery iron reaches the opening of the vein from which blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with suitable ointments until they heal.  And if you cauterize it with a large cauterization on cotton, it will be more effective.
Chapter thirty-six.
On cauterizing fistulas that are in the anus and its surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and the procedure we described in its place and shrinks from it, then perhaps he will heal with cauterization. If a fistula occurs and pus and corrupt moisture flow from it for a long time, then the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then know the depth of its cavity with the probe, then heat the cautery iron which resembles a needle, then insert it, hot, into the fistula itself, along the straight line of the fistula's depth and the extent to which the probe entered, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt bodies are burned, once or twice or three times, according to your need.
And be careful not to burn a nerve, if there is one, or a large vein, and if the fistula leads to the body of the bladder or to the body of the intestine.
Guard against all these places.  You do this only if the fistula is in a fleshy place and you judge that it is not penetrating. Then treat the place until it heals. If the place closes and the materials cease and it remains so for a time, then know that it has completely healed.
And if the materials do not cease from it, then know that it is penetrating, or that in its depth there is a corrupt bone, or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys from cold or a strong wind, and the patient's sexual function is diminished because of this, then it is necessary to cauterize it on the loins, on the kidneys themselves.  Cauterize each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery previously mentioned. And perhaps you will cauterize it a third time in the same session. Then three cauterizations will be applied in a row, and this will be more effective.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder from cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize it with one cauterization below the navel, on the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and one cauterization to the right of the navel and another to its left.
The cauterization on each side should be the size of the thumb joint.  And cauterize with one cauterization in the lower back, or two if needed.  The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On cauterizing the uterus.
If a substance from cold and dampness occurs in the uterus, and because of that it is prevented from conception and its temperament is corrupted and its courses are impeded, or pain occurs with the onset of menstruation, then three cauterizations should be made around the navel, as we mentioned in cauterizing the bladder. One cauterization on cotton in the lower back, or two, and the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Erroneous humors may descend to the hip joint, causing it to dislocate.  The sign is that the leg is longer than the other when one compares them, and you find emptiness at the place of the dislocation. Then one should cauterize the patient on the hip joint itself with a semi-circular cauterization.
After you have drawn with ink around the joint as it rotates, so that the joint itself falls in the middle of the circle.  The cautery whose image is presented in the cauterization of the stomach should be used. If you do not have this cautery, then use three cauterizations with the large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization deep enough to reach the entire thickness of the skin, then treat him until he heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-one.
On cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint and its cause is cold and dampness, and you have treated the patient with what we mentioned in the section, and the treatment has not been effective and it has become chronic, then it is necessary to evacuate the thick humors from the patient with  stinking beans or prickly pear seeds, or something similar.
Then cauterize it, and perform the cauterization in two ways: either cauterization with burning medicines or cauterization with fire. Cauterization with fire can be done in many ways; one of them is to cauterize on the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
Make them of a suitable depth, and let the distance between each cauterization and the next be equal to the thickness of a finger.  The cautery iron should be olive-shaped.  You may also cauterize one in the middle on the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations; or if you wish, cauterize it with the circle previously mentioned in cauterizing a dislocated hip, as a single cauterization.
So that the circle encompasses the entire hip and burns through the entire thickness of the skin. This is the manner of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two cauterizations on the thigh at the location where the patient indicates pain, and one cauterization above the heel four fingers towards the lateral side, slightly. The cautery iron should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with a pointed cautery iron three or four times, or more if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
And be careful in all your cauterizations not to burn a nerve or a large artery, lest you thereby cause the patient a serious or lasting injury.
And I have seen one and another who was cauterized above the heel, and the cauterization was excessive, so that the leg became gangrenous until the gangrene reached the foot and perforated it entirely, and the whole leg became corrupted, and then diarrhea and death followed. If the pain is in both sides, cauterize them both in this same manner.
Some learned physicians have mentioned regarding cauterization of the hip a cauterization of this type:  A cup-like instrument is made of iron, its diameter being half a span, and at its mouth it should be the thickness of a date pit, or slightly less, and inside this cup is another cup, and a third cup.
And the distance between each cup should be the width of a thumb joint, and the cups should be open on both sides, and their height should be about one or two finger joints.  And a handle of iron is firmly attached to the cups, and this is its image.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, then it is placed on the hip joint, the patient reclining on the sound side.  Three round cauterizations are performed at once, then he is left for three days and the wound is dressed with butter, and the wound is left open for several days.
Many, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The author of this book said: We have rarely used this type of cauterization because of its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, and because of the few who are found who can endure it; however, it is one of the good cauterizations for whoever endures it and its application is appropriate to the location.
As for cauterization with burning drugs, it is to make two cup-like instruments similar to the rings through which the uvula descends, of copper or iron.
Their rims should have a height of about two finger joints or so, and the distance between them should be the thickness of a finger, and they should be open at the bottom and open at the top, and some of them should be held together in this manner.
Then lower them onto the hip joint, the patient lying on his sound side, and firmly hold your hand, then pour between the two circles some sharp water, which is warmed slightly by the fire, and hold it for the duration of an hourglass; and let the patient endure its sting and burning, for he will feel a burning like fire.
Until the burning subsides, then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the sharp water from the whole hip with fresh water, then leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears, and leave it for days so that pus drains from it, for this is more effective.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it that holds water, in the same way as you did for the hip. This is the description of the sharp water, and I have already described it in the chapter on the preparation of medicines.
Take one part of alkali salt and one part of unslaked lime, crush them and place them in a new pot that has a single small hole in its bottom, large enough for a probe to pass through, and place another glazed pot beneath the bottom of the pot.
And pour fresh water over the alkali and lime to cover them by a finger's breadth, after having pressed them firmly with your hand, and leave the pot until the sharp water descends into the glazed pot below, then collect all that water, then pour it over more fresh lime and alkali.
Then it will be very strong and sharp, useful in many medical procedures and for cauterizing various limbs, because it acts exactly like fire.  There are also other medications that cauterize the hip, such as thapsia, linseed oil, and lime with soap, mixed together.
Galen claimed, recounting a story from an ancient man, a treatment for hip pain and sciatica, greatly emphasizing its importance and claiming that it needs no other treatment and cures in a single application.
To the point that one might enter the bath carried and leave cured.  This is done by taking green verdigris—if green is not available, take fresh dry verdigris—and finely grind it with some fat and apply it to the hip where the pain is, or on the leg, or on the thigh.
Then bind it on and leave it for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside, then take him to the bath. When his body is damp, put him in the bath; the pain will go away and he will be cured, God willing.  If he is not cured, reapply the poultice after ten days.
Then, God willing, he will be cured. Know that no one should cauterize with these medicines except after the body has been purged. Dioscorides mentioned that goat's hair, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
Cauterization is done in this way: take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the deep place between the thumb of the hand and the forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take dry goat hair.
Heat it in the fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out. Then take another and continue to do this until sensation reaches from the middle of the upper arm to the hip and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called cauterization with goat hair.
Chapter forty-two.
On cauterizing back pain.
Pain in the back may occur from many causes, either from a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like. It may be from the effusion of a cold, moist substance. Cauterization is only done in this type alone.
That which is from the effusion of a cold substance, it is necessary after the patient vomits with hellebore and the like, that he be cauterized on his back where the pain is, three rows across the width of the table itself, after marking the place with ink. In each row, five cauterizations or more, according to what you see of the patient's endurance and strength.
And the cauterization will be with a pointed cautery; and if you wish, cauterize it with three or four cauterizations with a medium-sized nail cautery, in this manner.
Chapter forty-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a hump.
This ailment often affects small children, and its beginning sign in the child is that shortness of breath occurs upon standing and movement, and you find at the end of the vertebrae of his back a bead that has protruded, standing out from the other beads.
If you see that and you wish to stop it, then cauterize it with a cautery that is round, in this manner. Let the cautery take hold on each side of the vertebra evenly. And if you wish, cauterize it around the vertebra with a pointed cautery, two or three rows, and let the points be close to one another. Then treat the area until it heals, as we have mentioned.
And be careful not to use cautery in a hump that is from a spasm of the nerve.
Chapter forty-four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are due to cold humors that settle in any member of the body, and if the pain occurs in the legs, it is customary for physicians to call that specifically gout.
If the gout, which is cold in cause, is treated with the types of treatment we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not go away, then cautery will remove it. This is done by cauterizing around the joint of the legs after evacuation with many cauterizations. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped and medium-sized, in this manner.
And if you need to punctuate on the surface of the leg, do so with a punctate cautery iron.  If the pain ascends to the knees or to the other joints—and this often happens—cauterize each knee three or four times on each side with this same olive-shaped cautery iron.
If you need more cauterization than this, cauterize, but do not deepen your hand with the cautery; rather, let it be to the thickness of the skin only. If the pain ascends to the hips or to the back, use what we mentioned of cautery in its chapter.
If the pain is only in the hands, punctuate around the forearms as if circling two rows. If the pain remains in the fingers, punctuate each knuckle with a point and the metacarpus. If the pain ascends after days to the elbows or shoulders,
cauterize them on each side. The patient should not be without good management and taking medications, for if he improves his diet and evacuates phlegm, he will be cured with this cautery, God willing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the cauterization of fistulas.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some of the intestine and epiploon descends to the testicle, and this is recent and beginning, then you should order the patient to abstain from food that day and to use laxatives to evacuate the bowels.
Then he should lie on his back before you, and you should tell him to restrain himself until the epiploon or intestine protrudes, then you should return it with your finger, then you should mark with ink below the hernia on the pubic bone a sign resembling a semicircle, its ends towards the upper part of the body.
Then heat a cautery of this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws off sparks, then return the intestine or epiploon to the abdomen. Then a servant should place his hand on the place so that the intestine does not protrude.  You have spread the patient's legs and placed a cushion under him, and another servant ties his legs.
And another on his chest holds his hands. Then lower the cautery onto the same mark, and your hand with the cautery should be firm and straight, and hold it until it reaches the bone, or repeat it again if it does not reach the bone the first time.
And be careful to prevent the intestine from protruding while you cauterize, lest you burn it, which will cause the patient either death or a great calamity. And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed.  And the cautery should be gentle for children according to their size and for adults according to their size.
Then treat the cauterized area after three days with butter until the scab from the fire is gone, then treat it with other ointments until it heals. And let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is sealed.
And you should make his diet during his treatment such that it softens his bowels, lest the intestine protrude during defecation. Then, if he wants to get up after forty days, use a firm bandage and keep it on for another forty days.  And he should reduce exertion, fullness from food and drink, and loud shouting.
For if this treatment is used in this way, he will be completely healed, God willing. And I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its chapter, God willing. As for the hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is in its beginning stages, if you wish to prevent it from increasing, cauterize a circular area around it, the size of the hernia. Let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds of it, then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing. And some of the ancients mentioned that the hernia should be cauterized with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you mark the hernia with ink, making the horizontal line at the upper side of the hernia and the other line downwards, and you apply a single nail-shaped cautery in the middle; the first cauterization is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If in some member, from a fall or blow, pain occurs and that pain lasts a long time, and treatment with medicine is not effective, then it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery; and the cauterization of each member should be according to its size and smallness, weakness and strength.
And if the pain and fistula persist, then if it heals from the first cauterization, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cauterization upon it, for it is the nature of these pains to move from the member to what is near it; therefore, you should follow them with cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy which comes from the corruption of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize it, observe: if the leprosy is incipient, and you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification, and it has not diminished nor stopped, and you fear that corruption will take over the entire temperament of the patient, then cauterize him on the head with five cauterizations. One in the middle of the head, as is customary; the second below it towards the forehead at the hairline; two on the temples; and one at the back on the nape of the neck.  And press your hand with the cautery slightly until it affects the bone a little.
And scales will be removed from it, so that the thick vapors may easily escape from it.  And cauterize it also on the same spleen, as previously. But if the leprosy has persisted in the patient and appeared clearly, it is necessary to cauterize him with these cauterizations which we have mentioned in the head. And a cauterization on the tip of the nose; two cauterizations on the cheeks; two cauterizations on the cervical vertebrae; six on the vertebrae of the back; one large one on the coccyx at the base of the spine; another above it in the same area; and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee; and two on the shoulders; and two on the elbows; and two on the breasts; and cauterize each joint of the fingers of his hands and feet with one cauterization; and each heel of his feet and the forearms of his hands.
And beware of the nerve which is on the back of the heels, lest you burn them. And one may cauterize with one cauterization on the pubic bone, and another on the pit of the stomach, and another on the liver. And know that the more cauterizations you perform, the more beneficial and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel the pain of cauterization as the healthy person does, because his body is numb. And the cauteries should be large and small according to the organs and joints, as has been previously described concerning the characteristics of the cauteries.
Then treat the cauterization with finely ground burnt alum mixed with honey and all other treatments until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness: When a limb becomes numb and is treated with medications, ointments, and dressings, but does not heal, then cauterize the numb limb with cauterizations according to the size of the limb, whether large or small. And let your cauterization penetrate a little into the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  And it may be that some numbness affecting the hand and foot in the vertebrae of the back, at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb, is cauterized, and thus the numbness will disappear.
And let none undertake this except one who is knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of leprosy.
If leprosy is longstanding and no medical remedy has been effective, cauterize it slightly, to the depth of the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour mixed with rose oil, lamb's tongue, and pigeon's blood or partridge's blood, one part of each. Mix everything together, apply it to a cloth, and bind it to the area until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is incipient and you wish to arrest it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it grows. Some scholars have mentioned that a strong cauterization should be done in its middle, but I do not agree with that because I expect it to ulcerate, and I have witnessed that many times.
Therefore, the correct method is to cauterize around it in a circle as we said, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of hemorrhoids.
If a hemorrhoid occurs and is slow to mature, either because of the strength of the agent causing it, or because of the age of the patient if he is old and anemic, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the hemorrhoid, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations in a punctate manner.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation. If you wish to slow it with cauterization, then take the cautery whose image is this.
And insert it into the middle of the hemorrhoid until it penetrates the skin, and let the cauterization be from what is below, so that the flow of pus is easy, then treat it as it should be until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
In cauterizing the ulcer.
An ulcer is nothing but corruption that spreads in a limb, eating it as fire eats dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, then take many small and large, nail-shaped cauteries according to what suits the place of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from every side until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing remains at all. Then leave it for three days and apply to the cauterized areas powdered sulfur with oil until all the eschar and all the corruption is removed.
Then treat it with ointments that promote the growth of flesh. If after three days you see that the flesh grows healthy without corruption, then that is it; otherwise repeat the cauterization on what remains of the corrupted areas.  An ulcer may also be treated with a sharp medicine.
For it acts in place of fire, more quickly beneficial. And I have mentioned its treatment with a sharp medicine in the classification; take it from there when you need it.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This ailment frequently occurs on the soles of the feet; it is a rough thing that pains the person.  Cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a sharp liquid.
As for cauterization with fire, you heat a hollow cautery iron resembling an eagle's feather, made of iron to the extent that it surrounds the nail from every side, and let it be thin-edged. Then you lower it, hot, onto the nail, and you rotate your hand with the cautery iron around the nail until the cautery iron reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it for three days, during which it will suppurate; then you apply a dressing of pounded wild lettuce with salt, and leave the dressing on it for a night, for it will then be extracted from its roots. Then you treat the site of the wound with a flesh-growing ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
It frequently occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet.  You should take a tube of copper, or iron, or an eagle's feather, and place it over the nail or wart, then pour a small amount of sharp liquid into the tube.
And hold your hand as you rotate it, while gently pricking with your hand, so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the sharp liquid finds a way to penetrate to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging of the sharp liquid for a short while.
Then you leave it, for the nail will be extracted by its roots. You do this one by one until you have dealt with all of them on the body; then you treat their sites after they are extracted with ointments that promote the growth of flesh.
Chapter fifty-four.
On cauterizing the wart.
If a wart is caused by a cold in the nerve, or by a quartan fever or something else, then it should be cauterized four or five times, on the back of the neck, with a cauterization between each vertebra, and one cauterization on its chest. And a cauterization on its stomach with the olive cautery, for the wart is calmed and the cold disease is hastened to ripen.
Chapter fifty-five.
On cauterizing the pustule occurring in the body.
Ugly pustules may erupt on the body, which are from cold, thick, corrupt materials.  At their first appearance, they should be cauterized, at the head of each pustule, with a gentle cauterization using a reed whose end has been kindled with fire, or with a long root of the zarwand, or with a lenticular cautery.
And abscesses may be cauterized at their first eruption in this manner, so that it does not increase, and the active cause is dissipated, and the patient is cured from it.  However, this should be after the patient has been bled.
Chapter fifty-six.
On cauterizing the bleeding that occurs when an artery is cut.
Often bleeding occurs from an artery that has been severed in a wound that occurs externally, or in the incision of a tumor, or the cauterization of a member, and so on, which is difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery and press it firmly so that the blood is contained under your finger and nothing comes out. Then, place several small and large olive-shaped cauteries in the fire and heat them until they are very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery has opened.  Apply the cautery to the same vein after quickly removing your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If, when you remove your finger from the mouth of the artery, the bleeding continues and the cautery goes out, quickly take another cautery from those prepared in the fire, and continue to do this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve that is there, lest you cause the patient another affliction.  Know that if blood flows from an artery, it cannot be stopped, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said; or by amputation, if it has not already been severed; for if it is severed, its ends contract and the bleeding stops; or it can be tightly bound with threads; or medications that stop bleeding can be applied to it, and it can be firmly bound with bandages.
And as for anyone who tries to stop it with a ligature or by tightening with cloths or by applying cauterizing substances and the like, it will not be stopped at all except rarely. If this happens to someone and a physician or medicine is not available, let him quickly place his index finger on the mouth of the wound itself, as we described.
And he should press it very hard until the bleeding stops and flows away from above the wound and onto the artery.  The finger should not be removed, and it should be constantly cooled with very cold water until the blood clots, thickens, and stops. In the meantime, you should consider what is needed in the way of cautery or medicine.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, punctures, bloodletting, wounds, and the like.
Khalaf said: In the first chapter, we mentioned every disease for which cautery with fire and cauterizing medication is suitable, as well as its causes, reasons, instruments, the shapes of cauteries, and I made that into sections from the head to the foot.
And I follow in this chapter the same method, so that the student may easily find what he seeks.
Before I begin with that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more dangers than the first chapter on cautery.
Because of that, warning should be stronger in it, because in the work of this chapter, there frequently occurs the evacuation of blood, upon which life depends, when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor, or puncturing an abscess, or treating a wound, or extracting an arrow, or incising a stone, and the like; all of which are accompanied by danger and fear, and in most of them, death occurs.
And I advise you against falling into what is doubtful to you, for there may come to you in this craft various kinds of people with various kinds of illnesses, some of whom are weary of their illness and death is easy for them.
