Because of the severity of his illness and the length of his affliction, and because of the illness, there are signs that indicate death; and there are some who give you their money and enrich you with it in hope of health, and his illness is deadly. Therefore, you should not at all help anyone who comes to you and has this characteristic.
Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness, and do not proceed with anything of that except after certain knowledge is confirmed with you about what will lead to a good outcome, and use in all the treatment of your patients the precedence of knowledge and warning about what leads to safety.
For in that you have help in acquiring praise, glory, mention, and thanks. May God inspire you, my sons, with His guidance and not deprive you of correctness and success, for that is in His hand; there is no god but He.
And I have arranged this chapter in sections, as was done previously in the chapter on cauterization from the horn to the foot, so that the thing you want from it may be easier for you, God willing.
Chapter One.
In the treatment of the water which collects in the heads of children.
This ailment often affects children at birth if the midwife presses the child's head without gentleness, and it may also occur from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have not seen this ailment in anyone except children, and all those I have seen of them have quickly approached death; therefore I have seen fit to leave treatment of it. And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day. Until the child could not sit up by himself because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
And this moisture either collects between the skin and the bone, or it collects under the bone on the peritoneum.  The treatment in this case, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the swelling is small, is that one should make an incision in the middle of the head, a single transverse incision, and the length of the incision should be about two fingerbreadths, so that the moisture flows out. This is a picture of the lancet.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling is larger, then make two intersecting incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the bone—and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open from every direction, and the water subsides if you press it with your hand inwards, and this is not hidden from you—then one should make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
And after the incision, all the moisture comes out, then you should bind the incisions with rags and bandages, then you should pour syrup and oil over it from above until the fifth day, then you should untie the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments.  Do not neglect to bind the head moderately, and feed the patient with every dry food that has little moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals. Another method of incision is to look where the bone of the swelling and the collection of water appear, because it may be in the back of the head more, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left.
Then aim with the incision where the swelling and the fullness of the water appear to you, and incise it as much as you can, and be careful not to cut an artery, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding along with the evacuation of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the mastoids.
When someone experiences severe colds in the eyes or chest, and this is prolonged, and the treatment of medicines does not work, then the best treatment for this is to cut these two arteries.
Then, if you want to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the artery appears. Then tie the patient's neck with a portion of his garment, and then look where the pulse beats and their locations, the two low-lying locations behind the ears, which are rarely hidden except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, and then cut them with a lancet, cutting to the bone. This should be done across the head.  And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and cut upwards. The length of the cut should be about two fingers' breadth.
If the artery is severed, blood will gush out in a pulsating manner, leaping forward in a continuous throbbing. If the artery is not palpable, you should measure three fingers' breadth from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise to the bone.  What should be...
...sent forth from the blood six ounces on average, and perhaps more or less; all this depends on the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries. Then look at the wound; if there remains anything of the peritoneum on the bone, cut it away.
Lest a hot inflammation occur. Then bind the wound with a thread of linen rags, and then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Incising the two arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute attacks caused by sharp humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headache, and so on, and has been treated with various medical treatments without success, then we have tried in these diseases incising or cauterizing the arteries of the temples, as we have described.
The procedure for incising them is that the patient's hair in the temples should be shaved, and the artery visible in the temple should be targeted, for it is discernible by its pulsation and rarely hidden except in a few people.
Or, in severe cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with a part of his garment, then you rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly and distinctly. Then take the scalpel, whose image is this. Then you incise the skin gently with it until you reach the artery, then you insert a hook into it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes beneath it on all sides; if the artery is thin, twist it with the end of the hook. Then cut a portion of it to the extent that its ends separate and contract, and do not cause bleeding, for if it is not severed and cut, the blood will not flow at all; then it is evacuated of blood from six ounces to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with strong doubled thread; and let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings, lest corruption quickly reach it before the wound heals and bleeding occurs.
Then cut what is between the ligatures; do this at that time or after another time; and if you wish to cauterize it with a knife-cautery to the bone until its ends are severed, this will act in the same way or better, as we said.
Except if the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, for cauterization is what helps to consume the humors and is more certain in benefit; and after removing the arteries, it is necessary to pack the place with worn cotton and apply tight bandages; and after healing, treat it with dry medicines which generate flesh, and with bandaging, until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your work, hasten to cut it, either by cauterization or by filling the place with vitriol and tightening your hand until the blood stops; and if you do not have any of that, place your finger until the blood clots.
And wash the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides and bind it as necessary. And what is lighter and easier than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the two places with ink.
And apply it very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that its ends are separated, for it will not reunite with this cauterization at all.  And this is the image of the cautery.
The blades should be sharp, resembling small scalpels, except that they should be much less sharp than a scalpel, because if they are as sharp as a scalpel, they will cool more quickly and the flesh will not be severed quickly.  If they have some thickness, the heat of the fire will remain in them.
And it severs the flesh quickly.  This procedure is better than any other procedure, and lighter and easier.  And let the distance between the blades be the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of the constant flow of hot tears to the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the outside of the skull, and nothing from the medical treatment of drugs has benefited it, and you see the patient's face is reddened. And he feels in his forehead a crawling like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their lashes are eroded and his eyelids are contracted from the severity of the tears.  Therefore, the ancients in their treatment resorted to this procedure, and they call it treatment with the sword. It is that you order the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions in the forehead along the length of the forehead.  The length of each incision is about two fingers. One incision is parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second is a little distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third is on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries which are on the sides, and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws. And let the distance after each incision be about three closed fingers. And let you have prepared pieces of sponge or many dry cloths with which to wipe the blood.
Then insert the double-edged scalpel, whose image is this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and with it you dissect all the skin which is between the two incisions, along with the membrane on the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove this scalpel. And also insert into the first incision another instrument called a sharp, one-sided knife, smooth and not sharp on the other side. And this is its image:
And place its sharp side upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle incision, and cut with it all the vessels which descend from the head to the eyes, without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do that in the middle incision to the other incision. And after a moderate amount of blood flows, squeeze the places of the congealed blood, then put in each incision a cotton wick, and place on it a compress moistened with syrup and oil, or vinegar and oil.
Lest a hot swelling occur. And on the third day, untie the bandage and use much irrigation with lukewarm water, then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and everything else with which you treat wounds, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When someone experiences many, sharp, severe, persistent discharges, and you see his eyes weak, small, and his vision weakened, and his eyelids ulcerated, and the eyelashes falling out. And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and consecutive sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is this: you order the patient to shave his forehead, then you make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and consecutive sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins. The best treatment is this: you order the patient to shave his forehead, then you make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or a little higher laterally, and you begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and the incision should be to the bone, and keep your hand away from the muscles of the temples that move when chewing, until the bone is exposed and all the blood is dried with a sponge.
Then separate the lips of the incision with worn cotton or a linen thread, then tie it from above with pledgets, and soak the pledgets with syrup and oil, lest a hot inflammation occur. And when you untie it and see that the hot inflammation has subsided, then you should scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins in it.
Then treat it with a drying preparation which makes the flesh grow, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and preparing an ointment from them, and using it for the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a semi-stone like iron and glass; or a vegetable seed like chickpeas and kernels and the like; or a flowing thing like water and vinegar and the like.
Or an animal.  If a stone or a kind of stone falls into the ear which does not grow in the ear, then turn the ear towards the sun. If you see the stone, then instill some violet oil or ceruse into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or sneezing with a sternutatory. And close the nostrils when sneezing comes, after having placed a ring of cloth or wool around the ear and extending the ear upwards. Often it comes out with this treatment.  If it does not come out otherwise, then try to remove it with a gentle scoop, the image of which is this.
If it comes out with the scoop, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly bent, blind probe. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the hole of the ear gently.
And seal around the tube with pitch softened with oil, lest there be a way for the wind other than the tube, then draw it with your breath strongly. And often it comes out with that. If it does not come out with what we have described, then take a little bit of the gum of Nabataea or of the prepared gum which is taken by birds, and place it on the end of a probe.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then insert it into the hole of the ear gently, after having dried the ear from moisture. If it does not come out with all that we have described, then proceed to incision before hot swelling or tension occurs.
The description of the incision is that you first bleed the patient in the temple and remove blood according to his strength. Then you seat the patient before you, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its lobe, in its lowest part.
And the incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Then suture the incision quickly and treat it until it heals.
And if the thing that has fallen into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, then try to remove it as we have mentioned. If it does not respond to being removed, then take a thin, delicate scalpel of this shape.
And try with it to cut that type of seed that has fallen into the ear. But you only do this if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the vapor of the ear until it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a blind probe or with a fine sponge or by suction.
As we have mentioned, then it is easy to remove it.  As for water entering the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with a handkerchief, having filled his ear with some old cotton while lying on the ear that contains the water. If it comes out by that,
otherwise, take many small, smooth, flat stones the length of the fingers, warm them slightly by the fire, and the patient inserts one into the opening of his ear. He then raises his leg on that side and strikes the stone in his ear with another stone.
He continues to do this with one stone after another until all the water comes out. The water may also come out by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and bringing the other end to the fire until most of it burns, then repeating with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or draw it out with the tube as was done before with the stone. As for removing an animal that has entered, see if it is small, like a flea or similar. Treat it as was mentioned in the section, and as for…
If its body is large and perceptible to the touch, try to remove it with forceps and hooks. This is easier than anything else that lodges in the ear. As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if you treat it as mentioned in the section on instillations, and your treatment is not effective…
you should look into the ear in the sun. If you see something of the worm, remove it with forceps or fine hooks. If you see nothing, take a tube of this form.
narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as much as the patient can bear. Then suck strongly, doing this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If it does not respond to being pulled out, seal around the tube with wax as I mentioned to you with the stone.
If it does not come out by what we have mentioned, use the instillations I mentioned, which the ancients have tested in killing worms, and you will find that in the section on instillations.  The pouring of oils and medicines into the ear is done with this instrument, and this is its form:
As you see, make it from silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top. And if you wish, let the nozzle inside the tube be of tightly fitting copper. And if you wish, take a reed and wrap cotton tightly around its end.
Then pour the oil or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear. Then insert the reed with the cotton from above and squeeze it gently with your hand until the oil is forced into the ear and it is felt.
by the patient, and let what you pour into the ear be warmed a little in the fire. And beware that what is dropped into it is neither very cold nor very hot, for the ear does not tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of the obstruction affecting the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear canals not perforated.
Also, some people may experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or newly grown flesh. This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal, beyond the reach of sight.
It may also appear slightly outside the perforation and be visible. That which appears deep within the ear and is not visible is, in most cases, difficult to cure. That which is visible should have the patient's ear placed in the sun. And look into it; if you see the obstruction, open it with a delicate scalpel, which has this form:
Its tip should be slightly broad, and the rest of the scalpel smooth on both sides, lest it injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently but firmly until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep in the ear, take a smooth, delicate probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction, and the patient feels heaviness in hearing, remove it gently.
Be careful not to injure the membrane of the ear. Then insert into the ear a wick the size of the perforation, having smeared it with Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are sure the wound will not close up. Or take a wick, moisten it with water, and sprinkle powdered vitriol on it.
Use it. If you see that the ear is swollen with a hot inflammation, you should remove the wick and replace it with another wick smeared with an ointment made of quince and rose oil until the hot inflammation subsides; then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear.  Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the chapter on haemostatics.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts appearing on the eyelids.
These warts which appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  You should grasp them with forceps or a hook and cut them off at their roots with a scalpel, then apply powdered verdigris to the area.
If it bleeds, cauterize it with a mild lentil-shaped cautery. Cauterization is best after cutting.  They often return if anything of their roots remains; if cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of the cold affecting the eyelids.
Something resembling a cold in its intensity and hardness often appears in the eyelids.
Therefore it is called a cold, which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The treatment is to observe; if the cold swelling is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves to every direction, then the matter is easy, so make a transverse incision on it.
Then carefully peel it from every side until it is removed, then hang it with a hook and cut it off. If you are not able to cut it off except after cutting through the eyelid, that will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, then close it with sutures and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, then there is no harm from it, for the ointment will heal and close it. If the cold swelling is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then turn over the eyelid and hang the swelling with a hook without needing to make an incision, and cut it from every side.
If it penetrates the eyelid with the cut, that will not harm anything. Then wash the eye after cutting the swelling with salt water and treat the area with something that heals until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of the pterygium which appears in the eyelid.
Pterygium is a fleshy excrescence that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid.  This mostly occurs in children, weighing down their eyes and causing them to suffer from colds in the mornings, and they are unable to look at sunlight. Because the tears flow quickly to them.
Therefore, you see them always leaning on their faces and sleeping on them, and their eyelids under their eyebrows are moist, swollen, with the pterygium clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the pterygium between them. The procedure is to have the patient place his head in your lap.
Then you take a linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick of a size that encircles the pterygium on all sides. Then you place it on the pterygium and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the pterygium in the center of the circle.
Then you make a transverse incision in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, and the incision should not be larger than an incision made in bloodletting. As for the depth, you should incise all the skin until you reach the pterygium.
In most cases, the pterygium protrudes from the incision like a piece of white fat. You pull it out with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, rotating your hand right and left until it is cured. Be careful not to enlarge the incision, lest the lancet reach the eye and injure it.
If the pterygium does not appear in the first incision, you should gently enlarge the incision a little until the pterygium appears. Then pull it out as we said, then dip a cloth in vinegar and water, place it on the area, and bandage it. Some people crush salt.
And they place it inside the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture. Then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with soothing compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of pterygium of the eye.
If superfluous membranes grow on the eyelid, outside the natural course beneath the natural eyelids and eyelashes, they harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, and opacity and thickening, to the point that this may cause blindness.
Pterygium of the eye is of four types:  either by cauterization with fire, or by caustic medication as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization; or the pterygium may be removed by excision and suturing, or by the reed, as I shall describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand; if it does not turn, then insert a needle with a thread from below the eyelid and pass the needle with the thread from above, near the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
Turn it with a probe, then incise the inner eyelid, avoiding the superfluous hairs, with a sharp scalpel from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus; then remove the thread and place a small pledget of cotton or linen under the eyelid, then mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of a laurel leaf, except that the shape should be according to the amount of eyelid elevation you desire, for this varies among people; some need a suitable amount of eyelid to be excised according to the degree of eyelid relaxation.
And some need less to be excised; all this is according to the relaxation of the eyelid. Then incise with the scalpel along the two lines you have marked, beginning from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, and each incision should be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a probe.
Then insert the hook into one corner of the skin, then flay it all off; then join the edges with suture using a fine woolen needle and thread, wipe away the blood, and stick the remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
If you wish to do so, otherwise it does not matter. Then leave the sutures and threads for about three or four days, then treat it. And if you wish, leave the wound without suturing and treat it with what dries and constricts, for the eyelid will rise when the wound is healed and joined; suturing is better.
This method of blepharoplasty was mentioned by the ancients, although it is burdensome for the patient; it is a good procedure and not dangerous.  Another method of blepharoplasty is to mark on the eyelid a shape like that of a laurel leaf, as we have described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, either separate or grouped in this manner:
Then cut off the excess eyelid with small scissors, evenly, in this manner:
If you cannot hold the hooks steady, take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger canthus and a third thread near the smaller canthus, and gather the threads together with your fingers evenly, then raise it with a moderate lift.
Raise all of the marked skin as we described, then close the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot inflammation appears when you cut or incise, treat that inflammation with kyriote and similar soothing ointments.
It may also happen that the lower eyelid inverts its edges, so you should use what we mentioned of cutting and suturing and cauterization with fire and with caustic medicine; cauterization with fire or with caustic medicine is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing, and you should do cutting and suturing in both.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this way: you invert the eyelid and make an incision on the inside as described, then you make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the length of the eyelid, and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you cut into their ends on both sides where the threads are grasped, then you gently gather them over what remains of the eyelid and tightly pull the two reeds from both sides and leave it for several days; the stretched skin will die, turn black, and become necrotic.
Until it falls off by itself; if it is slow, cut it with scissors, then treat it until it heals.  When it heals, the eyelid will be raised and the edges of the eyelid will not be inverted. This is an image of the two reeds; make them in this shape and this size in length and width exactly:
Chapter Twelve.
On raising ingrown eyelashes from the eye with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle and thread it with a smooth, thin silk thread, then gather its ends and tie them in a very delicate knot, and let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then attach another thin thread to the loop, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Then have the patient place his head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the work may be clearly seen, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the loop into the root of the ingrown hair.
And quickly pass it through the eyelid until you bring it out above the natural hair, then pull the thread upwards through the loop, and the hair or hairs, whether one, two, or three—no more—will enter the loop.
Then pull it with your hand until the hair comes out with the loop in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the loop and the hair does not come out with it, pull the loop downwards by the thread that you attached to it until the loop comes out of the upper opening.
Then return the hair into it and pull them both. Do this until it rises in the upper opening and you see it clearly. Then remove the loop and the thread and take them out, and bind the eye and leave it bound for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a long hair from the natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after a while, and then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the stye which occurs in the upper eyelid.
That which this stye is exposed to is called a harelip, and this stye is either natural or accidental. The accidental one is from the healing of a wound or a cut or cauterization and the like.
And the method of treatment is to incise that adhesion and separate its lips, and place a wick of linen between them and bind it until it heals.  And it is not appropriate to use things that dry and constrict in its treatment.
For if you do that, the stye will return more severely than it was.  Rather, use things that relax, such as fenugreek and lint with water in which mallow and flaxseed have been cooked, and diachylon ointment melted with some fat, and smear it with the wick and treat it with this.
And the key to its treatment is to strive by every means that it not adhere in the way it was initially. And this treatment that we have mentioned is only some improvement of the stye, not that the appearance of the place returns to exactly how it was at all.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the treatment of the stye which forms in the lower eyelid.
This stye which forms in the lower eyelid is that which is truly called a stye, and it is both natural and accidental. The accidental one is from a wound, or incision, or cauterization, and the like.
And the method of working in it is that you take a needle with a doubled thread, and you pierce it into the flesh and pass it from the left canthus to the right canthus, until the thread is at the edges of the flesh, then you stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is appropriate, otherwise you take a probe and place it on the site of the incision and turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions in the inner side of the eyelid and the ends of the incisions are from the corners of the cut which you cut until they meet and form an angle from them. Until, when they meet, its shape becomes similar to this shape:
Which is the Greek letter lambda, then you remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp edge is lower than what is next to the eyelid, then you gather the scattered parts with two stitches, sewing it with a wool thread, then you treat it with what we mentioned of emollient medicines and bandages until it heals.
And if the fistula is caused by an incision, suture, or cauterization, it is necessary to make a simple incision without the hair of the eyelids as well, as mentioned before, then separate the lips with a bandage as we mentioned.
In short, in the treatment of fistula, whether it is from above or below, you should proceed according to the appearance of the fistula, for it may vary greatly in form, and the skilled surgeon devises a solution in whatever way is possible.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it, and the disease itself indicates the appropriate procedure and instrument in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur in many people, such that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing its easy movement and hindering its natural functions; therefore, you should insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid.
And raise it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not sharp like the cutting scalpels, but rather blunt, lest the patient be disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slip, injuring the eye with its sharpness; and your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed; then pour into the eye salt water to wash it, or instill into it some vitriol solution diluted with water, then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen wick.
And place over the eye wool moistened with egg white, and after the third day use astringent eye drops until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
In cutting the pterygium and fleshy excrescences.
The pterygium is of two kinds: either nervous, resembling a thin, hard membrane; or non-nervous, resembling a firm white moisture which, if touched with an iron instrument or if one attempts to remove it with a hook, is torn to pieces.
And a hook does not hold firm in it. Both kinds begin from the canthus and gradually extend until they cover the pupil and obstruct the light and movement of the eye. The procedure for cutting them is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, and you raise the eyelid with your hand, then you grasp the pterygium with a slightly curved hook and stretch it upwards, then you take a needle and thread into it a horsehair or cow hair and a strong thread, and you bend the point of the needle slightly and pierce it into the middle of the pterygium.
Then you pass it through with the needle and tie the pterygium with the thread and stretch it upwards and dissect with the hair the side of the pterygium next to the pupil, as if you were spreading it with the hair to its end, then you cut the remainder at the root of the canthus with a fine scalpel or small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the canthus.
So that continuous flow of tears does not result from its excision.  The pterygium may be stretched with the hook alone or with the thread as we said, then the smooth-ended scalpel, whose shape is this: [presumably an image was here], is used to dissect it.
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, lest it cause a wound and injure the pupil.  Then, after cutting, put some powdered salt or vitriol into the eye, and leave it until the next day, then treat it as appropriate until it heals.
If the pterygium is not fibrous and you cannot insert a needle into it or fix a hook in it, this type of pterygium is often treated with medication and heals as described in the classification. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a fine, smooth scalpel with a weak edge, like this: [presumably an image was here]. And dissect the pterygium from above gently with it. If you see that it is loosening and fragmenting and the dissection has affected it, then instill into the eye from your [medicine] bottle vitriol or red vitriol or powdered salt, and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until it is all gone, unless a hot inflammation obstructs you in the eye; then leave it and treat the hot inflammation until it heals, then resume the dissection until it heals. As for the fleshy excrescence of the caruncle…
if the excrescence harms the eye excessively, then hook that excrescence with a hook and cut some of it, but do not overdo the cutting lest it cause a flow of tears, then instill into the canthus red or vitriol until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
Concerning the excision of the pterygium and what grows of excessive flesh in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, red flesh may grow, accumulating until it covers the pupil or approaches it or overflows onto the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids turn outward and it resembles a wild rose.
And the procedure is that the patient places his head in your lap, then opens his eyes, and you pick up all that red flesh with hooks suitable for that.
Or you grasp it with forceps or a pair of tweezers, then you cut the flesh, piece by piece, until you remove it all by cutting, and you protect the eye so that you do not injure it during the procedure, and your cutting of it may be either with the scalpel that we described in cutting the pterygium or with small scissors. Like those with which one plucks hairs, as its image will follow in the chapter that follows this chapter.
Then, when your cutting is finished and all the growth is gone, fill the eye with crushed salt or instill into it  red vitriol or similar caustic medicines, and apply a cotton compress soaked in egg white to the eye externally to prevent hot inflammation.
If some of the growth remains and the bleeding overwhelms you and you fear hot inflammation, then leave the eye and treat it with what soothes the inflammation, then return to the procedure until it heals.  Do likewise with excessive flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any eye weak by nature or by circumstance with instruments; and do not undertake any of these procedures until you see that the organ can tolerate the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the removal of pterygia from the eye.
Pterygia are red vessels that grow over the eye, hindering its function and weakening the eye over time.  First you should look; if the eye in which the pterygia are located is strong and has no other ailment besides the pterygia,
then the removal is performed by having the patient place his head in your lap, then you hook those vessels with one or two hooks, according to your skill, and the hooks should be delicately curved in this manner:.
Or they may be two double hooks in a single body, in this manner:
Then, with fine scissors, delicately remove those vessels, wiping away the blood from time to time until you see that the vessels have been removed from the eye and the bleeding has stopped.  Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and let your work be done midday, facing the sun.
And be very careful in your work, lest you cut anything other than those vessels.  Upon completion, instill into the eye green or red vitriol to cauterize any remaining pterygium. If you cannot remove it all at once, then bandage the eye with something that soothes the pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the inflammation is resolved, then repeat the procedure in the same manner until it heals. This is an image of the appropriate scissors.
And leave it [and let the patient's head rest on the pupil's lap, while another wipes away tears and keeps the eye open at the front and back, and you] the principle of the procedure is to open the eye with a fixed or triangular speculum held by an assistant.
Sharp, and let the patient's head rest on the assistant's lap, while another wipes away tears and keeps the eye open at the front and back, and you have the choice of beginning to remove it from the top or bottom of the eye; it is preferable to cut two threads at the upper eyelid, one at its end and the other at its beginning.
And grasp it between the middle and index fingers, then hook it from below opposite them, then grasp it between the little and ring fingers;  lift your hand gently until you see that the whole pterygium has been raised, then cut it from its narrowest part, if you wish, with scissors, or if you wish, with a fine scalpel.
And insert the thread onto a needle and lift the entire pterygium until it is raised from the conjunctiva, and do not cut the threads, for tears and blood will obscure your vision.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the eyelid from above and below, so that nothing remains attached except to the cornea. If you can lift it from the cornea by peeling it with the thread, that is best; and it may be possible in some people.
And when you have cut it, let its site be cauterized for several days to alleviate the pain and prevent excessive inflammation, then resume working on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
In replacing the nose.
Physicians call a nasal polyp a fistula. If you treat it with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine as previously described, and it does not heal, then there is no recourse except to incise the swelling when it is ripe and to extract all the moisture or pus within.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument of this shape:
The rasp, which is made of Indian iron, is called the head. Its head is round like a button, finely engraved with the pattern of a file or rasp. You place it on the corrupted part of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers, while slightly pressing down with your hand, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away.
Do this repeatedly, then dress the wound with drying, astringent medicines. If the wound heals, flesh grows in it, and the discharge stops, and it remains for forty days without recurrence, suppuration, or any incident,
know that it has healed. Otherwise, there is no recourse except to return the fistula to the nasal cavity in this manner:  You again expose the bone with an instrument or with a sharp medicine. When the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The tip of the iron instrument is triangular, and its shaft is conical, tapering towards the tip, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the ethmoid bone, and a little away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
He will feel air escaping from it if he holds his hand over his nose. Then dress the wound with the astringent, drying medicines as I have taught you. If flesh grows and the place hardens, then the matter which used to flow outwards will return to the nose, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In returning a protrusion of the eye.
If the whole eye protrudes and no damage or deficiency occurs in the sight, then you should relax the patient, then bleed him in the temples, then apply a cupping glass to the nape of the neck without restriction, and gently suck.
Then apply a poultice made of acacia, aloes, frankincense, and bdellium to the eye, and then bind the eye over the poultice with many soft bandages.  The method for the bandages is that you take many soft rags, and fold each rag into four layers, making many of them.
Then place them one over the other as much as the eye needs, then bind it tightly with a firm cloth, and repeat the cupping with suction without restriction, then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened gently. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise, repeat the poultice, bandages, binding, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the excision of the uvula.
If a hernia occurs in the uvular layer, then it protrudes and becomes prominent outside the eyelids like a grape, and thus the person's appearance is disfigured, and you wish to excise it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It is that you insert a needle into the base of the uvula from below upwards, then insert another needle with doubled thread from the side of the palate and pass it through, and leave the first needle as it is.
Then you cut the place where the doubled thread bends and tie some of the uvula around the needle with it and pull it firmly, then you remove the needle and place on the eye wool moistened with egg white and leave it until the threads fall out with the uvula, then you treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  You may also pull the uvula from every direction with a circle of cloth so that the eye is pulled even more firmly, then you incise with a fine scalpel reaching to the depth of the eye, so that the white moisture flows and the eye descends and withers in place, then you pull it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of canker.
This ailment which is called canker is a fluid which gathers in the eye resembling flowing water, but it is not. The method of treatment is that you seat the patient upright on a chair, then take his head in your hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the fluid go to the lower part of the eye, then it will remain there and not move, and the light will emerge and the patient will see things as he used to see them.  If it does not descend to the lower part we know that it is water. If the fluid does not descend as mentioned,
then seat the patient before you, then take a thin lancet and make a small incision with it above the cornea at the point where the conjunctiva joins the cornea in the ciliary body until the matter comes out.
When it has come out, instill in the eye warm water in which honey has been mixed, or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already mentioned the types of water in the classification and which of them is suitable for cupping, in a detailed and explained statement, so take that from there correctly. Then you should seat the patient before you, sitting cross-legged facing the light near the sun.
And you should bind his sound eye and bind it tightly, then lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye in which the water is, is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the cupper near the cornea, with the thickness of a probe, in the same white of the eye towards the smaller canthus, then you push the cupper forcefully while you rotate it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel with the cupper that it has reached something empty.
And it should be that the extent of the cupper's penetration into the depth is the distance that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, which is the cornea of the eye, for the copper is seen by the viewer himself in the eye because of the clarity of the corneal membrane, then you move the cupper upwards to the place where the water is.
Then press it downwards time after time, and if the water descends immediately, then the patient will see what his sight has been opened to immediately, and the cupper is in his eye, then it will remain still a little, and if the water rises, then lower it again without removing the cupper.
Then if it settles and does not rise, remove the cupper gently while you twist it with your hand little by little, then dissolve in the water a little pure Andaranian salt and wash the eye with it from the inside, then place on the outside of the whole eye a compress or wet wool.
With rose oil and egg white and bind it with the healthy eye, but as for us and the people of our time, we place on it crushed cumin with egg white. And if the cupper does not enter the eye because of its hardness, because some people have very hard eyes. Then it is necessary to take the scalpel which is called al-barīd, whose image is this:
Then pierce with it only the adherent part and do not go too far in the piercing, but rather it is that you make a delicate place for the cupper, then insert the cupper as we mentioned, and when your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm lying-down place where he sleeps on his back in a dark room.
And he abstains from all movements and from coughing, and you make his food that which softens his constitution, and he does not move his head to the right or left at all, and the bandage remains in place until the third day, then you untie it in that dark room and test his sight.
And you show him things, then return the bandage until the seventh day, and it is not proper to do that at the time of treatment or after the cupping from your hour, but rather it is necessary to avoid that before the water rises quickly with intense sight.
If a hot swelling appears, then it is necessary to untie the eye before the seventh and treat that with what calms the swelling, until when it calms, then at that time you release his sight and place on the face a kerchief that trains his sight from under it for days while he is in that dark room, then...
He leaves the house gradually and manages his affairs, and know that in cupping the learner cannot dispense with observation many times, then at that time he proceeds to the work, and it has reached me from some Iraqis that he mentioned that he makes in Iraq a penetrating cupper with which he draws out the water.
And I have not seen anyone in our country make that nor read it in a book from the books of the ancients, and it is possible that that is recent, and this is an image of types of cupping instruments for one ignorant of them to stand upon:
It is made of copper especially, and its end is with this very thinness, triangular-ended, sharp.
Chapter twenty-four.
In the treatment of the flesh growing in the nose.
There may grow in the nose different kinds of superfluous flesh, some of it resembling a scorpion with many legs, and some of it being cancerous flesh, hardened, dark-colored, and some of it being soft flesh, not dark-colored.
As for that which is soft flesh, not malignant nor cancerous, then it is necessary that you seat the patient before you facing the sun, and open his nostril and cast the snare into that flesh, then draw it outwards, then cut what you have grasped of it with a fine, sharp scalpel from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone. If any of it remains that you were unable to cut, then remove it with one of the fine instruments gently, until nothing of it remains. If the bleeding overcomes you, or a hot swelling appears,
then treat it as is necessary. Or if it was from the malignant tumors, then hasten to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone, then cast into the nose after the cutting vinegar and water or a drink. If the nose opens and the moisture passes from it to the throat,
know that he is healed. If the moisture does not pass as it should, then know that inside there is flesh growing on the top of the porous bones, which the instrument did not reach with the cutting. Then it is necessary that you take a linen thread of some thickness.
and tie many knots in it, and make between each knot the space of a finger or less. Let the patient skillfully insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or with whatever is possible for him after making it like a button, and draw his breath until it reaches the choana.
and it comes out at his throat. Often boys in school do this kind of action, and it is a matter easy for whoever wants to do it. Then gather the two ends of the thread, the one end which came out at the mouth and the other which remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to saw the flesh, doing that until you know that the flesh has been cut by the knots of the thread. Then take out the thread, and place in the nose after wiping the blood a wick which has soaked in Egyptian ointment, doing that for three days or more.
until the ointment eats away all that remains of the flesh. Then, as the last thing, put a lead tube in the nose for some days until it heals. If it needs treatment to dry it, use that.
And this is an image of the speculum with which to instill ointments and medicines into the nose:
It is made of silver or copper, similar to a small, open candlestick, and its passageway as well.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the speculum's passage flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when you heat the ointment or whatever you wish of juices or flowing things in it.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
A wart often grows on the tip of the nose, and it increases and grows larger with the days until its appearance becomes ugly.
Therefore, it should be cut in its first appearance and completely excised, then apply to the area either cautery or a burning medicine that acts in place of cautery. If you miss cutting it until it becomes large, then look: if it is hardened, solid, dark in color, and insensitive,
do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous tumor; and I have often seen that the cutting of this tumor resulted in great harm to its owner. But if the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that it can be completely cut, then let the patient vomit and cut it without hesitation or delay, and treat the area with what dries and constricts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is separated by a wound or something similar, know that when a separation of the connection occurs in one of these cartilages, the treatment rarely benefits except in some people.
Then, if something like that happens to someone, see if the wound is fresh with its blood. If so, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures, then treat it until it heals. And if the separation of the connection has separated its edges, and each cut is clean, then you should scrape each cut to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then bring the edges together with sutures, tighten them, and sprinkle on them mastic and frankincense, powdered.  Then place on top of the sutures a plaster of walnut ointment or another adhesive ointment, and leave it tied for two or three days, then untie it.
And change the medication and leave it until the threads fall out on their own, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The method of suturing is to bring together the separated connection, either with needles, as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread, as I explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the nodes which appear in the lips.
Many people develop small, hard tumors inside their lips; some resemble lentils, others are smaller or larger.  You should evert the lip, incise each nodule, and extract it with a hook.
Then cut it from each side, and after cutting, pack the area with powdered alum to stop the bleeding. Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the area with a styptic until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cutting away excess gum.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called epulis. You should hook it with a hook or grasp it with forceps and cut it at its base. Allow the blood to flow, then apply powdered alum to the area.
Or one of the astringent, drying powders. If the flesh returns after treatment—for it often does—cut it again and cauterize it; it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On extracting teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly crusts may gather on the surfaces of the teeth, inside and out, and between the gums.  These may become black, yellow, or green, even causing damage to the gums and disfiguring the teeth.
You should seat the patient before you, his head in your lap, and clean the tooth or teeth showing crusts and anything resembling sand until nothing remains.  Do the same with blackness, greenness, yellowness, and anything else, until you have cleansed them.
If the material is removed during the first cleaning, good. Otherwise, repeat the cleaning on another day, and then a second and third, until you achieve your goal. Know that a tooth may require different shaped and numerous instruments, according to what suits your work.
For the instrument used to clean a tooth from the inside is not the same as that used to clean it from the outside, nor the one used to clean between the teeth, which is of yet another form.  These are several forms of instruments, all of which you should have prepared:
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat a painful tooth by every means and delay its extraction, for there is no replacement for it once extracted, because it is a noble substance, except when its extraction is unavoidable.
If the patient is determined to have it extracted, you should make sure that you have correctly identified the painful tooth. Often the patient is deceived by the pain and thinks it is in a sound tooth, so he extracts it, but the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted. We have seen this done repeatedly by barbers.
If you have correctly identified the painful tooth, then you should incise around the tooth with a strong scalpel to loosen the gum on all sides, then move it gently with your fingers or with delicate forceps, little by little, until you loosen it.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps, holding the patient's head between your knees so that he does not move; then pull the tooth straight out so as not to break it. If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and gently insert it under it from all sides, and then move it as you did before.
If the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill the hole with a cloth and pack it tightly with the end of a thin probe, lest it crumble when you pull on it with the forceps; and you should thoroughly incise around the gum on every side.
Take care not to break it, for if some of it remains, it will cause the patient a greater affliction than his initial pain. And beware of doing what ignorant barbers do, rushing in and extracting it without using what we have described.
For they often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or it may be extracted with some of the jawbone, as we have witnessed many times. Then, after its extraction, let him rinse with a potion or with vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the site—and this often happens—then crush some alum and plug the site with it.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the alum does not help. The image of the delicate forceps with which you first move the tooth: they should have long ends, short handles, and be thick so that they do not bend when you grasp the tooth with them.  And this is the image of the large forceps:
They should be, as you see, thick in the handles, so that when you grasp them they do not give way or bend; short in the ends; and made of Indian iron or of well-tempered steel, with polished ends, and at the end teeth that interlock, so that they grasp firmly and tightly.
And the ends may be made in the shape of a file, which will also make them strong in their grip.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the extraction of the roots of molars and the removal of fractured jawbones.
If, when extracting a tooth, a root remains broken, you should apply a pledget of cotton soaked in butter to the area for a day or two until the area relaxes, then insert the forceps or hooks. whose ends resemble the beak of the bird called the balarjah.  And this is an image of the hooks:
They should be made like a file on the inside, or like a chisel. If you cannot extract it with these hooks, you should drill into the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert the instrument resembling a small lever, whose image is this:
short at the tip, somewhat thick, and not too pointed, lest it break. If the root is extracted by this means, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments, whose images are these: the first has a triangular tip and some thickness.
Another fine triangular image:
And we may also use this instrument with two branches, whose image is also this:
And other instruments and tools previously mentioned in the extraction of teeth.  We may also use this instrument resembling a large hook, whose image is this:
Triangular at the curved tip, somewhat thick so that it does not break, and not too pointed. Know that the instruments for teeth are numerous, as are all the other instruments—they are almost countless—and the skillful and experienced craftsman may invent for himself instruments according to what the work and the diseases themselves indicate.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention instruments, because of their different types.  If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth is broken or becomes infected, then search for it in its place with one of these instruments and hooks mentioned in the extraction of roots.
And we use a curette, its image is this:
It should be somewhat thick to grasp the bone so that it does not slip until the bone is removed. We treat the area with appropriate medication. If the bone has slight decay, scrape away its decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the extraction of molars growing on top of others.
If molars grow in a place other than their natural position, this is unsightly, especially in women and slaves.  One should examine it; if the molar has grown behind another tooth,
and its extraction or repositioning is impossible, extract it. If it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, whose image is this:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron.  The cutting should be done over many days because of the tooth's hardness, so as not to loosen other teeth.  But if it is protruding and its extraction is possible, extract it with a file from India, whose image is this.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, very finely worked, like the file used to make needles.  File the tooth little by little over many days, gently, so as not to loosen the tooth and cause it to fall out. Then smooth it further and remove it with some forceps.
If a tooth is broken and hurts the tongue when speaking, it should also be filed until the roughness of the fracture is removed, it becomes smooth, and it no longer hurts the tongue or impairs speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three
On binding loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything to eat for fear that they will fall out, and if astringent medicines have been tried without success,
the remedy is to bind them with a gold or silver thread; gold is better because silver tarnishes and decays after a few days, while gold remains unchanged forever. The thread should be of medium thickness, suitable for the space between the teeth.
The method of binding is to take the thread and insert its loop between two sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until the weaving reaches a sound tooth on the other side.
Then weave back to the side where you began, pulling gently and carefully so that the teeth do not move at all.  Tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose. Then cut off the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them together, twist them, and hide them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
So that it does not injure the tongue. Leave it tied in this way as long as it remains. If it becomes loose or breaks, tie it again with another thread.  The patient will enjoy this method for a lifetime. This is an image of the teeth, showing how the binding is done between two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
A single tooth or two, after falling out, may be returned to their place and bound as described, and will remain. This is done by a skilled dentist. He may carve a piece of ox bone, shape it like a tooth, and place it in the space where the tooth was lost. And it is tied as we have said, and remains, and the patient enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four
Concerning the cutting of the ligament which appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a healed wound.
And the procedure is that you open the mouth of the patient and place his head in your lap and raise his tongue, then cut that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its restraint.  If there is some stiffness and entanglement, and this is from a healed wound, then insert a hook into it and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knots are loosened.
And beware that the incision is not deep in the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and bleeding results. Then let him rinse his mouth after the cutting with rosewater or with vinegar and cold water, then place under the tongue a pledget of linen which the patient holds in place each night.
Lest it unite again. If bleeding occurs, place powdered alum on the area. If the bleeding overcomes you, cauterize the place with a lentil-sized cautery suitable for this. Then treat it with the other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On removing a frog that has formed under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small frog may occur under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and perhaps growing until it fills the mouth. The procedure is to open the patient's mouth toward the sun and look at the tumor.
If you see it dark-colored or black, hard, and the patient feels nothing in it, do not interfere with it, for it is cancer. But if it is whitish and moist, pierce it with a hook and incise it with a fine scalpel, freeing it on all sides.
If the bleeding is excessive during the procedure, apply powdered vitriol until the bleeding stops, then resume the procedure until you remove it completely. Then, gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And what grows in the throat from other tumors;  glands may appear inside the throat, resembling those that appear externally, called tonsils. If you treat them as described in the section and they do not heal,
then look: if the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not use the cautery on it; and if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not use the cautery on it either, for fear of hemorrhage, but leave it until it matures, then either cauterize it or it will break open of its own accord.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut.  The procedure is that you should look before operating whether its hot inflammation has completely subsided or diminished somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, his head in your lap, open his mouth, and have an assistant hold down his tongue with an instrument of this form:
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue down with it and the tumor is visible, take a hook and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outward as much as possible without pulling any of the membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument of this form: it resembles scissors, except that its two ends are curved, each sharp and close to the other, made of Indian iron or Damascus steel:
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel of this form:
sharp on one side and blunt on the other. After you cut one tonsil, cut the other in the same way. Then, after cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or myrtle leaves, or similar astringents, have been boiled until the bleeding stops.  Then treat it until it heals. Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils; cut them as I described for the tonsils.
I treated a woman with a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a polyp, somewhat insensitive, almost obstructing the throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and was prevented from eating and drinking water.
She was on the verge of death if she remained for a day or two.  Two branches extended from the tumor, reaching her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserting a hook into one of them and pulling; a suitable piece was extracted.
Then I cut it where I reached it through the nostril. I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, depressed her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut some of it. Only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and eat.  We continued to cut away portions of the tumor repeatedly over a long time, the tumor replacing what was cut, until this went on for a long time for both of us.
Then I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat with a cautery. It stopped growing, and she travelled away. I do not know what God did with her afterwards.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cutting a uvular tumor.
That which is called a uvular tumor: if it descends to the uvula, swells, and is white and elongated, it is called a column; if it is thick below and round, it is called a uvular tumor. If it is treated as mentioned in the classification and the treatment is ineffective,
and I saw that the hot tumor had subsided from it and it was thin, then it should be cut, and whatever was of it that was gathered, round, and did not have length, and was bloody or the color of pus, or black, or insensitive, then it should be avoided cutting it.
For there is danger to the patient. Therefore, if you see it in the manner that I have described, of its whiteness and length, then seat the patient next to the sun, and compress his tongue with the instrument previously described, then pierce the uvular tumor with the hook.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments mentioned in cutting the tonsils.  It should not be cut except for that which exceeds the natural state without addition, for if you cut more of it, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after cutting, use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of cutting it, then it is necessary to use a method to cauterize it without fear or caution. The method of cauterization is with a strong medicine, and it is that you place the patient's head in your lap.
Then compress his tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take some of the strong water which we mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unquenched lime, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument with it. This is its image:
The end in which you place the medicine has a concavity like the concavity of a spoon, and you place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lies on his side so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat, lest anything descend into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine while you press it against the uvula for about half an hour until you see that it has blackened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. And if you wish, take cotton, wrap it around the end of a probe, and moisten the cotton in the medicine.
And insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula, then leave it; it will wither and fall after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter and wipe away the medicine from around it. Then, rinse with cold water and treat externally with lotions and internally with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and less frightening.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cautery and cutting; it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take futhung, zovfa, savory, sithab, wormwood, chamomile, and qisum, and similar herbs. Gather them all or some of them in a pot and submerge them in vinegar.
Boil it, the pot being coated with  philosopher's clay, and there should be a hole in the middle of the lid through which a hollow instrument is fitted, made in this manner, of silver or copper:
Insert the end with the rose-shaped head into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube until the uvula is thoroughly moistened. Then repeat this several times until it withers. And beware of using this treatment at the very beginning of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling. Rather, it should be done when its hot swelling subsides. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit an eggshell to its end, lest the patient's mouth be burned.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the vapor from burning the mouth. And this is a good treatment, and safe.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other things that lodge in the throat.
Often a bone or fishbone or something else lodges in the throat.  It is necessary to remove what is visible to the eye, after compressing the tongue with the instrument, towards the sun.
So that what is in the throat becomes clear to you.  And as for what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary to make the patient vomit before his food is digested in his stomach.  Perhaps the lodged object will come out with the vomit, or the patient may swallow a piece of turnip or a stalk of lettuce.
Or he may swallow a piece of dry bread, or you may take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a string, and then he swallows it. When it reaches the location of the thorn, pull the string quickly; do this repeatedly.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out. If it does not come out by the means mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this manner:
It should be slightly thicker than an awl, and at its tip a hook. The patient inserts it gently into his throat while raising his head upwards, and he should be careful not to touch his throat, lest it cause coughing and push the bone or thorn. Or the physician inserts it with his hand.
And it is better for the patient to insert it himself, because he knows the location of the lodged object, and he pushes it downwards or pulls it upwards with the instrument, all this as much as he can manage, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the section on medicinal treatment and it does not work, then look in the patient's throat in the sunlight after you have depressed his tongue with the instrument which I described to you.
If you see the leech, then pull it out with a small hook or with a soft, firm swab. If you cannot manage this, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert a rod heated in the fire into the inside of the tube.
Do this several times, and the patient should abstain from water for the whole day. Then he should take a jug full of cold water, open his mouth into it, and rinse his mouth with it, not swallowing a drop, moving the water around from time to time with his hand.
The leech will fall out when it feels the water. If it does not come out as we described, then fumigate the throat with urine and asafoetida using the instrument described for fumigating the tonsils; do this several times, and it will fall out.
The method of fumigation is that you take a pot containing glowing embers heated by fire, the pot being covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole.  You fit the end of the instrument into this hole, then throw in the fumigant.  The patient puts his mouth to the end of the tube and closes his mouth so that the fumigant does not escape, until he knows that the fumigant has reached his throat.
Then the leech will fall out. If it does not fall out, then repeat the fumigation several times. The patient should endure thirst and eat salt and garlic, and not drink water; it will necessarily come out with this treatment. This is an image of an instrument with which the leech is drawn from the throat when it is seen.
It resembles forceps, as you see, except that it has this curvature which enters the throat, and its tips resemble a bird's beak; they have the roughness of a file, so that if they grip something, they do not let go.
And another instrument with which the tongue is compressed; it is a wide iron ring, large enough to encompass the mouth over the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so that the mouth remains open without the patient's will, and he is unable to remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened in this very way.
Chapter Forty.
It will treat of the cauterization and incision of tumors.
Tumors are of many and varied kinds, as will be described one by one in this book. They differ in their cauterization and incision in two respects: one, from the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
The second type [of difference] is because of the locations in the body where they occur, for a tumor occurring in the head is not the same as a tumor occurring in the rectum, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy location is not the same as a tumor occurring in a joint; each has its own treatment.
Some tumors should not be cauterized except after the pus in them has matured and is complete, while others should be cauterized while they are still raw and have not fully matured, such as tumors that are near the joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it corrupts what is around it, it may destroy the ligaments or nerves of that joint, thus causing the dysfunction of that limb; or the tumor may be near a principal organ.
For if you delay its cauterization until it matures, you will harm that principal organ; or it may be near the rectum, so cauterize it while it is raw.  It is necessary to cauterize a tumor while it is raw and not fully mature if it is near the rectum, lest it corrupt the tissues and penetrate into the interior of the rectum and become a fistula.
And it becomes something that does not heal. You should know the time for cauterizing tumors that have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor softens, and all other signs [of maturation] appear.
And you should apply the cautery to the lowest possible part of the tumor if you can, so that the flow of pus is easier downwards, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  The cautery should be lengthwise if the tumors are in the hands or feet.
And the locations of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and in general, all flat places that do not bend. As for those that bend, apply the cautery according to that location. But if the tumor is in fleshy places, it is best to leave it uncauterized.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said, for if you cauterize it before that, the flow of pus from it will be prolonged, and it will be very harmful and dirty, and perhaps its lips and base will harden. Some tumors may be cauterized across the body, if necessary, or according to what the organ requires.
And you should use a single cautery for small tumors, and for large tumors a wide cautery or many incisions, according to the size of the tumor. There may be tumors in which it is necessary to remove the skin and cut it if it has become like a rag.
