And it has reached the point where it has died, as happens in many abscesses and hidden collections of pus.  Some are incised with a three-cornered incision, and some are cut in the shape of an ivy leaf, like an inguinal tumor.
And some require a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions. As for those that do not have a head, like smooth, flat tumors, they should only be cauterized simply. If the tumor is large and has collected for a long time, and you cauterize it, you should not rush to remove all the pus at that time.
Rather, remove some of it, then dress the tumor until the next day, then remove some of the pus also; do this repeatedly gradually until you remove all of it.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old man, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden removal of pus, so the patient may die and you do not realize it; therefore, be very cautious in this matter.
After cauterizing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and look at it. If the wound of the tumor is small, or if the incision is single and simple, then use a pledget of linen or old cotton.  If the tumor is large and the cauterization incisions are many,
then you should insert a tent into each incision until some reach each other. And if some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or thinned, then you should pack it with old cotton or linen lint without moisture and dress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after having soaked a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the management, treatment, and styptics that we have described in many places in this book and in the sections. If it is winter and the tumor site is very sensitive, you should moisten the pledgets with a warm syrup and oil.
And place them on the site. If it is summer and the sites are fleshy, you should apply pledgets soaked in water and oil or in syrup and oil, all of it cold, until the third day, as we said. Then you should remove the dressing and wipe the tumor.
And use similar treatments until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, I have mentioned how to treat each tumor, categorized and summarized.
Chapter forty-one.
On the incision of tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp; they are a type of boil, contained within membranes, with conditions like a chicken’s crop.  Their types are numerous; some are fatty, and some contain a moisture resembling mud.
Some contain a moisture resembling pus and broth, and so on; and some are hardened and solid.  There is no danger in incising and removing all of them, unless you encounter an artery during the incision.
The procedure for their incision is to first probe them with the instrument whose image comes after this, which is called the *muddass*, so that you know what it contains. If what it contains is moisture, then incise it lengthwise with a simple incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from point B to point J. When the moisture is emptied, remove the sac that contained that moisture and cut it all away, leaving nothing at all, for it often recurs if any of it remains.
Then dip cotton in yellow ointment, if available; otherwise, in salt water, and fill the wound with it, and leave it until the next day, for it will eat away what remains of the sac. Then apply the cotton with the yellow ointment again and again if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the swelling contains fatty matter, incise it in this manner:
Make a firm incision, and catch the hooks in the lips of the wound and strip it from every side.  Do your best to remove the membrane that contains it. If an artery obstructs you, do as I have described for you; similarly, act in the case of a swelling if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as I have described.  Incision of a hardened swelling is easier because it has little blood and moisture.  Some of these swellings in the head in some people may have no moisture at all; I once incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman, and I found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone; it could not be broken, and if anyone were to throw it, it would cut him.  And it was not like the other swellings occurring in the head, other than this, such as the swellings that appear in the heads of children.
And at the roots of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision, and make their base from their lower parts so that the flow of the matter to the bottom is easier, then treat them with what is suitable for them from the treatment.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning the incision of the tumors which frequently appear in the neck. These tumors frequently appear in the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groins, and they may appear in the rest of the body. That which appears in the neck may be one or many, and some of them develop from others.
And each of these tumors is within a special membrane, as is the case with abscesses and the tumors of the head as we have described. And the types of these tumors are many; some are calcified, and some contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
And I have not seen one of them in good condition to the touch, and its exterior was near the color of the skin, it moved in every direction, and it was not attached to a nerve of the neck, nor to a vein, nor to an artery, nor was it sunken. Then it should be incised with a simple incision from the top to the bottom of the body, in this manner, from line J to line B.
And it should be dissected from every direction, and the edges of the skin should be stretched with a hook or with two hooks, or with many hooks if it is necessary, as we have said in the case of the tumors of the head, and it should be removed little by little, and care should be taken not to cut a vein or a nerve, and let the scalpel not be too sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cutting, or the patient be troubled, and you cut what you do not need to cut.  If you cut a vein or an artery and it hinders you from working, place in the wound powdered glass or some of the things that stop blood.
And bind the wound and leave it until the intensity of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to fester, for the bleeding will then stop. Then return to your work until you finish, then examine with your index finger if there remain any other small tumors, then cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the tumor, then it should not be cut from its root, but it should be tied with a doubled tight thread and left until it falls off by itself without harm, then stuff the wound with old cotton which has been soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it.
And if the whole tumor is cut, then it is necessary to bring together the edges of the wound and stitch it immediately after you know that no residue remains at all. If you see that some residue of the bone of the tumor remains,
then it is necessary to aim the cutting at its root and base, and use stitching and what we mentioned.  And as for tumors containing fluids, apply a simple poultice where their maturation appears, and place the poultice on the lower side, as we said.
Then, after the poultice, use a dressing with Egyptian ointment or similar to consume what remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound has been cleansed, then treat it with ointments that generate flesh until it heals.
Chapter forty-three.
Concerning the incision of the throat for a tumor that occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the account of their words. As for those with angina, they should avoid an incision of the throat, for they will not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and the lung will be weak. But those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no illness in the windpipe, then it is necessary to use an incision of the throat to escape the harm that results from suffocation.
Therefore, if we want to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the windpipe, a small transverse incision between two rings, to the extent of the incision in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This place is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are far from it. If the physician is cowardly, he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until he reaches the windpipe, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he incises the peritoneum that we described.  The incision of the windpipe is indicated by the phlegm that comes out of it along with whatever is lacerated, and by the cessation of the voice. The wound is left open for a time. When the time during which suffocation was feared has passed, the edges of the wound are brought together from the skin.
Its incision is alone, without the cartilage. Then use medicines that grow flesh until it heals.  The author of this book said, the explanation of all this talk that we have related is only if they see that the patient's throat is obstructed by one of these tumors which were mentioned.
And the patient was close to death, and his life was about to end, they went to incise the throat so that the patient might breathe somewhat through the wound and be saved from death. And they were ordered to leave the wound open until the crisis of the disease passes, and its duration is three days or so.
Then they ordered the wound to be sutured and treated until it heals. What I witnessed myself is that a maidservant took a knife and ran it across her throat, cutting some of the lung windpipe. I was called to treat her, and I found her expiring like a slaughtered animal.  I uncovered the wound.
I found the blood that came out of the wound to be slight, and I was certain that she had not cut a vein nor an artery.  Air was coming out of the wound. I proceeded, incised the wound, and treated it until it healed.  Nothing happened to the maidservant except hoarseness in the voice, nothing more.
And after days she returned to her best condition. Therefore, we say that incision of the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-four.
Concerning the incision of the tumor that appears in the throat from the outside, and is called the throat’s elephantiasis.
This tumor, which is called the throat's elephantiasis, is a large tumor of the color of the body. It is frequent in women and is of two types.
Either it is natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it; as for the accidental, it is of two kinds: one resembling fatty swellings, the other resembling the tumor that results from the entanglement of an artery, and its incision is dangerous. Therefore, one should not at all use the iron on it unless it is small; if you probe and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles a fatty swelling and is not attached to anything of the veins, then incise it as you would incise fatty swellings. And remove it with whatever encloses it from the sac if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove all of it, then treat the area with whatever treatment is appropriate.
Chapter forty-five.
On the incision of various swellings.
Swellings are numerous in types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a swelling and an abscess, since it is difficult. I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and terrifying pains until the boiling of the matter subsides. And the suppuration is completed, then the fever and intensity subside.  A swelling is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain, and it is contained in a membranous sac, which is its special covering; it is the color of the body, and its beginning is like a lentil, and it becomes like a blister, larger or smaller.
And it is of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture. The types of moisture are numerous, as I have mentioned in the classification.  Therefore, when you come to treat a swelling, you should first probe and examine it with the instrument called the probe, the image of which comes in the chapter after this.
Assess the size of the swelling, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If a fluid of any color comes out, then incise it with a simple incision, as I have mentioned in other swellings. If no fluid comes out after the probe, then know that it is fatty.
Then make a firm incision on it in this manner, as I have taught you.  Hang it with hooks and peel the skin from each side gently, and preserve the sac if you can, so that you may remove it whole with the swelling.
If the sac is perforated during the procedure and you cannot remove it whole—and this often happens—then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part remains, however small or large, the swelling will most often return.
If it overcomes you and a small part remains, cauterize the wound with some sharp cauterizing points upon completion.  Then close the wound and apply something to calm the hot swelling, and treat it with the other treatments until it heals. If the swelling is large, stitch its edges and treat it with what unites.
If you encounter a pulsating or non-pulsating vein and bleeding occurs, immediately cauterize the area with powdered vitriol and leave it bound for two or three days until the wound becomes infected and the flow of blood stops. Then return to removing what remains of the swelling.

Chapter forty-seven.
On treating the breasts of men.
The breasts of some people may swell at puberty until they resemble women's breasts, remaining swollen and unsightly.  Whoever dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this manner.
From line B to line C. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with styptic medication, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the breast inclines downwards and becomes flaccid due to its size, as happens to women,
it is necessary to make two crescent-shaped incisions in its upper sides, each connecting to the other at their ends so that the larger line encircles the smaller one in this manner: From line B to line C, then remove the skin between the incisions and remove the fat, and use what we have mentioned of sutures and cauterization and whatever treatment is needed until it heals.
If you are unable to remove what needs to be removed because of the patient's anxiety or because of bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it consumes the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
On the excision of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the type of hard swellings contained in a sac, and some contain fluids.  As for those containing fluids, an incision should be made in a crescent shape like this.
From line B to line C. If all the fluid in the tumor is evacuated, pack it with old cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with a suitable ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the type of hard swellings, incise it as we mentioned in the incision on swellings. If the wound continues to suppurate, cauterize it as we mentioned previously.
Chapter forty-nine.
Incising a tumor that appears near an artery or vein, called aneurysma.
If an artery is injured and the skin above it heals, a tumor often results; similarly, a vein may also develop a swelling and tumor.
The signs by which one knows whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or a vein are: if the tumor is from an artery, it is oblong and deep-seated in the body; and if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel as if it has a murmur.
That which is from a vein, the tumor is round on the surface of the body. Incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many other places in the body, and if they are very large, one should avoid treating them with iron.
And those that are in the extremities or head should also be avoided.  As for those that are a swelling of the mouth of an artery, incise it in the skin lengthwise, then open the incision with hooks, then strip the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I have shown you in ligating the two arteries in the temples; then incise with a scalpel the area between the ligatures until all the blood in it comes out.
Then the tumor will resolve, and you use treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a vein incision, then you should grasp with your hand as much of the tumor and skin as you can, then insert a needle below the place you grasped with your hand, and pass it with a doubled thread until it comes out the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly with it, as I have described to you in tying the uvula from both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads will slip, then insert another needle with another thread under the whole tumor at the intersection of the first needle.
And tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its middle until what is in it comes out. Then cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied. Then place on it a compress that you have soaked in a potion and oil. Then use treatment that involves cauterization and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor which results from the twisting of a nerve.
Just as a tumor occurs in an artery and vein, so a tumor occurs in a nerve if it is struck or if it results from excessive exertion or the like.  This is most common in the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a hard tumor resembling in color the rest of the body. In most cases it is not painful, and if pressed hard the patient feels a kind of numbness.  The tumor is not deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, but not forward or backward. As for that which is in the joints, it should not be treated with iron, for it may cause harm. And as for that which is in the head or forehead...
then incise the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its base. If it is large, hang it with a hook and flay it, then remove it and close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter fifty-one.
On the excision of warts which appear on the abdomen.
Many people develop warts on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies, called fungal warts because of their resemblance to mushrooms. Their base is thin and their head thick; their lips may become inverted. Some are small, and some become very large indeed.
I once saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen; they were very like mushrooms, white and thin at the base, their lips inverted and cracked, with moisture constantly flowing from them. I excised them; one weighed about eighteen ounces, the other about six.
In excising them, observe that if the patient is moist and the wart is white, moist, and thin at the base, excise it with a broad scalpel; keep cautery irons ready in the fire, for often a great deal of blood flows when they are cut.
If the bleeding is excessive, cauterize it immediately. If you find the patient cowardly and afraid of being cut with iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the wart of this type, leaving it for two days; then tighten the lead further, continuing to tighten it.
Keep tightening until the wart is affected, so that it separates and falls off by itself without trouble. If the wart has a thick base, there is some danger and uncertainty in excising it, especially if it is in the abdomen; however, it is sometimes possible to excise half of it or a part of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat it until the wound heals. Beware of excising a wart that is dark-colored, insensitive, and foul-looking, for it is a cancerous tumor; we shall speak of cancer later.
Chapter fifty-two.
On the treatment of umbilical hernias.
Umbilical hernias result from many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum of the abdomen, from which the omentum or intestine protrudes, as occurs in other hernias; or from blood emanating from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from wind collecting there.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of the omentum, then the color of the swelling will resemble that of the body, it will be soft without pain, and will appear in various positions. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, its position will be, as we have described, more variable. If you press it with your fingers, it disappears then returns, and there may be gurgling. It increases greatly with entering the bath and with severe exertion. If it is due to moisture, then it will be soft.
It does not disappear when pressed with your hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, then along with these signs the swelling appears dark. If it is due to newly formed flesh, then the swelling will be hard, firm, and remain at a constant size.
If it is due to wind, it will be soft to the touch. The procedure in this case is to observe. If the umbilical hernia is due to blood from an artery or vein, or to wind, then you should refrain from treating it, for there is danger and uncertainty in that, as I have informed you in the chapter where I mentioned swellings arising from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical hernia is due to the intestine or omentum, then you should instruct the patient to restrain himself and stand erect and extended, then you should apply a bandage around the entire umbilicus. Then you should instruct him to lie on his back between your hands, then you should incise with a wide scalpel around the umbilicus at the place marked with ink, then you should draw the middle of the swelling upwards with a large forceps, then you should tie the incised area with strong thread or silk sinew, tightly.
The knot of the ligature should be a slip-knot. Then you open the middle of the pulled-tight swelling above the ligature and insert your index finger, and seek the intestine. If you find that the ligature has grasped it, loosen the slip-knot and push the intestine into the abdomen. If you find omentum, draw it out with forceps.
And cut off its excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, ligate it carefully and return to your work. Take two needles and thread them with strong threads, and insert the two needles into the incision that you made around the swelling, crossing them, having passed them through. Then tighten the swelling in four places on the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the constricted flesh becomes putrid and falls off by itself, or cut it if it becomes soft and putrid. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical hernia is from flesh that has grown there,
or from moisture, then it is necessary to constrict the swelling as we said, and to remove the blood or moisture that you find in it, then treat it with what will close the area.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medication, and warned against treating it with iron lest it ulcerate, and we mentioned cancer that develops in the uterus and warned against treating it.
And the ancients mentioned that when the cancer is in a place where it can all be excised, like the cancer that is in the breast or in the thigh and similar easily removed organs, especially if it is small and beginning, but if it is old and large, then it should not be touched.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve that, and the procedure, if it is possible as we said, is to first purge the patient of black bile several times, then to bleed him if there is obvious fullness in the veins.
Then you position the patient in a position that allows you to work, then you apply hooks suitable for it to the cancer, then you scrape it from every side with the skin completely, until nothing of its roots remains, and let the blood flow and do not stop it quickly.
Rather, squeeze the areas and drain all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you can, and if you encounter during the procedure great bleeding from a severed artery or vein, then cauterize until the bleeding stops, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter fifty-four.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already described in the classification the types of dropsy, how the accumulation of water occurs, and the signs of each type and its treatment with medication. That which is treated with iron is only the ascitic type; the tympanitic type and the fleshy type are absolutely not approached with iron.
For that is deadly, if you have treated this type of ascitic cancer with medications and your treatment has not been effective, then look: if the patient is extremely weak or has another illness besides the cancer, such as…
a cough or diarrhea or the like, then beware of treating him with iron, for it is dangerous. If you see that the patient is strong, has no illness other than the cancer alone, and is neither a child nor an old man, then the procedure is to make the patient stand before you. and an attendant behind him squeezes his belly with his hands and pushes the water downwards towards the pubis, then you take a pointed scalpel of this shape:
pointed on both sides, long, with a sharp point like a scalpel, except that it has a slight bluntness so that you do not go through to the intestine during the procedure and injure it. Then look: if the cancer has arisen from the side of the intestines…
then it is necessary to make the incision three fingers' breadth below the navel, directly above the pubis; if the cancer has arisen from a liver disease, let your incision be three fingers' breadth to the left of the navel; if it has arisen from a spleen disease, let the incision be on the right side, three fingers' breadth.
Nor should the incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie down, lest the discharge flow to that weak place.  Then pierce the whole skin with the instrument, and then insert the instrument into that incision and raise your hand.
with the scalpel between the skin and the peritoneum, as if you are flaying it, and the amount you flay should be the size of a fingernail or thereabouts. Then pierce the peritoneum until the scalpel reaches the empty space, which is the place of the water, then withdraw it and insert into the hole the instrument whose shape is like this.
It is like a small tube made of thin cane, silver, copper, or smooth, polished lead. It has a small hole at its lower end and three holes in its sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And it may have its sharpened end shaped like a pen nib, in this manner:
At its upper end is a ring. For when the instrument reaches the water, it will immediately flow down the instrument, and a moderate amount of water will be evacuated at once. For if you evacuate more than is necessary at once, the patient may die from the dissolution of his vital spirit.
Or he may suffer a fainting fit close to death. But evacuate according to his strength and what the patient's condition indicates to you of the strength of his pulse and the good color of his complexion. Then remove the instrument and the water will be retained, for it will be retained immediately.
Because of the skin which holds the hole in the peritoneum, which I told you to suture in that manner. Then return the instrument another day, if you see the patient able to bear it.
And you will also evacuate a little water, doing this for several days while you are watchful and cautious to avoid error, until only a little water remains.  If you fear for the patient and leave a large amount of water, then treat him by burial in hot sand.
And excessive sweating in the bath and sun, and accustoming him to thirst, and treating him with drying medicines until he recovers.  And cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after evacuating the water as I described above.
Chapter fifty-five.
In the treatment of children who are born with their urinary passages imperforate, or with a narrow opening, or in the wrong place.
Some boys may be born with their rectum imperforate;  it is necessary to perforate it immediately upon birth.
With a very fine scalpel in this manner:
Then place a thin lead nail in the opening and bind it and keep it there for three or four days. When he wants to urinate, remove it and then replace it. If you do not use lead, it is possible that the urine passing through the place will not allow it to close.
As for the one whose opening is narrow, it is treated with lead as we have said for many days until it widens.  And those whose opening is in the wrong place—some are born with the opening at the end of the rectum, and they are unable to urinate forward.
until he raises the urethra upwards with his hand, and it is not born before because the semen is unable to reach the uterus straight, and this is a very bad condition. The method of treatment is that the patient lies on his back.
Then stretch his rectum with your left hand with a strong pull and incise the tip of the urethra with a sharp blade or scalpel, the size of a pen, or as if you are carving something so that its middle is prominent, similar to a penis, and the opening is located in the middle as it should be.
And be careful during your work not to cause bleeding, for this often happens; therefore, stop the bleeding with something that cuts it off, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
On the ulcer that appears in the foreskin and penis, and the blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the foreskin to the penis.
This ulcer often appears in the urethra; it is a fleshy, hard growth, and there is a malignant and a non-malignant type.  The non-malignant type should be tied off with a fine ligature.
And cut it until you have completely removed it, then apply a pledget of cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it afterwards with palm ointment until it heals. But if the ulcer is malignant and dark in color, then you should cauterize it after cutting and removing it.
If the ulcer is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and part of the ulcer is inside the foreskin and part outside, then you should remove the ulcer from the inside first, until it heals; then treat it from the outside.
Because if you treat both at the same time, you risk perforating the foreskin.  It may also appear in the labia and in the foreskin, blackness and corruption; then you should excise everything that has blackened and threatens to become corrupt, or that has already become corrupt.
Then apply honey mixed with crushed and sifted pomegranate peel and comfrey, then treat it with other treatments until it heals. If bleeding occurs, use cauterization with a crescent-shaped cautery iron, in this manner:
For cauterization is beneficial for both conditions, I mean, bleeding and the wound if it becomes corrupt. If the ulcer is consumed and disappears entirely at some point, then you should insert a lead tube into the urethra so that the patient can urinate through it.  As for the adhesion of the foreskin to the ulcer…
This adhesion only occurs in those whose foreskin is intact and who have not been circumcised.  It may also occur due to a wound or swelling. Then you should separate it with a blunt scalpel until the constriction is loosened and the ulcer is freed from all sides.
If it is difficult to distinguish them upon close examination, then you should separate some of the ulcer and some of the foreskin.  Because the foreskin is thin, it may quickly perforate due to its thinness. Then separate the foreskin from the ulcer with a thin linen cloth soaked in cold water so that it does not adhere again.
Then treat it with an astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter fifty-seven.
On the cleansing of boys and the treatment of what may befall them of ailments.
Circumcision is nothing other than the separation of a connection like other wounds, except that since it is done by our will and is used especially in boys, it is necessary to describe the best procedure in it.
And the easiest way leading to safety.  I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is something we have acquired.
by experience.  And that is because I have found the majority of surgeons and barbers using cleansing with the scalpel and with scissors, and they use the curette and ligature with thread and cutting with the fingernail. I have tried all these methods.
I did not find better than cleansing with scissors and ligature with thread, because cleansing with the scalpel often adheres to the skin, because the skin of the prepuce is two layers, so perhaps the upper skin is cut and the lower layer remains.
so one is forced to make another cut and renewed pain, and cleansing with the curette does not prevent cutting the end of the urethra, because it may enter its hole. As for cleansing with the fingernail, the skin may slip and your work will be spoiled, or the boy's skin may be naturally short.
Therefore, many are born like that and do not need cleansing, and I have seen that. As for cleansing with scissors and ligature with thread, experience has revealed to me its excellence, because the cut of the scissors is proportionate, because the blade that is above is like the blade that is below.
So when you squeeze your hand with the proportion of the two blades, you cut to one measure and in one time, so the thread becomes like a wall for the skin of the urethra from all sides, and there is no mistake at all. The method of operation, firstly, is to reassure the boy.
Especially if he is one who understands a little that you are only tying the thread in his urethra and leaving him until another day, then rejoice him and please him in every way you can and that he accepts with his mind, then stand him up erect before you.
And do not let him sit, and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the boy's eye does not fall upon it at all or upon any of the instruments, then you insert your hand into his urethra and inflate the skin and lift it up until the head of the urethra comes out.
Then cleanse it from whatever filth gathers there, then tie the marked place with a doubled thread, then tie a second ligature a little below it, then grasp with your thumb and forefinger the lower ligature firmly and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly raise the skin upwards and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and causes less swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some of the burnt ashes of dried gourd, for it is the best thing I have tried, or fine barley flour, for it is also excellent.
Then quickly raise the skin upwards and bring out the head of the urethra, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and causes less swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some of the burnt ashes of dried gourd, for it is the best thing I have tried, or fine barley flour, for it is also excellent. Then apply on top a pledget of white cooked egg in rose water, mixed with fresh fragrant rose oil, and leave it on until the next day, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And this is the image of the scissors which are suitable for circumcision:
It should be sharp-edged, cutting, not crooked, with a well-fitting rivet; and the length of the two blades should be equal to the length of the handles. As for the mistake that occurs in circumcision, it may be that the whole or part of the inner skin is inverted during the cutting; so you should extend it immediately with your fingernail before the place swells.
And cut it evenly; if you cannot grasp it with your fingernail, then pull it with a hook and cut it. If three days have passed and what is under the head of the urethra remains swollen and inflamed, then leave it until the acute inflammation subsides and then remove it gently.
And cut it as you see fit, and be careful of the glans penis; for if something of the glans penis is cut, it does not harm, so treat it with what closes the wound from the pledgets which we described in the chapter on pledgets.
And if more of the skin than necessary is cut and it retracts upwards, this also does not do much harm; so treat it as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter fifty-eight.
On the treatment of urine retention in the bladder.
Urine retention in the bladder is due to its obstruction by a stone, or clotted blood, or pus, or a growth, and the like.  If you treat that with what we mentioned of the types of treatment described in the classification.
And if the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is because of a stone that has lodged in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that the patient sit on his knees, then someone should sit on his back, and stretch his belly towards his back, and the patient should hold himself as much as he can.
Then the neck of the bladder will be forced to push the stone backward, and the urine will flow. If it does not flow by what we mentioned, and the matter becomes severe for the patient, then it is necessary to use its removal with the instrument called a catheter, whose image is this:
It is made of silver, and it is thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's feather in thinness, about a span and a half long, with a fine funnel at its head. The way to draw the urine with it is to take a doubled thread, and tie to its end wool or cotton firmly.
And insert the end of the thread into the bottom of the catheter, and cut with scissors if there is any excess of the wool, so that it enters the tube and closes it like a button. Then lubricate the catheter with oil or butter or egg white, and have the patient sit on a chair.
And clean his bladder and urethra with moist ointments or oil and lukewarm water. Then gently insert the catheter into the urethra until it reaches the root of the urethra, then bend the urethra upwards towards the navel, then push the catheter inside.
Until the urethra is fully bent, then push the catheter inside until it reaches near the rectum, incline the penis downwards with the catheter inside, then push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels that it has reached something hollow.
This is done in this manner because the passage through which the urine passes is tortuous. Then gently pull the thread with the wool; the urine will follow the wool. Then remove it, and the urine will flow out, and replace the catheter.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter Fifty-Nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
And the instruments suitable for this are made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as pus.  And if you wish to instill waters and medicines into it, that is done with an instrument called a syringe, and this is its image:
It is made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as a hair, entirely hollow except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And the hollow part in which the piston is, should be just the size to fill it without excess, so that when you draw up some moisture with it, it is drawn up, and when you push it, it is pushed further, like the syringe with which oil is thrown in sea battles.
Then, if you wish to introduce moisture into the bladder, you insert the tip of the syringe into the moisture and draw the piston upwards, whereupon the moisture is drawn into the body of the syringe. Then you insert its tip into the urethra, as we described in the catheters, and then push the moisture with the piston.
Then that moisture reaches the bladder immediately, so that the patient feels it. And this is the image of a delicate clyster also used to inject the bladder:
It is made of silver or lead, its upper end resembling a small funnel, and below it a groove in which the ligature is placed. Then you take a camel's bladder and put in it the moisture which you wish to inject into the bladder; then you tie it tightly between the two parts with a doubled thread.
And you warm that moisture slightly over the fire, then insert the tip of the clyster into the urethra, then press your hand firmly on the bladder with the moisture until the patient feels that the moisture has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around. Then insert a strong thread into those holes as it goes round, then gather the circle as you gather a tablecloth after you have put in it what you want of moisture, ointments, and water.
Then tie it to the instrument and do with it as you did with the bladder, pressing your hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On the Extraction of a Stone.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and mentioned the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and the signs of all that, and mentioned the stones in which the hand and incision are used.
And this is the stone generated especially in the bladder, and that which becomes fixed in the urethra, and I am describing that with clear explanation and brevity.
So I say that the stone generated in the bladder most often affects boys, and among its signs is that urine exits from the bladder similar to water in its thinness.
And sand appears in it, and his penis itches and he plays with it, and it often hangs down then spreads, and the anus protrudes with it in many of them, and it is easy to remove from boys until they reach fourteen years, and it is difficult in old men, and as for young men, it is intermediate between that.
And the one whose stone is larger, his treatment is easier, and the small one is the opposite.  So if we proceed to treatment, it is necessary first that the patient be injected with an enema which removes all the feces in his intestines, for it may prevent the presence of the stone upon examination.
Then the patient is taken by his feet and shaken and jolted downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place repeatedly, then you seat him between your hands, erect, and his hands under his thighs so that the whole bladder becomes inclined downwards, then you examine him.
And palpate him from the outside. If you feel the stone in the space, then immediately begin to incise it.  If it does not fall under your touch at all, then you should lubricate the index finger with oil from the left hand if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If he is a fully grown boy, then you insert it into his rectum and search for the stone, until when it falls under your finger, you move it little by little to the neck of the bladder, then you press on it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place you wish to incise.
And you order a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and you order another servant to extend with his right hand the labia upwards, and with his other hand he extends the skin under the labia away from the place where the incision will be. Then you take the sharp scalpel, whose image is this:
And you incise between the rectum and the labia, not in the middle, but to the left of the anus. The incision should be on the stone itself, and your finger in the rectum, as you press it outwards obliquely so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, to the extent that the stone can exit from it.
Not larger, for perhaps the finger in the rectum will press the stone during the incision, and it will come out without difficulty. And know that there may be stones that have angles and edges, making their removal difficult because of that.
And some are smooth, resembling an acorn and round, so their removal is easy. But those that have angles and edges, enlarge the incision a little. If it does not come out like this, then you should devise a way, either by grasping it with a tight forceps whose end is like a file.
To secure the stone so that it does not escape from it, or to insert underneath it a delicate instrument with a hooked end. If you cannot manage it, enlarge the incision a little. If you are overcome by any blood, stop it with alum.
If there is more than one, push the largest one first towards the opening of the bladder, then incise over it, then push the smaller one afterwards. Do likewise if there are more than two. If it is very large, it is foolish to make a large incision over it, because it exposes the patient to one of two things.
Either he will die or he will have a constant trickle of urine because the place will not heal at all. But try to push it out, or devise a way to break it with forceps until you remove it in pieces.
When you have finished your work, cauterize the wound with myrrh, aloes, and acacia, and dress it, and place over it a cloth moistened with oil and syrup or rose oil and cold water to calm the hot swelling. Then let the patient lie on his back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day.
When it is loosened, wash the place with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with emollient ointment and basilicon ointment until it heals. If a hot, excessive swelling and gangrene or something similar occurs in the wound, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing the passage of urine, this is known from the passage of blood with the urine.
Insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains it will lead to corruption and putrefaction of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type with a similar treatment until it heals.
Throughout the treatment, it is necessary to bind and bring together the thighs to secure the medicines placed on the area. If the stone is small and is in the urethra, and is stuck there, and the urine is prevented from coming out, treat it with what I have described before it necessitates incision.
Often I have dispensed with incision by this treatment, which I have tested. It is to take a probe of steel, which is of this form:
The tip is triangular, sharp, and fixed in a stick. Then take a thread and tie the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder, then gently insert the probe into the urethra until the probe reaches the stone itself.
And turn the probe in the stone itself little by little, intending to pierce it until you pass through it to the other side. Then the urine will flow immediately. Then squeeze what remains of the stone from outside the penis, for it will crumble and come out with the urine, and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not feasible for you due to some impediment, tie a thread below the stone and another thread above the stone, then incise upon the stone in the penis itself between the two threads, and then remove it.
Then untie the ligature and cleanse the clotted blood which has formed in the wound.  The reason for tying the thread below the stone is to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other above so that when the thread is untied after the stone has been removed, the skin will return to its place and cover the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should raise the skin upwards so that it will return and cover the wound when you are finished, as we said.
Chapter sixty-one.
On the removal of stones in women.
Stones are rarely generated in women; but if one should appear in a woman, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One is that the woman may be a virgin; another, that you will not find a woman who permits herself to a physician.
If she is chaste or one of his relatives, and thirdly, that you will not find a woman who is skilled in this craft, especially in working with the hands; and fourthly, that the place of incision for stones in women is far from the location of the stone, so it requires a deep incision.
And in that there is danger. If necessity demands it, then a skilled female physician should be sought; but they are rarely found.  If you cannot find one, seek a chaste and gentle male physician, or procure a skilled midwife experienced in women's matters, or a woman who has some knowledge of this craft.
Then prepare her and instruct her to do everything you order her to do, first examining for the stone.  That is, she should see if the woman is a virgin.  If so, she should insert a finger into her rectum and search for the stone; if she finds it and presses it beneath her finger,
then you should order the incision. If she is not a virgin but a widow, then order the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If she finds it, she should move it from the opening of the bladder downwards as far as she can reach, until she reaches the groin, then incise at the midpoint of the vulva at the groin, whichever side is most accessible and where she feels the stone.
And her finger should not leave the stone, pressing beneath it. Let the incision be small at first, then insert the dilator into that small incision. If she feels the stone, enlarge the incision as much as she thinks necessary for the stone to come out. Know that there are many kinds of stones.
Some are small and some are large, some smooth and some rough, some long and some round, some with branches.  Know their types so that you may know what to do. If the bleeding overcomes you, sprinkle powdered verdigris on the area and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
Until the stone comes out.  And be sure to have with you the instruments that I mentioned for removing stones from men, to assist you in your work. If bleeding overcomes you and you know from the pulse of the blood that it is from a severed artery, then apply the styptic.
And bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it and do not touch it again, and leave the stone and do not remove it, for the patient might die. Then treat the wound.  When the severity of the bleeding has subsided after some days and the area has become infected, return to your work until you remove the stone.
Chapter Sixty-two.
On the incision on the water bladder.
The water bladder is merely an accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle, which is called the scrotum.  It may be in a special membrane prepared for it by nature in a part of the testicle.
So that one thinks it is another testicle. And it is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum that we mentioned, but this occurs only rarely. This bladder is generated from weakness that affects the testicles so that this material settles there, and it may result from a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture is of many colors; it may be yellowish, or bloody red, or blackish, or whitish—and this is most common.  And the signs by which it is known…
Where the water gathers, if it is in the white peritoneum that we mentioned, then the swelling is round, slightly elongated, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it from all sides.
And if the moisture is in a special membrane for it, then the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, a person imagines that it is another testicle. And if the moisture is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum, then it is palpable. But if you wish to know the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe whose image we have shown previously; then whatever comes out in the wake of the probe, you judge it according to what is within.
Then if we proceed to treatment with cautery, it is necessary that you order the patient to be bled if he is able and you see his body is full.  Then he should lie on his back on something slightly raised, and you should place many cloths beneath him, then you sit on his left.
And you order a servant to sit on his right, stretching his penis to one side of the scrotum's skin and toward the region of the groin. Then you take a broad scalpel and incise the skin of the scrotum in the middle lengthwise to near the pubis.
And make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum's skin in two, until you reach the white peritoneum containing it.  Then peel it back and be careful not to cut it; your peeling should be from the side attached to the testicle more, and complete the peeling as much as you can.
Then widely flatten the peritoneum filled with water and remove all the water. Then separate the edges of the incision with hooks and stretch the peritoneum upwards, and do not touch the containing scrotum skin, and cut the peritoneum however you can cut it, either entirely or piece by piece.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely cut it, you will not be safe from the water returning. If the testicle protrudes outside its skin during your work, then when you have finished cutting the peritoneum, return it, then close the edges of the scrotum skin with sutures.
Then treat it like other wounds until it heals. If the testicle is affected by another disease, then you should tie the vessels in the spermatic cord for fear of bleeding, then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle, then treat it as we have mentioned.
If the collected water is in both sides, then know that they are two hydroceles.  Incise the other side as you did the first, and if it is easier for you to do the work in one go, then do so. Then wipe away the blood.
And insert into the incisions wool that you have soaked in oil or rose ointment, and outside place more wool that you have soaked in a potion and oil, and spread it over the testicles and the flanks of the abdomen, and place folded cloths on top, which are the compresses. And tie them from above with a bandage which has six ends, in this manner:
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together, and stuffed with fluffy wool in this manner.  Make the ties with which it is fastened from cotton or wet wool, and gather them tightly in the waistband of the trousers; one end is taken to the buttocks and back.
The second is taken towards the pubis, and the two ends to the thighs below, and the remaining two ends to the groins. Gather all the tightening in the waistband of the trousers. This hydrocele may also be treated by cauterization with a hot iron instead of incision.
And it is that you take a fine, knife-like cautery and with it incise the skin of the scrotum while it is hot, as we described, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed; then take another cautery of this shape:
It resembles the Greek eye. Then cauterize that peritoneum with it while it is hot until all the moisture comes out. Then stretch the peritoneum with hooks and flay the containing layer with the cautery and cut it as much as you can until you excise it all, and take care that the testicle is not touched by the fire.
Know that this procedure is safer and less prone to bleeding, and better than the incision method. If during the incision or cauterization you encounter a hot swelling, bleeding, or something else, you should treat whatever arises with the appropriate treatment, as previously described.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure because of its unpleasantness, then use cauterization as I describe, if it seems appropriate to you in light of what we have presented.
If the hydrocele is watery, then the patient should sit on a high chair, then cauterize the lower part of the swelling with a wide scalpel.  Make the cauterization along the length of the body and make the opening wide so that all the water flows out. Then apply cotton to the area and bind it.
Leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out during cauterization, it is because the tunica albuginea is obstructing the opening of the wound, preventing the water from coming out.
Then you should insert a goose feather into the wound, or the instrument used to extract the water from hydroceles, the image of which has been shown previously, or slightly enlarge the opening of the wound.
Know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so. If it does, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should abstain from sexual activity for some days.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
Concerning the incision on the fleshy scrotum and its treatment.
Know that an incision on this scrotum is one of the most dangerous things, leading to death in most cases.  Therefore, we consider leaving it alone and avoiding it to be safer. However, I will mention the procedure and the types of tumor.
I say that many tumors may occur in the bodies which comprise the female genitalia, and this is due to many causes, either from a sharp, acrid humor descending to the genitalia or from a blow. The tumor is the same color as the body, and it does not contain water.
The tumor may be soft, and perhaps the tumor may be hardened, its color dark and without sensation. It may be from the twisting and swelling of the arteries, as previously mentioned, or from the swelling of the veins.  That which is from the swelling of the arteries is known by the dispersal of the tumor.
If you press it with your fingers, it does not disperse. It should absolutely not be incised.  As for that which is from the swelling of the veins, nothing disperses from it when pressed with the fingers. This type may possibly be incised, as well as other fleshy parts.
And it is that you incise the skin of the testicle, then draw the testicle upward and bring it out of the white peritoneum, and free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and tie the vessels, and cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from every part of the testicle.  If the testicle has adhered to the growing flesh…
then you should remove and cut the testicle. And if the adhesion is between some of the peritoneum or among the vessels, then you should free all that adhesion and cut it with a circular incision.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is behind, then you should cut all of it and remove the testicle as we said. When your work is completed, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-four.
In the treatment of the hydrocele that is with a hernia.
A hernia is a twisted tumor, somewhat twisted, similar to a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the intestines, and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.  The treatment of this condition is similar to what was mentioned before, and is fraught with danger.
However, we should mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that you should make the patient sit on a high chair, then push the intestinal contents downwards, then grasp the skin of the scrotum with your fingers along with the vessels which are close to the penis.
And let an assistant hold it and stretch it tightly, then you incise with a wide, sharp scalpel, making an oblique incision along the vessels until the vessels are exposed.  Then dissect from each side as I described to you in the dissection of the arteries in the armpits, then insert a needle with a doubled thread.
And tie it at the first place where the hernia appeared and tie it also at its end, then incise it in the middle with a vertical incision along its length and remove what has collected within it of foul, putrid fluids.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds which you want to heal over time, so that the vessels which nourish one of the intestines fall away; this is not a problem. But if the hernia involves all the vessels, then it is necessary to remove one of the intestines along with the vessels, lest the testicle lack nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, they wither and become unusable.
Chapter Sixty-five.
On the treatment of intestinal hernia.
This hernia occurs from a tear which appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen near the groins, from the flanks of the abdomen, and the intestine descends from that rupture into one of the testicles.
And this rupture is either from a tear in the peritoneum or from its extension.  These two types occur from many causes, either a blow, a jump, a shout, or lifting something heavy, and so on.  Its sign, if it is from an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs little by little over a long time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is even towards the depth because the peritoneum constricts the intestine.  Its sign, if it is from a tear in the peritoneum, is that from its beginning there is great pain suddenly, and the swelling is different, appearing under the skin nearby.
That is by the exit of the intestine and its passage outside of the peritoneum.  And with the intestine, the epiploon may exit; this hernia is called intestinal and epiploic.  It may be with gas. And the faeces may pass into the intestine and be retained there, and with that the patient perishes.
Because it causes severe pain and gurgling, especially if it is constricted.  Treatment of this type of ailment with an iron instrument is dangerous, so it should be avoided. The method of treatment is to order the patient to return the intestine with his hand into his abdomen if it is possible to return it.
Then he lies on his back in your hands and raises his legs. Then you stretch the skin next to the groin upwards and incise all the skin of the scrotum lengthwise. Then you insert hooks into the edges of the incision to the extent needed to open the incision with them.
And the incision should be such that the testicle can come out of it. Then you peel away the membranes under the skin of the scrotum until the white, hard peritoneum is exposed on every side. Then insert your index finger next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum under the skin of the testicle and the second peritoneum, and you loosen the adhesion behind the testicle.  Then you draw it inwards with your right hand into the scrotum’s skin, and with this you stretch the white peritoneum upwards with your left hand and raise the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
And you order the assistant to pull the testicle upwards, and you yourself completely loosen the adhesion from behind. And you search with your finger lest there be something of the twisted intestine in the hard white peritoneum. If you find something, push it down into the abdomen.
Then take a needle with thick thread, twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotum's skin next to the incision. Then cut the ends of the doubled thread so that there are four threads. Then place some on top of others firmly, and tie the peritoneum, which we said is under the scrotum’s skin, tightly from two sides with it. Then also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish it can reach it. Lest a hot swelling result from that. And also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures leave as much of the peritoneum under the scrotum's skin as the width of a finger.
Lest a hot swelling result from that. And also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures leave as much of the peritoneum under the scrotum's skin as the width of a finger. And cut all the rest in a circle and remove the testicle with it. Then incise the lower part of the scrotum's skin, an incision from which blood and pus flow, as we described previously. Then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound and use the ligature which we described.
And one may cauterize the white peritoneum which we mentioned after cutting it, to guard against bleeding, for that often occurs.  And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself. If it is slow to fall off, then loosen it with warm water and apply something that makes it sweat until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
Concerning the windy hernia.  I have not seen anyone dare to treat this windy hernia with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as we described in the hernia with a sac, that is, that the vessels should be gently tied below after incising them.
Then incise in the middle and treat the swelling with what opens it until the vessels fall away, and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
Concerning the hernia which is in the groin.
A hernia may appear in the groin, as we said, so that the place swells but nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum; and if it descends, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if time passes, the fissure in the peritoneum increases until the intestine and fat descend into the scrotum; and this occurs from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the place swells and protrudes, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I have previously described.
It may also be treated with the iron in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, then you incise the swollen, protruding hernia horizontally to the extent of three fingers, then you separate the peritoneum which is under the skin.
Until the white peritoneum which follows is uncovered, then you take a roll and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum and press it into the depth of the abdomen; then you sew the two protruding parts of the peritoneum to the edge of the roll and join one to the other with the suture.
Then you remove the roll, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal hernia; then you treat it with the treatment of wounds.
If the threads are cut, I clean them and dress the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the hernia protrusion does not increase.
Cauterization is better in this location because it is closer to safety.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the skin of the testicle.
Often the skin of the testicle relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly.  He who wishes to treat it should place the patient on his back.
And he cuts all the relaxed skin on the thick skin, then brings the two lips together. And if you wish, first sew the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, and make sure of the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book.  But I have mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that it may be in the knowledge of the physician if he is asked about it, and he may know the treatment of one who suffers from it; and the other, that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, goats, cats, and the like of animals.  So I say that castration is of two kinds: either by crushing or by incision and cutting.  That which is by crushing, its method is that we seat the animal in hot water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are not perceptible to the touch. As for castration by incision and cutting, you should hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the suspensory ligaments and make one incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles appear, then cut them off after you have skinned them, and do not leave on them any of the membranes except the thin membrane which is on the vessels.  This kind of castration is better than that which is by crushing.
Because crushing may leave something of the testicles, so the animal may desire coition. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
On the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types. One is that which appears in the area following the scrotum or in the skin of the scrotum between the testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from that which is in the skin of the scrotum.
As for women, there is one type, and it is above the vulva on the pubis like small male genitals protruding outward, one of them like a man's penis and the two like testicles.  And the treatment of the three types.
The two types in men and the one type in women: it is necessary to cut away that excess flesh until its trace is gone, then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals. As for the second type in men, from which urine flows from that which is in the scrotum's skin, there is no action in it nor is there any benefit from it at all.
Chapter seventy-one.
On the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris may exceed the natural amount, until it becomes large and its appearance ugly, and it may enlarge in some women until it spreads like that of men and becomes suitable for intercourse.
It is necessary that you grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or with a hook and cut it, and do not exaggerate in the cutting, especially in the depth of the root, lest bleeding occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And sometimes it protrudes outwards like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the "tail" disease. It should be cut like the clitoris, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
In the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vulva is not perforated, or the perforation is small. This may be congenital or acquired. The acquired type is due to a pre-existing condition, which may be an excessive growth of flesh.
or a thin or thick membrane.  This may be located deep within the uterus, or on its sides, or above or below it. It prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and may also prevent menstruation.  This is determined by the midwife's examination.
If the obstruction is visible and close, then if it is not visible, she should examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is a thin membrane and is close to the labia, then proceed to pierce it.  This is done by placing something like pledgets on the labia, then placing both thumbs together.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread. Then, the labia are strongly stretched until the thin membrane is pierced and the obstruction opens. Then, she takes wool, soaks it in oil, and places it on the area. The woman should have intercourse every day, so that the area does not close up again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, it should be incised with a wide scalpel, resembling an aspen leaf. If the obstruction is from an excessive growth of flesh, then it should be hooked with hooks and cut. You should have styptic medications that do not sting, such as acacia, shih-shian, and frankincense, mixed with egg white.
Then, use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not close quickly. Keep it in place for several days and use a wick of dry linen, then treat it with other treatments for healing until it is healed.  Another growth of flesh may occur in the uterus; this should be cut in the same manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor; a cancerous tumor that is in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red sores that appear in the vulva of women.
As for hemorrhoids, it is a swelling of the openings of the veins, so that much blood constantly flows from them. If hemorrhoids persist, they become warts.  I have mentioned their types and signs in the classification, and we will mention them here.
What accepts treatment from them and what does not accept treatment. I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and do not appear to the touch, are not treatable with iron; those that are at the mouth of the uterus, which can be felt, are those that are treated.
Then the woman should enter a cool room, and then stretch the warts with a scraper or a rough cloth and cut them from their roots.  Then apply to them, when bleeding from one of the sharp instruments that stops the blood without causing laceration.
Such as acacia,  [a type of] cautery, and frankincense and the like. Then she should raise her legs to the wall for a while, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding persists, seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, gallnuts, and the like. Then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it as a compress to her back. If the bleeding stops, otherwise, apply cupping to the loins and breasts without restriction. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of  celandine, or the juice of Verbascum, or the juice of the branches of  Spartium, with a decoction of gallnuts, and apply it to the place.
If the swelling subsides, then treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pustule, it resembles the heads of  [a type of] grass, rough in appearance. It should be cut as much as appears, in the same way as I mentioned for warts, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the suppuration which occurs in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may occur in the uterus, such as cancer, the hardened tumor, ulcers, fistulas, phagedena,  fissures, hemorrhoids, warts, and hot tumors.  I have described all of these diseases, their types, and their signs in the classification.
It is necessary to mention in this section the hot tumor which occurs in the uterus, if it is among the tumors which gather for a period of time, how its suppuration is done with an instrument. It is necessary to observe; if the pain of the tumor in its beginning is acute with throbbing, inflammation, and fever,
and you see it red when you touch it, do not hasten to its suppuration, and treat it with what helps in ripening, until when these symptoms subside, then it is necessary that the woman sit on a chair which has two seats and leans on its back.
and brings her legs together towards the lower abdomen and her thighs are spread apart, and her arms are placed under her knees, and they are bound with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the uterus is opened, whose image comes later.  And it is necessary, if you want to use this instrument, to estimate with a probe the depth of the woman's uterus, lest what enters from the instrument be more than the depth of the uterus.
then it harms the patient. If the instrument is longer than the depth, then it is necessary to place pledgets on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus, and it is necessary that the spiral which runs in the instrument be from the upper side.
And you hold the instrument and the assistant turns the spiral until the cervix is opened.  Then if the abscess appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and thin, then it is necessary to incise it at its head with a wide scalpel. Then when all the pus is evacuated,
it is necessary that a soft pledget soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent be placed in the wound, and the pledget should be outside the incision in the cervix, and clean wool soaked in water in which alum has been boiled should be placed externally on the uterus and on the pubis.
Then treat it after two days with the ointment which is suitable for that until it heals.
And the uterus and wound may be washed by irrigation with water and honey, then with water in which licorice root or long zedoary has been boiled, then return to the ointment. If the abscess is hidden inside the uterus,
then it is necessary to refrain from treating it with iron, but it should be treated with what we mentioned in the classification.
Chapter Seventy-five.
In instructing midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural manner.
The midwife should first know the form of natural childbirth; and of its signs, if you see the woman straining downwards and desiring to inhale air, and what she suffers from childbirth becomes easier for her.
And the emergence of the fetus is hastened; so know that this birth is in the natural manner, and its emergence is head first with the afterbirth together with it or hanging from its navel.  If you see these signs, then it is necessary to press her abdomen so that the fetus emerges quickly.
For if it descends headfirst, the afterbirth descends with it and cleanses itself of those impurities well. And besides this birth, it is unnatural and blameworthy, because the fetus may emerge on its feet and its hands may emerge before its head and feet.
And one hand or one foot, or its head and hand may emerge, or it may emerge folded, and perhaps it may turn over on its back and similar blameworthy forms.  It is necessary for the midwife to be skillful and gentle with all these things and to beware of slips and mistakes.
And I am explaining each form and how to manage it so that you may be guided by that and understand it. If the fetus emerges headfirst, the natural emergence, and that is severe for the woman and the labor is difficult for her, and you see that her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and order women to hold her.
And compress her womb with cooked fenugreek and with moist ointments, then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers and cuts the afterbirth with it, or cuts it with her fingernail, until the moisture within it flows out, and she presses the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then it is necessary to inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with shirāj ointment, then after the injection, order her to cough and make her sneeze with hellebore, and hold her mouth and nose, then the fetus will emerge immediately. If the hands of the fetus emerge first, then it is necessary to return them gently little by little.
If it does not readily enter, place the woman on a stool and raise her legs upward, then shake the stool on the ground while the woman holds on so that she does not fall during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, then cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately, then he will come out.  The emergence of the fetus on its feet: If the fetus emerges on its feet, then it is necessary to push them upward, then turn the fetus little by little until you straighten it, then take one of its feet and extend it little by little.
