If they come out to the thighs, then order her to cough and make her sneeze with hellebore, then it will come out. If it does not come out as we described, otherwise return the fetus little by little until you put it in the natural position, then it will come out easily. If it is prevented by all that we described.
Then take the saliva of the khutmī and the saliva of fenugreek and shirāj ointment and dissolved gum, and pound everything well in a mortar, then apply it to the woman's vulva and lower abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the sharasif.
If you see that her lower part has become soft, then make a pessary for her from myrrh and let her endure it. If the pessary stays for an hour, then seat her on a chair, then make her sneeze, and plug her mouth and nose, and gently squeeze her lower abdomen; then the fetus will come out immediately. The emergence of the fetus on its knees and hands.
If it comes out in this manner, then gradually insert its hands, then the woman lies on her back and lets her hips hang down while lying on a bed, and she clasps her hands, then gently press above her abdomen. Then remove the pillows from under her legs so that her lower part is raised. If the fetus does not come out, then take both her legs and move them vigorously, then gently squeeze above the sharasif until the fetus ascends upwards, then the midwife inserts her hand and adjusts the fetus little by little.
And instruct the woman to strain until the fetus comes out. The fetus comes out sideways, hanging by one of its hands. Gradually return its hand; if you are unable to return it, then raise the woman and make her walk; if she is unable to walk, then she lies down.
on her bed and shake her legs vigorously, then use the poultice that I described from the saliva; if labor begins, then seat her on a chair, then work on returning the hand and adjusting the fetus to its natural form, and make the woman sneeze and instruct her to strain until it comes out.
The fetus comes out on its back, extending its hands, and its face is towards its mother's back. The midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then gradually turn it and gently adjust it.  When it is straight, apply to its front what we described of oil and poultices.
Then tell her to move little by little; if she does that, then seat her on the chair and tell her to lean to the right side, then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with the [instrument/tool] kands, then it will come out.  The exit of the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the placenta, then split the placenta with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture. If it adheres to the face and neck of the fetus, then cut it from the umbilical cord lest it suffocate and die, then push the fetus inwards until it is as it should be.
And tell the woman to strain repeatedly, then it will come out easily. The exit of twins or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and three or four may be born and live, although that is rare. As for five, it is something outside of nature, they do not live at all.
And a single placenta surrounds them, even if they are many, and they are separated by a membrane barrier between them, attached to the umbilical cord of each one. And they may come out as a single fetus comes out, naturally and easily, and they may come out badly as we described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and become as we have said, in unnatural forms, then treat them as we have described. And let the midwife be gentle, and let her do what she does with gentleness and slowness, and let her contrive for every form with whatever means is available to her leading to safety.
Mentioning what is conceived in the womb of fetuses that are aborted. One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived in the womb. It has been confirmed to me that a woman aborted seven, and another fifteen, all of them conceived.
By the estimation of the All-Knowing, All-Powerful.  All of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing will happen to her one day, so that she may meet it as is appropriate.
Chapter Seventy-Six.
On the removal of a dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus with what I mentioned in the section on treatment, and it does not come out, and you are compelled to work with your hand, then you should look. If the woman is strong and healthy and has none of the symptoms which cause fear of death for her, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be tilted downwards and her legs raised, and a servant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, lest her body be pulled when the fetus is drawn out.
Then moisten her vulva with moist ointments along with mallow juice, fenugreek, and flaxseed. Then the midwife should lubricate her hand with these ointments and juices, then gently insert it into the womb, then search with it for a place to insert the hooks into the fetus, and the appropriate places for that.
Look, if the fetus is positioned to come down head first, then insert the hooks into its eyes, or into the nape of its neck, or into its mouth, or into its palate, or under its chin, or its shoulder, or into the places near the ribs, or under the clavicles.
But if it descends feet first, then [insert the hooks] into the pubis and the middle ribs.  It is necessary to hold the hook with the right hand and to place its bend between the fingers of the left hand and to gently insert the hand and insert a hook into some of those places we mentioned.
Until you reach something firm, then insert another hook opposite it, or a third if needed, so that the traction is even and does not lean to one side. Then pull with a steady pull, and the pull should not be straight only, but moved in every direction.
So that its exit is facilitated, as is done with a tooth when it is extracted.  It is necessary during this to relax the traction. If it is obstructed from one side, then the midwife should lubricate some of her fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to move what is obstructed. If part of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other places.
She should do this until the entire fetus emerges. If a hand emerges before anything else and cannot be returned because of its compression, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it and then pull it until, when it is completely out, you cut it.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow. Thus she should do with the other hand and with the feet. If the head of the fetus is large and encounters difficulty in exiting, or if there is collected water in its head, then it is necessary to insert a lancet between the fingers.
And make an incision in the head with it so that the water exits, or pierce it with the instrument called the *mishdakh*, the image of which comes with the images of the other instruments in the chapter after this.
She should do likewise if the fetus is naturally large-headed. Then it is necessary to perforate the cranium or crush it with the *mishdakh*, as we said, then remove the bones with hooks. If the head emerges and is compressed at the clavicle, then let an incision be made until the moisture in the chest flows out.
Then the chest will contract. If the chest does not contract and exit is not possible, then cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or contains a tumor, then it is necessary to incise it so that the moisture flows out.
If the fetus is in a feet-first presentation, then pulling it is easy and straightening it towards the mouth of the womb is simple. If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, then it is necessary to pull it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and incise the abdomen or chest until what is within them flows out.
If all the members have been extracted and the head remains and is obstructed, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, let her insert her hand into the neck of the womb and search for the head with it, and pull it with her fingers towards the mouth of the womb, then insert one or two hooks into it and pull it with them.
If the mouth of the womb has contracted because of a hot swelling affecting it, then it is not necessary to use force on it, but then it is necessary to use the pouring of greasy moist substances and apply compresses, and sit in waters that relax and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, if it is possible to straighten it, then use what we mentioned for the living fetus. If that is not possible, then cut and extract it.  It is necessary that nothing of the afterbirth remain inside at all. When you have finished your treatment, use what we mentioned for the treatment of hot swellings which occur in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, then let her descend into astringent waters and use all other treatments. I have indeed witnessed a woman who had conceived, and the fetus died in her womb, then she conceived again upon it.
Then the other fetus also died, and after a long time a swelling appeared in her womb and it swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus.  I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close, so I applied to it some strong, attractive ointments.
Then a bone came out from the place, and then after some days another bone came out. I was amazed by that, since the abdomen is a place where there are no bones. I judged that they were from the bones of the dead fetus, so I examined the wound and extracted from it many bones from the bones of the head of the fetus.
I continued doing this until I extracted many bones from her, and the woman was in the best of health. And she lived for a time after that, discharging a little pus from the place.  And it is for this reason that I have brought this rare case here.
Because in it there is knowledge and aid for what the skillful physician attempts with his hand in treatment.
Chapter seventy-seven.
In it are images of the instruments which are needed in extracting the fetus.
An image of a screw to open the mouth of the womb.
This is the binder with which books are bound, having two screws at the ends of the two pieces of wood, except that this screw should be finer than the binder and should be either of ebony or of boxwood, and the width of each piece of wood should be the thickness of two fingers.
And their width about a finger, and their length a span and a half, and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two guides of the same material as the wood itself, which are fastened in them, their length being half a span or a little more, and their width about two fingers or a little more, and these two guides.
are those which enter the mouth of the womb to open it by turning the screws. An image of another instrument for the same purpose, finer and lighter:
It is made of ebony or boxwood in the shape of hooks, except that it has two guides at its end, as you see. The length of each guide is about a span, and their width is two fingers.  If you wish to open the womb with it,
then seat the woman on a bed with her legs spread apart between her thighs, then insert these two guides together into the mouth of the womb, while you hold the end of the instrument below between her thighs, then open your hand with the instrument as you do with the hooks, according to how much you want to open the womb. until the midwife does what she wants.  An image of another screw mentioned by the ancients:
Image of the instrument which pushes the fetus.
Image of the forceps with which the head of the fetus is extracted.
It resembles scissors, it has teeth at the end as you see, and it may be made rectangular like hooks, in this image as you see, it has teeth like the teeth of a saw, you cut and tear with it.
Image of a pusher also.
Image of a hook.
This hook should be somewhat thick so that it does not break when pulling the fetus with it. Image of a hook with two prongs.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All these instruments, the more numerous their types and the better prepared by the craftsman, the faster his work and the higher his standing among people. Therefore, do not disdain any instrument to have prepared, for there will certainly be need for it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
In the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold herself, then cause her to sneeze with a sternutatory, and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, well and good; otherwise, take a measure.
And pierce a hole in the lid, and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as fumitory, rue, dill, chamomile, wormwood, betony, and centaury; all of these drugs, or some of them. Immerse them in water, and place them on the fire, then place a silver tube on the hole in the lid of the pot, and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the vapor reaches the bottom of the womb, then cause her to sneeze as we said. Then the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in  oil of shergesh or mallow sap.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta. If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out little by little. If it is attached deep in the womb, insert the hand as we described, until you find the placenta, then pull it out little by little, gently.
Lest the womb fall out with strong pulling, but it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then increase the amount of pulling; then it will yield and be freed from the adhesion. If the mouth of the womb is constricted, we have described the treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, then avoid violence upon it in pulling again. But it is necessary to tie what has come out of it to the mouth of the womb to the thigh of the woman, then inject her with the four-ingredient ointment, for it will cause it to rot after days.
And it will dissolve and come out.  However, if it becomes putrid, a bad odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, and this will harm the patient. Therefore, suitable fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients have tried the fumigation of rue and dried figs.  The image of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated when menstruation and the placenta are retained, and so on:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper. The thin end is placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the incense is placed on the embers.  She holds it until that incense is gone and replaces it with more.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the non-perforated fistula.
Many boys are born with their anus imperforate; a thin membrane may have closed it.  It is necessary for the midwife to perforate that membrane with her finger, otherwise she should puncture it with a sharp scalpel and be careful not to touch the muscle.
Then she should place wool soaked in syrup and oil on it, then treat it with ointments until it heals. If she fears that it will close up, she should place a lead tube in the opening for many days and remove it whenever the child wants to defecate.
The anus may also become obstructed by the healing of a wound or a tumor; this adhesion should be incised, and then treated as previously described, with the addition of the lead tube.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower parts.
Fistulas which occur in the lower parts are a complication and thickening which occurs near the anus, externally or internally, from one side or another.  The fistula may be single or multiple.
If this complication becomes chronic, it opens and a watery, white fluid or thin pus discharges from it. Some of these fistulas may penetrate to the rectum and intestine; others may not. Those which penetrate may be recognized by the passage of feces and flatus when the patient defecates.
Worms may also emerge from them.  There may be fistulas, if they are internal, penetrating to the bladder and the urethra; and there may be some penetrating to the hip joint and to the sacrum.  A fistula penetrating to the rectum may also be distinguished from one which does not.
You should insert your index finger into the rectum and insert a thin probe into the fistula, made of copper or iron, if the fistula is not tortuous. If it is tortuous, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and nothing of feces, flatus, or worms emerges from the opening, as we have said, know that it is not penetrating, and proceed to treatment.
If the fistula penetrates to the bladder or urethra, the sign is the passage of urine from it and the failure of the area to heal with medication.  If it penetrates to the hip joint or sacrum, the sign is the probe reaching there.
If there is no tortuosity to the bone, and pain exists in that direction, and pus constantly emerges from it, and treatment is ineffective and it does not heal with ointment—all these penetrating fistulas are incurable.
And their treatment is a vain effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt them. As for those which are not penetrating and not chronic, recovery is hoped for with cautery, as I describe and as I have experienced.  This is to have the patient lie on his back between your hands.
And raise his legs upwards and tilt his thighs towards his abdomen. Then insert a copper or lead probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel with your finger that the probe has penetrated by itself, uncovered, without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is penetrating, so do not tire yourself with it, for it is incurable, as we said.
And they have said that it heals in some people rarely. And of the treatments which hope for benefit is that you heat a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as preceded, and insert it, hot, into the fistula until it reaches towards the anus, then repeat it two or three times.
Until you know that all those superfluous, matted tissues resembling the tube of a bird's feather have been burned. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter until the burned flesh comes out. Then treat it with cicatrizing ointments, and if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment. But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger which is in the anus, and there was between it and the probe a thick partition of flesh or membrane, and you see the fistula near the surface of the skin, then incise the skin from the beginning of the fistula. And you pass with the incision along with the probe while it is in the fistula until you reach with the incision where the tip of the probe ended, and the probe is released and falls out. Then cleanse those matted tissues that resemble a bird's feather tube, and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with cicatrizing ointments until it heals. If blood overwhelms you and prevents you from cutting those tissues, the best and quickest remedy is cauterization with fire or a caustic medicine, for cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning those superfluous tissues.
And cutting the blood and drying the moisture. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter or sulfur powdered with oil until the area suppurates and those burned tissues come out in the pus. Then treat the wound with cicatrizing ointments that grow firm flesh.
These are medicines which constrict and dry, for if it is closed, then it is healed and you do not fear recurrence. If the fistula has reached the depth of the anus and is distant from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a membrane of peritoneum or flesh, and it was near to penetration, then the action in it is only by way of hope, that is, to use one of three methods: either to cauterize it as we said.
Or to incise it until you reach its bottom so that you are able to insert the lint and treat from near, and do not exceed in the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus and cause the patient involuntary defecation. Then treat it as we mentioned, for perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or to penetrate that membrane and perforate it after you incise it near the anus as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then cleanse all those matted tissues which are in the incision to wherever you are able to completely.
Then suture the whole wound together with the incision and the upper opening until it is closed and the fistula remains open inside the anus, which will be easier for the patient.  And the fistula may be perforated in this manner.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is on one side of the anus towards the surface of the body with the skin and the sphincter of the anus, then take then a probe with a perforated tip like a shoemaker's needle in this manner:.
And insert into it a twisted thread of five threads or so, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom. If it is penetrating in the margin of the anus from the inside near.
Then bring out the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and bringing out the end of the thread and bring both ends together and tighten them and leave it for a day or two. Every time the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues between the ends of the threads are severed and fall out. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, penetrate it however you can, except that it should not be too deep, for you should not do that because of the muscle lest you cut it. Then do with it as we mentioned until it heals.
The image of the hooked scalpel with which fistulas are incised, its curve is very sharp and the other side is not sharp lest you cut what is not needed, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
Concerning the opening of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red swellings or like grapes, and there are small and large ones, and blood constantly flows from them.
And they are single or numerous, and they are outside the rectum and at its edges, except that those which are outside the rectum are in most cases less moist, with a constant flow of yellow water or a little blood, and they are the color of the body.
The treatment for those inside the rectum is that you order the patient to strain and bear down until the rectum protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you.  Then you quickly hook them with hooks or grasp them with your fingernail and cut them at their base. If the hooks do not hold them because of their moisture and laxity,
then rub them with a rough cloth or a piece of goatskin, or pull them with your fingers, then cut them. Then, after cutting, sprinkle on them some sharp powders to act in place of cautery, or cauterize them as previously explained in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the rectum does not come out, then inject the patient with an enema containing a little irritant to wash what is in the rectum and facilitate its quick protrusion when the patient strains. As for the hemorrhoids outside the rectum, their treatment is easy and simple.
That is, you take it with your fingernail or hook it with a hook and cut it, then treat it as we mentioned until it heals. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron should use a ligature in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the hemorrhoid upwards and pass the needle through its base from the other side, and wrap the ends of the thread below the needle while it is crosswise, and pull the hemorrhoid tightly. Then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all the hemorrhoids, leaving one unligated.
so that the excess blood flows out. Then place on the rectum a cloth soaked in rose oil or cyperus oil, and place it on the rectum, and tell the patient to be still, then leave him until they fall off. When the hemorrhoids fall off, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often occur from dryness of the stool and retention of the temperament. If it becomes chronic and medicine does not work, then you should scrape it with the blade of a scalpel or with your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper crust which prevents its healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more severely than before until it bleeds well and sloughs, then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts, and the ant.
A nail is a round nodule, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail; it occurs on the whole body, but especially on the soles of the feet and the fingers, and causes pain when walking.
It should be incised around the nail and removed from its root with a hook or a snare, then treated; and if you wish, cauterize it as described in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, clustered outgrowth, round on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children.  Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it off, or to pierce it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off; and if you wish, cauterize it as previously described, with fire or a caustic medicine.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument I have described in the treatment of the ant. As for the ant [wart], it is also a small, clustered, thick outgrowth on the surface of the body, going very deep; and if it is exposed to cold, one feels in it something like the sting of ants.
It occurs on the whole body, and most often on the hands.  Its treatment is to take a tube of strong goose or vulture feather, and place this tube over the ant [wart] so that the circumference of the tube surrounds it on all sides, then rotate the tube with your hand until you have incised around the ant [wart].
Then push it into the depth of the flesh, and the ant [wart] will be removed from its root. And if you wish, do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be solid and twisted to make it easier for the fingers to grip and twist it. And if you wish, cauterize the ant [wart] as previously described in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Concerning the shapes of instruments used for injections in cases of rectal problems, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, china, or cast or beaten copper.  These instruments may be made small or large according to the users. Those used in treating small children will be small, and those for patients with narrow or painful rectums will be very gentle. Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, where the bladder is tied, is wide in this manner; it has a partition where the bladder is tied, as you see.  Its lower end, which is inserted into the rectum, is solid, very smooth, and delicate; on one side are two holes, and on the other side one hole.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a probe, or slightly thicker. The bladder, in which the medication is placed, is made from an animal's bladder or sheepskin, shaped like a small bag, its diameter being a span.
Many holes are pierced in a circular pattern, the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger. Then a strong thread of twisted yarn, of ten threads or so, is inserted into these holes and the bladder is gathered like a purse. When the medication is placed inside, the top of the syringe is tightly tied between two stones, then it is injected.  This is an image of the circular bladder and the holes:
The method of injection is to fill the skin or bladder with warmed medication, to a degree tolerable to the patient. Beware of it being too hot or too cold. Then the patient lies on his back with his legs raised.
A cloth or sheet is placed beneath him. Then the tip of the syringe is lubricated with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or the like. Then it is gently inserted into the rectum.  An assistant then takes the bladder in both hands and squeezes it strongly until everything in the syringe is emptied into the intestines.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient raises his legs to the wall. If the medication stimulates evacuation, let him hold it as much as possible. If he sleeps all night with it, it will be more beneficial. If he is injected for diarrhea and evacuation is hurried, repeat the injection until he is cured.
If there are painful hemorrhoids or tumors in the rectum, the syringe should be very gentle and smooth, smoother than those used to inject children. This is an image of a gentle syringe with skin stretched over it:
Chapter Eighty-Four
On the Treatment of Wounds
I have mentioned in the classification the treatment of what resembles cauterization, and I will here discuss its treatment by hand and by medication completely. I say that wounds differ according to the thing that causes the wound and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things that cause wounds are numerous, such as the blow of a stone, or the cut of a sword or knife, or a stab wound from a spear or stick or arrow, and so on from many things.
As for wounds according to the locations of the body, there is the wound that occurs on the head, or the neck, or the chest, or the abdomen, or the liver, and similar organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple wounds of the head, especially because the compound ones will be mentioned in the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
So I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone, I look; and if it is from a blow of a stone or the like, and the skin has only been lacerated, and the wound is large, and I fear for the patient the occurrence of hot inflammation, then act quickly and incise it (the wound) appropriately. And do not delay that, and let blood be drawn from him according to his strength, if nothing prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood coming from the wound is little, and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the injured area.
And warn the patient against fullness and strong drinks and foods; and if anything prevents his bloodletting, then let him lessen the food and not approach meats or drinks. And apply to the wound, if hot inflammation occurs in it, a cotton wool soaked in rose oil alone or with a drink that has astringency.
And if you are safe from hot inflammation, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh and the air has not altered it, the  powder which I describe to you shortly; but if the air has altered it, then apply to it some ointments.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. And if the wound is large and is from a cut of a sword or the like and its edges do not come together by themselves, then unite it by stitching, as I describe in the stitching of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs down, and its attachment is only by a small piece, then cut it off, then treat the wound with medicines which produce firm flesh instead of the skin. And order the patient to nourish himself with foods that have firmness, such as heads and entrails and the like.
If there occurs putrefaction in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all the putrefied part and throw it away, then treat it.  If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding blood, and the bleeding does not stop with medicines, then search for the artery.
If you find it and it is not severed, then sever it with the scalpel and tie it. And if necessity demands, if what we have mentioned does not work, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, then their treatment is easy and simple; it suffices to treat them by sprinkling this powder on them while they are still bleeding.
Before the air changes them. The preparation of the powder is that one part of frankincense, two parts of gum arabic, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime are taken; all are crushed, sifted, and the wound is filled with it and bound tightly until it adheres well.
It will coagulate with the blood, then bandage it from above with compresses and leave it; do not untie it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine will not separate from it until it heals.  And the lime alone may do this if you do not have frankincense and gum arabic.
This action may also be done with cobwebs in many large wounds if its application and binding are expertly done, and the wounds are fresh with blood, before the air changes them. But if the air has changed them somewhat, especially in summer.
Then there is no point in applying this powder; treat it with what we have mentioned, by applying some ripening ointments or a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until pus is produced, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
But if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is slight, then pull it with a forceps. I have already mentioned the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in the surgery of the neck. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a head wound if it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with things that cause strong constriction, such as verdigris and vitriol and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause spasms.
And nothing cold at all should be placed on the wound, because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection is to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  What you treat it with should be soft medicines, such as natron washed many times with fresh water, moistened with oil or rose ointment and tutty.
If it is also washed with fresh water and other minerals in this manner, and moist ointments, but if the wound is large, use suturing or bind its edges with pledgets, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a depth and a cavity forms at its bottom where pus collects, lance it in its lowest part. If an artery is severed in the wound and you see its blood flowing, ligate it or cauterize it, or if necessary, amputate it.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx and the vessels are unharmed, join the edges of the skin by suturing over the windpipe, but do not touch the windpipe; rather, smooth and restore it to its natural shape, then bind it tightly and leave it for some days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, explore it; if there are bone splinters, extract them all, or as many as possible; what you cannot reach, leave until the wound suppurates and heals, making their removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we have described. If a bone becomes necrotic in the wound and it becomes fistulous, treat it as described in its chapter, in the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife and you see a depth to it, observe: if air escapes from it when the patient breathes, know that it is a mortal wound. If it has no depth and is fresh with its blood, do not initially apply powder to it nor bind it, lest the blood be retained in its depth.
It will then spread to the heart and kill the patient.  However, apply an absorbent ointment and dress it twice a day. If you do not have ointment, place a used piece of cotton in the mouth of the wound to absorb the moisture that comes from it, and have the patient sleep on the wound.
So that what gathers there may flow out. And if three days or more have passed since the infliction of the wound, and the patient has not experienced convulsions, or a bad palpitation, or shortness of breath, and you see all his other conditions to be good, then know that the wound is safe, and treat it with cautery and other treatments until it heals.
If it is difficult to see it and the pus flows constantly, know that it has become fistulous, and treat it according to its chapter.  The patient should lie on the wound so that what gathers there may flow out.  If the wound is in the chest or back, treat it with the stitching mentioned before.
If it is large, cauterize it; if small, use powder. If it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, explore the wound and hasten to remove those fragments if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them until...
the wound becomes suppurated, for then it will be easy to remove them. As for other wounds occurring in other members, their treatment is governed by what we have mentioned.
Chapter eighty-five.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The wound which occurs in the abdomen may be large, or it may be small or medium.  A large wound may allow the protrusion of one or several intestines; their replacement and suturing will then be more difficult and arduous.
A small wound may also be difficult in another way, so that replacing the intestine becomes more difficult. If the intestine is not immediately returned to its place, it swells and thickens, making its replacement more difficult. For this reason, a medium-sized wound is preferable, since returning the intestine is not as difficult with it as with the other two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is of four kinds. Two sutures are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing all other wounds of the body. The other two sutures are special, applicable to all wounds, but they are especially suitable for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and some of the intestine protrudes and its replacement is difficult, this is due to one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestine has been inflated by the cold air.
If this is the case, then it should be warmed by dipping a sponge or a wet cloth in lukewarm water alone, or in water in which anise, rue, and wheat have been boiled, and applying it to the intestine until the inflation subsides.
A styptic drink may also do this, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.  If you see that the inflation has subsided, then wet the intestine with water in which  plantain or mallow has been boiled, for this makes its entry easier.
If its return is still difficult after this treatment, then incise the wound slightly with the instrument with which you incise fistulas. The first treatment, if successful, is better than incision.  One resorts to incision only out of necessity and after other methods have failed. This is the image of the instrument:
One side of it is curved and pointed, the other side is not pointed. The thin end is not as thin as a lancet, but is a little blunter. It is an instrument resembling a small forceps, as you see. If the wound has widened and the intestine has entered, then it should be replaced in its natural form.
And its proper place, if you can manage it, is best. But if the wound is wide and is in the lower abdomen, then the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head; if it is in the upper abdomen, then his head and chest should be raised higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always make the side with the surgery higher than the other side.  This should also be done in major and moderate surgeries.
As for minor wounds, position it as best you can. Then have a gentle assistant at hand to hold the entire wound with both hands, bringing its edges together, then revealing it to the person performing the stitching, little by little.
This is the description of a single general stitch: Take one needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then leave a space from the edge of the wound equal to the thickness of the little finger, and insert a needle without thread into the edges of the skin. together with the edges of the peritoneum which is under the skin from the inside, until you pass it through from that side.  You have now brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers. Then, you tighten a doubled thread around the needle several times from both sides until the edges of the wound are tightly joined.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger, and insert another needle, then fasten it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as needed until you complete the entire circumference of the wound. Let the needles be of moderate thickness.
For very fine needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick needles are also difficult to insert into the skin. Therefore, they should be of moderate thickness.  And the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too close to the wound.
Lest the flesh quickly break and the wound open before it heals, and do not also space the stitches too far apart, lest the wound fails to close.  Then cut off the ends of the needles, lest they injure the patient while he sleeps. And make him pads of linen cloth on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of stitching with needles is best suited for small wounds, because it may suffice to stitch them with one or two needles, or so.
As for the method of suturing for a general wound, it is that you gather with the suture the four edges—that is, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum—at once with a needle having a twisted thread, moderate in thinness and thickness. Then when you have passed the needle through these four edges, return the needle from the side where you began.
So that the thread falls interwoven from the top of the wound, so that the stitching is according to the stitching of a pouch with which goods are tied. And make between each stitch a space of the width of the little finger. And this stitching is known to all people.
With this stitching, a wound was stitched for a man in his abdomen. He had been wounded with a knife, and the wound was more than a span long, and about two spans of the small intestine had protruded, and the wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours.  The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he was cured. He then lived many years, managing all his affairs, while the physicians had judged that he would never recover.
And it is remarkable that I did not treat him with an ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines.  I used to put on the wound worn cotton twice a day and attend to washing it with honey-water until it healed.
As for one of the two kinds of special stitching, I mention it according to the words of Galen. It is that you begin the stitching from the skin, and insert the needle from outside to inside.  When the needle has pierced the skin and the muscle going straight along the whole length of the abdomen...
...I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side into which I inserted the needle, and I passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, into the other edge of the peritoneum. When I had passed it through, I passed it again in this same edge of the peritoneum from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through, pass it also through the edge of the peritoneum on its side, until you pass it completely through.
Then begin again from this same side, and stitch it together with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out from the skin near it. Then return it into that skin and stitch the edge of the peritoneum on the other side together with this edge of the peritoneum.
And bring it out from the skin on its side, and do that again and again, until you stitch the whole wound in that way. In short, the description of this stitch is that you stitch like the stitching of furriers for fur.
That you stitch the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leave it once from the other side until you finish. As for the second type of special stitching that Galen also mentioned, this is his wording exactly.
Which is that you stitch it in the way that some healers stitch it, by bringing each part to its counterpart that is naturally similar to it, so that they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge. This is as I describe to you.
It is necessary that you insert the needle into the edge of the peritoneum near you from the outside and pass it to the inside in it alone, and leave the edges of the peritoneum. Then return the needle and pass it from the outside to the inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then return it again.
And pass it from the inside to the outside in the edge of the other peritoneum which is on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than the easy common stitching, which is the stitching previously mentioned. This is Galen's exact words.  And some have said that the stitching of the abdomen may be straightened with two needles. That is, that you insert one thread into them, and begin by inserting the needle from your side and pass it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side and pass it from your side, according to the stitching of the basket-makers, exactly.
And know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then its stitching is more difficult than in other places of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its rule in its treatment is the rule of all other wounds. That is, if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it, and you return the intestine and stitch it and secure it, then apply to it the medicated lint.
If the wound has been changed by the air, then apply to it some of the ointments which you prepare until it suppurates and the threads fall out and the peritoneum and the omentum unite, then treat it as you treat all other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, then apply to it, from when it begins to suppurate, old cotton and change it twice a day as I have taught you until it heals. For you do not need another treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple. If you fear that the wound might involve the principal organs in the pain,
then it is necessary that you dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or in rose oil and place it around the areas between the groins and the armpits. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines – and that often occurs – then inject into him a lukewarm astringent black drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a wound penetrating to his interior. And know that what is thick of the intestines is easier to heal, and what is thin of them is more difficult to heal.
And as for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not accept healing from a wound inflicted upon it at all. This is because of the multitude of its veins and their size, and the thinness of its substance and its proximity to the nature of a nerve.  But if what protruded from the wound was the omentum and you found it moist, then return it in the same way as you would return the intestine.
If a period of time has passed and it has turned green or black, then it is necessary that you tie it with a thread above the place which has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for in the omentum are veins and arteries; then cut below that ligature.
And leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to pull it and remove it when the omentum sloughs and the wound suppurates.  [He mentioned] the wound which occurs in the intestine; but if a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small…
it may be possible for it to heal in some people, for I saw a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a lance thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach.  The wound became chronic and became a fistula from which feces and flatus emerged. So I treated it, although I did not hope for its healing. So I continued to treat him gently until it healed and the place united. When I saw the place had united, I feared for the patient that some bad incident might occur in his abdomen, but no bad incident at all occurred to him, and he remained in the best of conditions. He eats and drinks properly, copulates, enters the bath, and works at his service. Some experienced people have mentioned that whenever a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sutured in this manner.
And it is that large-headed ants are taken, then the lips of the wound are brought together, then one of them is placed, with its mouth open, on the lips of the wound. When it bites and tightens its mouth, its head is cut off, for it adheres and does not come undone.  Then another ant is placed near the first.
And you continue to do that with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you cover it and suture the wound, for those heads remain adhering in the intestine until the intestine heals and recovers, and no harm at all happens to the patient.  Also, it may be possible to suture the intestine with fine thread.
That which is drawn out from the intestine of the animal adhering to it after it is entered into a needle; it is that the end of this thread is taken from the intestine, then it is drawn gently, and then a fine twisted linen thread is tied to its end, then that thread is entered into the needle. And in it is the intestinal thread, and with it the intestine is sutured, then it is returned to the abdomen.  This type of suturing with ants and intestine is only by way of hope and expectation.
But if the rupture is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, then there is no remedy in it and no healing at all.
Chapter eighty-six.
In the treatment of the cold and fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and prolonged and becomes an ulcer and does not heal, and continuously discharges pus that does not stop, is generally called, in whatever organ it may be, a fistula; and we call it a cold, and a fistula.
It is in reality a compacted, solidified, white mass with no pain, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather; and it is sometimes moist, continuously discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture may cease; and this moisture may be abundant or scant, thick or thin.
And I have stated in the classification, when mentioning wounds, that every ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow, is so because of one of nine reasons: one of them is either due to a deficiency of blood in the body, or to its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is abundant in harm and filth; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter it discharges is poor in quality; or because the medication is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a corruption that has occurred in the region, of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a particular characteristic of the region.
Or because it is abundant in harm and filth; or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter it discharges is poor in quality; or because the medication is unsuitable for its treatment; or because of a corruption that has occurred in the region, of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a particular characteristic of the region. As was evident in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is naturally slow; or because it contains a single bone or several bones. I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medications in the classification.
It is necessary here that I inform you of the treatment of fistulas and abscesses which is done by hand. Know that fistulas may occur in all parts of the body; some of them extend to large veins and arteries, or to a nerve.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the compound joints, such as the hand and foot; and some lead to the vicinity of a principal organ, and so on.
Now, those fistulas which are of this nature are among the diseases difficult to cure, which do not accept treatment, and to work on them is troublesome and ignorant.  If, however, there are some fistulas which have not reached these places which I have mentioned, and hope and expectation remain for their treatment,
and you wish to arrive at knowledge of that, take a probe of copper or iron; if the fistula runs straight, examine it with this. If there is a winding in the fistula, examine it with a probe of thin lead, because lead is soft in body.
It will slip in easily and bend towards the winding. If the fistula has many openings, and you cannot discover them with the probe, inject one of its openings; for the moisture which you inject will pass towards the other openings and flow out.
Then thoroughly investigate in whatever way you can to know if there is bone or nerve, or if the bottom of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a single fistula with many openings.  Establish all this to the extent of your ability by questioning the patient and by observing his pain when you touch the place.
And similarly with other signs and causes leading to the swelling. If you have established all this truly, then proceed to treatment with confidence. That is to say, consider if it is...
the fistula is outwardly near, or in a safe place far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the places I mentioned to you, then incise the fistula as I described previously and remove what is within it of clots, putrid flesh, and excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its opening of flesh or wart and the like, treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep-seated and straight, then you should incise it deeply as much as you can, and then cleanse it of all its putrid flesh.
Then use a twisted thread coated in strong medication and insert it to the bottom of the fistula which you can reach with an instrument. Do this repeatedly until that strong medicine consumes all that remains of corruption in the bottom of the fistula.
Then bind it with ointments that produce healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal by this, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, then investigate it and incise it as I have told you, if nothing prevents you from doing so.
a nerve, tendon or principal member, as we said. If the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with what will heal it until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues as it was doing,
know that you have not reached the complete removal of the corruption.  Expose everything on it again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with the greatest effort, then bind it. If it heals, otherwise know that that corruption is in a deep recess. Expose everything on it again.
and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with the greatest effort. If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, then pull it out with fine forceps suitable for that.
If there are many bones, thoroughly extract them all and do not leave any of them, to the best of your ability. If something of the ligaments obstructs you and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and the flesh, if it is there, and free the bones.
If you are not able to extract the bones at once, then apply to the wound something that will cause it to fester, and leave it for days until it festers what is around those bones of flesh and ligaments.  Keep the wound from healing the incision that you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place on it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For when those ligaments have festered and the bones have become loosened, it will be easy to extract and pull them out.
If it is one large bone, such as a bone of the leg or a bone of the thigh, and so on, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption is gone, then dress the wound. If a large part of it is corrupted and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to excise it.
And cut it all away to where the corruption ends. Then treat it until it heals. And I will tell you of a case of gangrene that occurred in a man's leg, to make it an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause moving within his body until the materials reached the leg and swelled into a large swelling. There was no external cause. Then time passed with the mistakes of the physicians until the swelling opened.
And much material flowed from it, and he was poorly treated until the leg became gangrenous and many openings appeared in it, all of them emitting pus and the body's humors. A number of physicians treated him for about two years, and among them there was no one skilled in the craft of surgery.
Until they sought me out. I saw his leg and the material flowing from those openings in great quantity, and the man's body was emaciated and his color yellow.  So I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
Then I found that some of them led to others from all sides of the leg. So I began by incising one of those openings until I exposed some of the bone. I found it corrupted, eaten away, black, putrid, and perforated until the perforation reached the marrow. Then I excised what was revealed to me.
And I removed the corrupted bone. I believe that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I cut and excised, and that I had completely removed it. Then I proceeded to dress the wound with cauterizing medications for a longer period, but it did not heal. Then I returned and uncovered the bone again, beyond the first uncovering.
Then I found the corruption connected to the bone.  So I excised what was also revealed to me of that corruption. Then I attempted to dress it, but it did not heal nor unite. Then I uncovered it again, and I continued to cut the bone piece by piece and attempt to dress it, but it would not heal until I had cut about a span of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow. Then I dressed it with medications, and it healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition in the operation and incision was necessary because of the patient's weakness, his low tolerance, and my fear for his death, because he frequently experienced severe fainting from excessive vomiting.  He recovered completely.
And in the place of the bone, firm flesh grew, and his condition in his body improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs, and in walking he was not afflicted by any harm whatsoever. If a prominent bone in any part of the body is fractured,
it should be excised in this manner: you take a bandage and tie it around the end of the prominent bone, and instruct someone to pull it upwards. Then make another bandage of thicker wool than the first bandage.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, while you expose the flesh from the area you wish to excise, so that the saw does not injure the flesh.  Place a firm piece of wood or board beneath the bone from below.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the decayed bone. The sawing should be done slightly above the area of decay, so that there is no decay in the cavity of the bone which is not apparent to the touch, necessitating another sawing.
If the bone is decayed and not prominent, but its parts are connected and the decay is in its middle or in part of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides. Then place the wood underneath, and saw it from the first side where the decay is.
When you have finished sawing from one side, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the decay, as we said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the decay is in the articulation of two joints, there is no remedy other than excision. If the decay is in the metacarpus of the hand or foot, its treatment is very difficult; however, you should peel away the decay however it appears, excise it, and clean it in whatever way you can.
And by whatever method it straightens itself for you, provided that a vein or nerve does not impede you. And know that the instruments and saws for cutting these bones are numerous, according to the position, setting, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and porosity of the bones. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare for each type of work an instrument similar to that work.
And know that the operations themselves may indicate to you the type of instrument you need, if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this craft, because mastery of the craft shows various kinds of diseases. He may therefore devise for himself instruments similar to those for each disease, and I have illustrated for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.

Chapter Eighty-Seven.
In the amputation of limbs and the sawing of bones.
Limbs may become putrid, either from an external cause or from an internal cause. If you treat this corruption with medicinal treatment and the treatment is not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing preventing it,
then it is necessary to amputate that limb to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved thereby from death or from a great calamity which is greater than the loss of the limb.  A sign of this occurring is that the limb turns black until you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may become putrid after turning black, until that putrefaction spreads to what is next to that limb and affects the whole body; therefore, hasten to amputate it.  Likewise, if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of some venomous creatures, like a sea scorpion, a snake, a spider, or the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, amputate the finger and do not delay so that the corruption spreads to the hand.  Likewise, if it has affected the hand, amputate it from the base of the forearm, and do not delay so that the corruption spreads until it affects the bones of the forearm.
Likewise, if it has affected the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow in the joint itself. If the corruption spreads and you see it has reached toward the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient. Use other treatments as much as possible.
And likewise you do with the leg: if the corruption affects the toe, amputate the toe at one of the joints; and if it affects the metatarsus, amputate the whole foot. If it ascends to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to abandon it and leave the patient to death. The method of amputating or cutting off a limb is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut off and tie another ligature above the place.
And one assistant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another assistant pulls the upper ligature upwards, and you yourself dissect the flesh between the ligatures with a wide scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then it is cut or incised. It is necessary to place linen cloths on all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy part, which would cause the patient additional pain and hot inflammation. If bleeding occurs during your work, staunch the place quickly, or apply some styptic powder, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. And I will tell you about an example of a man who had this very condition in his leg, namely that blackness with burning heat like fire occurred in his leg.
And that corruption first occurred in his toe until it took the whole leg.  So the man, on his own initiative, when he saw the corruption spreading in the limb, along with the intense pain and burning, cut it off at the joint and recovered.
Then, after a long time, that same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand.  He came to me, and I attempted to check its progress with the medications I applied to the hand after cleaning his body. But the corruption did not abate and continued to spread to the other finger.
Until the corruption took hold of the hand. He asked me to amputate his hand, but I refused, hoping to stop the corruption's progress, and also fearing death for him if I amputated his hand, for the man's strength was failing.  When he despaired of me, he went back to his town.
Then word reached me that he had, on his own initiative, amputated his entire hand and recovered.  I have recounted this story to be helpful in cases of this type of disease and to serve as a guide to be followed and acted upon.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On the treatment of fistulas and how to inject them with medication.
If a swelling occurs in one of the fleshy parts and the duration of the swelling is prolonged, until it gathers matter, then bursts or is incised, and all the matter within it comes out, leaving the place empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a thin rag, not severely damaged to the point of affecting bone, nerve, or ligament.  Therefore, it deserves to be called a fistula, and not a sinus, unless its duration is prolonged to the point that the damage affects any of these parts.
Then it is called a sinus or a fistula, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin completely, especially if it has become thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place due to its damage.
If you hope that the skin will adhere and the damage has not reached that extent, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. This is done by looking at the fistula; if it is large and the pus that flows from it is foul-smelling, inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris in equal parts, mixed in a vessel and cooked over a fire until the medicine thickens to the consistency of honey. Then take what you need and dilute it with water and honey, and inject it into the fistula.  Tie its opening and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then remove it by pressing. Do this for several days until the fistula is cleansed and the foul smell is gone.  You may inject it with ash water if this medicine is not available. This is made by taking the ash of grapevine wood or oak wood and pouring water over it.
Then filter it and inject it until you are certain that the fistula has been washed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then inject it with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and syrup mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, and syrup closes the fistula, especially if the syrup is astringent and drying. When you have done this several times and are sure that the fistula is healed, inject it with something that will cause flesh to grow in it.
Such as taking palm ointment and diluting it with rose oil and an astringent syrup, or inject it with some of the other medicines and ointments whose properties we have described in the chapter on ointments. If the opening of the fistula is narrow and does not allow the injection tube to enter,
widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place in it a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or theriac ointment until it widens.  Similarly, if the opening is too wide, bring its edges together with sutures, leaving only enough space for the injection tube to enter.
Similarly, if the opening from which the pus flows is high up, incise it in the lowest part so that the pus flows downwards, for if the pus collects in the depth of the fistula, it prevents the growth of flesh.
If you cannot incise the fistula downwards as desired, then position the member so that the pus flows out easily, in whatever way is convenient, so that no pus at all is retained.
As for the cataplasms which are applied externally to the fistula, and the manner of their application: take one of the suitable cataplasms, spread it on a linen cloth, the cloth being large enough to cover the entire fistula. Then, with scissors, cut a hole opposite the opening of the fistula, slightly larger than the opening itself.  Then spread some soft ointment on another cloth, to fit the hole, and place it there, some distance from the opening of the fistula, so that the pus may easily escape; do not remove the larger cloth until afterwards.
several days; but that on the opening of the fistula you remove at all times, so that whatever pus collects there may come out, and you may know whether what flows from it is much or little, mature or immature.
Moreover, examine the fistula itself; does the patient feel pain in it, or not? Is there swelling, or not? If your treatment continues thus, and you see the matter which comes out is slight at the opening of the fistula…
know that the fistula has adhered, or is near to adhering. Then, resolve it on the morrow, or on the third day. And when you open the wound, change the cloths and renew the ointment. If, after a long time, thin pus issues from the fistula, do not despair of its healing.
Its healing is near, for some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others the opposite. If, after many days, immature pus comes from the fistula, know that the fistula has not healed. If the healing of the fistula is slow…
and its condition prolonged, increase the drying medicines, and let them be medicines which are moist in their consistency, and dry in their power, such as palm ointment if there has been added to it an excess of litharge. Among the most efficacious things with which it is treated is palm ointment…
and it is resolved with rose oil, and sprinkle on it old wine of moderate consistency, then knead it with this and use it. He may do this if palm ointment is not available: honey, if you cook it until it thickens, and use it; or you may take myrrh, aloes, and costus, crush them all together, and sprinkle them over honey while it is over the fire. Then spread it on a cloth and bind it on the fistula; or it may be spread with honey which you have cooked until it thickens, and sprinkle on it the drugs, and place on it the cloth and bind it.
Long rhubarb, the root of asphodel, the dust of  costmary, and centaury may be used in such a case, singly or together. Crush and sift them, and sprinkle them on the honey described above, and use it. If the fistula's condition is prolonged and it does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And if it has affected the bones, or other hard nerves, or ligaments, treat it as a fistula, as described above.
Chapter eighty-nine.
On the treatment of the felon, the bruised nail, the excision of the superfluous finger, and the separation of adherent fingers.
A felon is an excessive growth of flesh under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and it may grow in other fingers. If its condition is prolonged and its treatment neglected, it swells with a hot swelling.
Or it becomes putrid and foul until it eats the root of the nail, and perhaps destroys it entirely; and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone until it has a foul smell, and the end of the finger becomes broad, and its color becomes livid.  If you treat it with what we mentioned in the division, and your treatment is not effective,
then you should cut away with an iron instrument all the superfluous growth which grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterwards; for cauterization is very beneficial in this.  But if the bone is sound and the nail is also sound,
and the angle containing the nail has drawn the flesh inwards and caused it to be pinched and injured, then you should place a thin probe under the angle of the nail which pinches the flesh, and raise it upwards, and cut that flesh gently.
And you should apply to what remains of the flesh of the cauterizing escharotics until it is all gone, then treat it with ointments until it heals. But if the corruption has affected the bone, then you should cut away that bone and remove it, for it will not heal at all as long as there is corrupt bone in it.
If you see the erosion and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the phalanges as mentioned before, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or injury and severe pain occurs in it, then you should bleed the patient first.
Then you incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, an oblique incision from above downwards, and be careful not to reach with the incision to the flesh under the nail, for by doing so you cause the patient severe pain and it will be a cause of the growth of superfluous flesh in the place, then treat the place with that which alleviates pain.
As for the superfluous finger which grows in some people's hands, it may be wholly fleshy, and it may have bones in it, and it may have a nail in it, and the growth of some of them is at the root of the joint of some of the fingers.
Or its growth may be in some of the phalanges of the finger. Those that grow in some of the phalanges of the finger do not move, and those that grow at the joint of the finger may move.  As for that which is fleshy only, its cutting is easy, and that is to cut it at its origin with a broad scalpel.
As for that whose growth is at the root of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting it. As for that which grows in the finger at one of the phalanges, it should first have its flesh cut away in a circular cut down to the bone, then spread the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion which occurs to the fingers, some to others, this often happens, and it is from that with which a man is born, and it is at the healing of a wound or a burn of fire and the like.  Then it should be incised, that adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural form, then place between them a pledget or a piece of lint soaked in rose oil, lest it adhere quickly, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until the place heals as it should.
And likewise, if adhesion occurs to some of the fingers with the palm, incise that adhesion according to what is appropriate and improves the shape of the member.
Chapter Ninety.
On the excision of haemorrhoids.
Haemorrhoids are twisted, thick veins, filled with black, superfluous matter, occurring in most parts of the body, and most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of those who carry burdens and those who work in the fields and vineyards.  First, the body should be purged with black hellebore, repeatedly and strongly.
Then bleed the patient in the basilica vein. As for treating them with iron, there are two ways: one is to incise and remove the black blood; the other is to extract the vein entirely.  As for incision, the leg should first be bathed in warm water.
until the thick, turbid blood becomes fluid. Then the patient's leg is bound from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage, and then the vein is incised in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then you remove the black blood with your hand, from below the leg upwards and from above downwards, until you remove the amount of blood that you consider sufficient, or what the patient's strength can bear.  Then you bind it and instruct him to avoid foods which produce black bile; and purging and bloodletting are repeated.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient. As for extraction, it is done in this way: the patient's leg is shaved if it has much hair; then he is put in a bath and his leg is bathed in warm water until it reddens and sweats, or he performs strenuous exercise.
If a bath is not available, until the limb is warmed; then you incise the skin opposite the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee, or at its lower end near the heel. Then you open the skin with hooks and strip the vein from every side until it is palpable.
And when it appears, you see it is dark red; and when it is freed from the skin, you see it also like a tendon. Then you insert a probe beneath it until, when it is raised and emerges from the skin, you hang it with a smooth, blunt hook; then make another incision near that incision, about three fingers wide.
Then strip the skin from over the vein until it appears; then raise it with the probe as you did before and hang it with another hook as you did before. Then make another incision, or many incisions if you need to.
Then extract it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel; then pull it and extract it until it emerges from the second incision; then pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it from the third incision, above all the incisions, until when it has all emerged, you cut it.
If it does not yield to pulling and extraction, then insert a needle with strong doubled thread, and tie it and pull it; and insert the probe beneath it and twist it with your hand to every side until it emerges, and be careful that it does not break; for if it breaks, then its extraction will be very difficult for you.
And harm comes to the patient from it.  When you have extracted it all, place wool soaked in a syrup and rose oil or olive oil on the site of the incisions, and treat it until it heals.  If the varix is constricted with twists and turns in various directions,
and is not straight as we said, then it is necessary that you make an incision on it at each of its twists and places of turning, then hang it with hooks until you extract it completely. And be careful when incising it that you do not cut or injure the vein, for that will make its extraction difficult for you; therefore, exert yourself carefully.
Image of the incision by which the varix is incised.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not be hooked like other hooks, nor should it be sharp-pointed, lest it injure the vein; rather it should be thick, smooth and bending, for if it is thin it will cut the vein by its thinness; rather it should have a greater thickness, as we said.
Chapter ninety-one.
On the removal of the varicose vein.
