This vein is generated in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot, arid countries with little fertility; and it may be generated in other places in the body besides the legs, and its generation is due to a corruption that occurs under the skin.
Just as living creatures and worms occur inside the bodies, and the gourd-worm and the worm generated between the skin and the flesh, and a sign of the beginning of the generation of this vein is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the place swells, then the vein begins to come out from the place of that swelling.
As if it were the root of a plant or an animal, and when its end appears, it should be wrapped with a small piece of lead weighing from one dirham to two dirhams, and tied, and the lead is left hanging from the leg.
Then, whenever anything comes out of it to the outside, wrap it in lead and tie it. If it is very long, cut some of it and wrap the remainder; and do not cut it from its root before it all comes out, for if you cut it, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing swelling and corruption in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be treated carefully and pulled out little by little until it comes out completely and nothing of it remains in the body. And from this vein, in some people, there may come out something that is five or ten cubits in length.
And it has reached me that it came out from a man twenty cubits long.  If it breaks off while you are treating it, then insert a probe into its hole, and cauterize it with a long cautery alongside the body until all its matter is emptied, and try to purify the place with medicines for some days.
Then treat it with the treatment of tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, producing many openings, and from each opening a branch comes out. Treat it as we mentioned in the classification and what preceded.
Chapter ninety-two.
Concerning the incision for the worm that is generated under the armpit and is called the cow's disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries the cow's disease, because it frequently affects cows. It is a small, single worm that is generated between the skin and the flesh and moves throughout the body, ascending and descending.
It is perceived by touch as it moves from one member to another until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin and comes out.  Its formation is from the corruption of some humors, as happens with worms, snakes, and gourd-like growths in the abdomen.
Its harm is expected to be that if it moves in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and come out, thus destroying the eye. This happens frequently. If you want to treat it and remove it, that is done when it moves and becomes perceptible to the touch.
It is necessary to firmly tie above and below it with a good binding, then incise and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you do not find it, apply cautery with fire to the area until you burn it. The greatest harm to be expected from it is damage to the eye, as we said.
If you see that it has reached the head near the eye, tie firmly below it on the forehead, then incise and remove it. The patient should be treated with purgative medicines to cleanse the body of bad, corrupt humors and should abstain from foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
Concerning the incision for the disease known as the *nafir*.
This disease, which in our country is called *nafir*, is a pain that appears in some members and then moves from one member to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in the countryside. She bared her arm and showed me a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm. After a short time, I saw that swelling moving with the ulna, like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
When it passed from one place to another, the pain disappeared from that place and settled in the shoulder. Then, after a while, it spread throughout the body until it reached the other arm. Then she told me that it circulated through her whole body, as I witnessed.
I was amazed at the speed of its passage from one member to another. I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this way, although I had seen several people whose pain moved from one member to another. I had not, however, seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
I could only account for this by the fact that the woman was from the countryside, with a dry body and visible veins; therefore, that moving wind appeared to the senses.  It should not appear in this way in people of comfort, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain, and if it appears to you visibly as we have mentioned, then bind above and below it tightly with a bandage and incise it until that confined wind emerges, and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by means of things that dispel the body's humors and disperse the winds, such as the seeds of the squirting cucumber and the seeds of the spikenard, and similar medicines.
Chapter ninety-four.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places in the body where they lodge. As for their differences according to their types, there are large ones and small ones, hollow glass ones and solid glass ones, and some have three points and some have four points.
Some have barbs and some have splinters. As for those according to the organs in which they lodge, they are of two kinds: either the organs are hollow principal organs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like; so if an arrow lodges in one of these organs and you see the signs of death which I shall describe to you later, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for death will overtake its owner in most cases. But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not hidden deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And of the signs [of injury to] the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane which is upon the brain, then there results from that a severe headache, stupor, and dizziness.
But if these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not hidden deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound. And of the signs [of injury to] the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane which is upon the brain, then there results from that a severe headache, stupor, and dizziness. and redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, convulsions, delirium, vomiting, and perhaps blood comes from the nostrils or ears, and perhaps speech is lost and the voice is gone, and a white moisture resembling gruel comes from the place of the wound, and from it flows something like the water of flesh.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the patient and from extracting the arrow if it has not been extracted. As for the signs [of an arrow] striking the heart, if it is near the left breast and you feel it as if it had been driven into something solid and not into something hollow,
and perhaps the arrow has a movement resembling the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and this is followed by coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting; then know that death is inevitably approaching. And a sign of the arrow wounding the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels which are near the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily and desires to inhale cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm which is in the chest, then it will be near the lower ribs, and the breathing will be labored with severe pain.
And he sighs and all the members of the shoulders move. If the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in its redness comes from the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, perhaps something undigested from the food comes from the wound.
And its condition is apparent. If the arrow strikes the abdomen and lodges in it, and something of the stool, or fat, or intestine which has been perforated comes out of the wound, then there is no hope of treating it or of extracting the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine comes out,
and something protrudes from it to the outside and the pain becomes severe for the patient, then know that he is perishing. As for the other members, like the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar members, they are mostly safe.
When the arrow does not encounter a vein or nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned. And I inform you of some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may deduce from that your treatment. And that is that an arrow once struck a man in the corner of his eye at the root of the nose.
Then I extracted it for him from the other side, under the lobe of the ear, and he recovered, and no harm occurred to his eye. And I extracted another arrow for a Jew which had struck him in the lobe of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had become hidden, and I only reached the small end which is attached to the wood.
And it was a large arrow from the composite bows, square-headed, smooth, without barbs.  And the Jew recovered, and no ill event occurred to his eye. And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arabic.
And it is the one with two barbs. So I made an incision upon him between the two muscles of the neck, and it had sunk into his throat. So I gently worked at it until I extracted it, and the Christian was safe and recovered. And I extracted another arrow for a man which had struck him in the abdomen, and we estimated that he would die from it.
Then when thirty days or so had passed, and nothing in his condition had changed, I made an incision upon the arrow and manipulated it and extracted it, and he recovered and no ill event occurred to him. And I saw a man struck by an arrow in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
Then when seven years had passed, the arrow emerged at the root of his thigh. And I saw a woman who had been struck in her abdomen by an arrow, and the wound had closed, and the arrow remained, and nothing in her condition changed, nor did she experience any harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man struck by an arrow in his face, and the wound closed and he felt little pain from it.  And there are many similar cases. And I extracted an arrow for a man, one of the Sultan's guards, which had struck him in the middle of his nose, inclining slightly to the right side.
And the entire arrow was hidden. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow had struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I probed it with a thin probe, but I did not feel it. And he felt a stinging and pain under his ear on the right side.
I hoped that this stinging was from the point of the arrow, so I applied a dressing to the area with drawing and ripening power, hoping that the area would swell and the sign of the arrow would appear, so that I could incise it. But nothing happened in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached that place.
So I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened. Then, during that time, the wound closed, and the patient remained hopeless about its removal, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. He informed me of this, and I applied a strong caustic medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it, and I felt the thin tip of the arrow which adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared clearly to us.  And I spent about four months with it.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to being removed.  So I continued to gently manipulate it and devise ways with various instruments until one day I grasped it with firm forceps, as its image appears at the end of the chapter.
And the physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound united, and the patient recovered completely without any further harm.  And I tell you about the method of removing some arrows so that you may use this as a measure and guide for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description, nor encompassed by a book.  Rather, the skillful craftsman measures the much by the little, and the present by the absent, and devises a new work and a new instrument for strange emergencies.
If we descend from this craft, then I say that arrows are removed from the members in which they are embedded in two ways: either by pulling from the place where they entered, or from the opposite side, and those that are removed from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, and it is pulled out. If it does not yield to being pulled out at the time it is encountered, then you should leave it for some days until the flesh around it putrifies, so that it is easy to pull and remove it.  Likewise, if it is embedded in bone.
And if it does not yield to being pulled out, then leave it also for some days and try pulling and moving it every day, for it will come out. If it does not yield to being pulled out after days, then you should pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine borer, until you widen the space for the arrow, then pull it out.
If the arrow embedded in the bone of the head has penetrated deeply into one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of those symptoms which I have mentioned to you appear in the patient, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after some days.
then proceed to pull out the arrow. If it is deeply embedded and does not yield to pulling, then use the borers around the arrow as I have described to you, then treat the area until it heals. But if the arrow has disappeared in a part of the body, and is hidden from sensation, then search for it with a probe. If you feel it, then pull it out with some instrument suitable for pulling it out. If you are not able to reach it because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is neither bone, nor nerve, nor vein,
then incise it until the wound widens and you can reach the arrow so that you may pull it out. If it has barbs, grasp them, and free the flesh caught in them from every side by every means you can. If you cannot free the flesh, break off the barbs and twist them until you are free.
And when you attempt to remove the arrow, wherever it may be, use the pressure of your hand with forceps in all directions until you free it. Exercise the utmost gentleness, lest the arrow break and it becomes difficult for you to pull it out. If you cannot remove it at the time,
leave it for some days until the flesh around it becomes putrid, then try again; for it will then be easier. If bleeding hinders you, use what we have mentioned in its chapter. Guard carefully against cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon, and use skill.
In every way you can free the arrow, and let this be done gently and carefully and firmly as I have described to you.  It is necessary that when you pull out the arrow you should place the patient in the same position he was in when the arrow struck him; this is more suitable.
If you cannot do that, use whatever positions you can. As for an arrow that emerges from the opposite side, either something of it will have protruded outwards, or you will find the point of the arrow by touch just beneath the skin nearby and see it projecting.
Then make an incision upon it; let the incision be of a size sufficient for the forceps. Then pull it out, for it will be easy to remove. If it is stuck in a bone, rotate your hand until the arrow affects the bone and makes room for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for several days, then try again until it comes out. If the arrow shaft is still in place, push it with that. If the shaft has fallen off and you want to use pushing, then insert the hollow instrument so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow, then push it with that.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters that cavity, for the arrow will then easily come out. If the arrow is poisoned, it is necessary that you cut away all the flesh into which the poison has penetrated, if you can do so, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow lodged in the chest, or in the abdomen, or in the bladder, or in the flank, and it is near what can be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision upon it, make an incision and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and take it out.
Then suture the wound if it needs suturing, then treat it until it heals.  Image of the forceps with which arrows are extracted:
Their ends are like the beak of a bird; they are made like a file, so that when they grasp an arrow or something, they do not let go.  And various sizes—large, small, and medium—are made, all according to the size of the arrow, the size of the wound, and its narrowness.
Image of the hollow pusher:
Image of the solid pusher:
This has a solid end like a punch, to make it easier to enter the hollow arrow and push it.
Chapter ninety-five.
In the incision of veins.
The veins which it is customary to incise in the body are thirty veins; of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the “sensitive” ones; and the two arteries in the temples, which are apparent; and the two veins in the corners of the eyes, known as the “visual” ones.
And the vein rising in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose, and the two jugulars which are in the neck, and the two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins which are in the lower lip—these veins are known as the “jaw” veins—and the two veins which are under the tongue.
As for the veins which are incised in the arm and hand, they are five veins: one of them is the cephalic, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the “head” vein; and the median, which is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the “body” vein.
And the basilic, which is situated on the inner side, and it is also called the axillary; and the common people call it the “belly” vein; and the vein of the forearm, which is situated on the ulna, and to which it adheres, and which appears very clearly above the thumb; and the azygos.
And this is the vein which is between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one of them is that which is below the hollow of the knee on the outer side; and the second is the saphenous, and its place is at the heel on the inner side; and the vein of the women, and its place is at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them. As for the two veins which are behind the ears, the benefit of their incision is for chronic colds, migraine, ringworm, and chronic bad sores of the head, and their method of incision is as I describe.
And that is to shave the head of the patient and rub his head well in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the two veins and their position appear behind the ears in the two low places of the head, then you feel them with your finger, and where you feel their pulsation under your finger, there you mark with ink.
Then you take a lancet, which is known as the *nashal*, then you insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone, then you raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin a cut, and the length of the cut is the amount of two joined fingers.
Or something similar, and you let out the amount of blood that you want, then you bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal.  And I have already mentioned in the beginning of the book their cutting and cauterization. As for the two arteries which are in the temples, the benefit of their incision is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and the flow of sharp humors directed towards the eyes.
And their method of incision is as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the veins appear clearly to the touch and their pulsation is evident under your finger; then you mark with ink. Then you raise the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
And you insert the lancet, the *nashal*, from below and raise the vein upwards and cut it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out blood according to your need, then you untie the patient's constriction and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it and bind it tightly from above and leave it until it heals. And I have already mentioned them, their cutting, and their opening, in the beginning of the book. As for the incision of the vein of the forehead, its benefit, after the incision of the cephalic vein, is for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, ulcers, and persistent redness.
And the method of its incision, as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the vein appears; then you take the instrument which is called the axe, and this is its image:
You place the projecting spike which is in the head of the axe on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or something similar to it, and you let the blood flow to the amount you desire; then you untie the patient's constriction and bind him until he heals.
And it may be opened with a wide lancet, except that it should not be sharp-pointed like other lancets, but rather slightly wide-ended, and you incise it obliquely, because the bone is near, and the lancet might break in it if it is thin.
As for the two veins which are at the temples, their benefit is for ailments of the eye, such as ringworm, redness, and cataracts, and diseases of the face. As for the method of their incision, it is that the patient constricts his neck with a turban, then you incise them while you are standing over his head, and let the incision be slightly oblique and lengthwise with a small, slightly wide lancet.
For the place has no flesh in it; if the lancet is thin-pointed, it might break. Then you draw off the amount of blood you need, and you place cotton on them and bind them for one night, then you unbind them.  As for the incision of the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headache and diseases of the face such as erysipelas which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic.  The method of its incision is that the patient constricts his neck, then you hold his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long lancet.
And you pierce it in the middle of the nasal septum itself, between the nasal partition, straightly, because the vein does not appear to the touch there; then the blood will flow immediately. And you should move your hand with the lancet a little and draw off the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night, for it clots quickly.
As for the jugular veins, the benefit of their incision is for shortness of breath, the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, impetigo, bad ulcers, and cankers.  The method of their incision is that the patient constricts beneath them in his neck with a ligature, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you incise the vein lengthwise with a slightly wide incision, then you draw off a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see as necessary. Then you do likewise with the other vein, then you untie the ligature.
And you tie the two veins with a moderate tightness, lest the patient be choked, and you leave him until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the veins of the temples, the benefit of their incision is, after the incision of the cephalic veins, that it benefits from canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, cracking of the lips, and bad ulcers.
which are in the nose and around it.  The method of their incision is that you seat the patient before you and tie his neck with a turban, then you move his lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see to the right of the lip and the other to the left of it, and you recognize them by their blackness.
And that is also because around them are small, black veins, so cut them with a cutting incision. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, then aim to cut the largest and most obvious of them.  Likewise, do in the two veins which are in the lower lip.
As for the two veins which are under the tongue, the benefit of their incision is, after the incision of the cephalic veins, for the angina which are in the throat from a disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth.  The method of their incision is that you seat the patient between your hands towards the sun, and raise his tongue, and look under the tongue, on one side a vein and on the other side a vein, and their color is towards blackness.
Then you incise them and be careful not to go too far in cutting them, for beneath them are arteries, so perhaps bleeding from those arteries might occur. As for the three veins which are incised in the elbow, they are those which custom has established for incision in all people, and their incision is in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, blunt-ended or olive-shaped lancet to the depth of the flesh, or by cutting with a scalpel-like lancet, which is the *nashal* [and this is its image], the image of the wide, blunt-ended lancet:
It is wide as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins which contain thick, turbid blood. And this is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
And this lancet is less wide and thinner at the tip; it is suitable for the incision of thin veins which contain thin, yellowish blood. And this is the image of the *nashal* lancet:
This is the *nashal*, which is suitable for cutting, and there are types of it, wide and narrow, according to the width and narrowness of the veins also. And by this one may infer other things, and it is well-known among artisans.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from what is next to the chest and abdomen.  It is necessary for the phlebotomist, when incising it, to be cautious and to be at a distance from it, for beneath it is an artery. If he makes a mistake and goes too deep with the lancet, he will cut that artery.
Then a hemorrhage of blood occurs. Therefore it is necessary that its incision should not be with the lancet for puncture, but its incision should be a cut with the *nashal*. If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to avoid it and turn to another, or to look for one of its branches.
Or incise in its place the vein of the arm, for it is clear, and incise it with the *nashal* lancet as we said. If you wish to incise it itself, then before tightening the arm it is necessary to feel the place until you identify the location of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then you tie the arm and incise the vein with an oblique incision with the *nashal* lancet, as we said, and take care that the blow falls far from the location of the artery. And whenever you see, when tightening the ligature, a swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, at the time of incision, spurt like the urine of a boy, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery. Then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the blood stops, and it often does stop, then tighten the arm.
And leave it. And warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be on a diet and not move it for some days until it heals. If the blood does not stop and it overcomes you, and you do not have medicine at hand, then amputate the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract and the blood will stop.
Or take a walnut shell and split it, and take one half and bind it tightly to the vein with a ligature and bandages until the next day. If the blood stops, well and good; otherwise treat it with what was previously mentioned of placing styptics, and stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the tightness of the ligature in the arm, inform him of it.  As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the top of the head and the lower body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
And it is necessary for the one who is bleeding him to be on a diet from his bleeding, for there is a nerve under it. If he exceeds in the insertion of the lancet and hits the nerve, numbness occurs in it, which is difficult to cure, and perhaps it will not heal at all.  And this nerve is often perceptible to the touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and not apparent, then you should make your incision of it a longitudinal incision and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the head.
And it benefits from diseases of the eyes, and it is necessary in this vein in particular, if you wish to bleed it, to insert the olive-shaped lancet or the wide, basil-shaped lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery or nerve under it.
Except that it is necessary for you during bleeding to avoid only the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft place.  And it does not harm him if he is not hit with the first strike to be bled again several times, except that it might swell in some people if it is not bled in the first strike.
But that swelling does not harm him at all. As for the method of bleeding and its complications and what should be done beforehand in its treatment, the first of that is that you should know that bleeding is either used only to maintain health.
And it is either used in diseases.  And that which is used to maintain health and its duration and to guard against the occurrence of diseases is that bleeding should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, I mean the cephalic, the median basilic, and the basilic, and that bleeding should be at the beginning of spring if signs of fullness appear.
And the bleeding should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of the day have passed. As for the bleeding that is used in diseases, it does not have a limited time, but whenever necessity and need dictate it, by night or day.
And in every hour and at every time.  And children should not be bled until fourteen years have passed over them, nor should the elderly who have passed sixty years be bled.  If someone intends to bleed for any reason whatsoever,
he should cleanse his bowels before bleeding with a gentle enema if there is much retained fecal matter in them, lest putrid matter be drawn into the veins from the bowels during bleeding, harming the principal organs. And do not bleed the consumptive, nor the drunkard, nor the intoxicated until that passes from them.
And let him also beware of bleeding after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive coitus, exertion, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything that weakens the strength, whether physical or mental.  Then look to the thinning of the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
with foods, drinks, and medicines if he can, then he should enter the bath if nothing prevents him, or perform some exercise so that the blood may be thinned, and let his bleeding be in the middle of the day, as we have said, and let him intend to clear his breast that day of all bad psychological conditions.
like worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical conditions like excessive exertion and fatigue, coitus, and the like. And let there be present in his session those things which people customarily use, such as various perfumes, scents, entertainments, and the like, each person according to what he can manage.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion higher than the cushion on which the person to be bled sits. Then he extends his arm, and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the ligature with the bandage and twists it two or three times; let the tightening be moderate.
For if the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightness, it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is toward relaxation, it also prevents the flow of blood. Then, after the tightening, it is necessary that the person to be bled rub both his hands together.
until the veins swell and become perceptible to the touch. Then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then places the index finger of his left hand on the same vein a little below the place which he wants to bleed, lest the vein evade and avoid the incision. For some veins you find are like a tendon; they evade when bled, and some are full of wind; when the lancet is placed on them, they sink below the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein, or if it opens it, it is only a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be certain and deliberate in all these matters, then he lowers the lancet. If the vein opens at that time, well and good; otherwise, he repeats it again a little below or above that place quickly, if the place is not swollen.
If the patient swells or is frightened, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the ligature, for it might cause a hot swelling, and do not enter the bath. Then he may repeat the bloodletting if he wishes.  If the lancet penetrates and the opening is small and the flow of blood is thin,
and you fear that the amount of blood you desire will not come out, then return the lancet to the same hole gently and straight, and increase the opening a little, and do this quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place may swell with a small opening.
But if you see that it has swollen, do not repeat it at all, for it will not help you. Place something of oil dregs on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood, and it is better in this case than the oil itself and other ointments as well.
Therefore use oil dregs in all your phlebotomies when the flow of blood is difficult.  The theriac and the  shakhzanāj may also do this if one of them is placed on the site;  the blood will thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of the bloodletting—and this often happens, especially to someone who has only been bled once or whose vein opening was small—then act quickly and place a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water on the swelling and bind it for an hour, for it will resolve.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has completely come out from the vein itself or from another vein. If something black or green remains in the place after a few days, it does not harm.  If you wish, apply some dissolved aloes and bitter drug to it.
Or something of the juice of the *fudanj* and the like. Often a swelling and a lump occur when bleeding the basilica vein. Place your hand on it; if you find it fluctuates when you tap it, then that is a bad lump, so beware of putting anything of what we have mentioned on it.
For perhaps arterial blood will bleed from it, but bandage it with something constricting to solidify the area, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals. It is necessary that you remove blood from each person according to his strength and what appears of the color predominant in the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it come out until it reddens; and likewise if you see it thick, send it forth until it thins; and likewise if it is sharp, until its sharpness goes away.  It is necessary that someone who is full-bodied and strong and needs to have blood removed at once...
...should widen the vein incision and the lancet should be wide. And for someone who is weak, the opposite is true. It is necessary that you remove it in stages and that the hole be narrow.  And the best thing to use in bloodletting is something sharp, lancet-shaped, incised, not pierced.
And this type of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve; it is better and safer than bloodletting across or lengthwise. And if someone is accustomed to fainting during bloodletting, it is necessary that you feed him before bloodletting something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or oxymel.
If he is feverish and blood is removed from him in three or four times, then if he is cold-natured, let him take before bloodletting bread soaked in hydromel or in honeyed drink flavored with mint or in a sweet-smelling basil drink.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and its cause is the removal of much blood, it is necessary that he be given meat broth and thin basil drink, and he should be perfumed with ambergris, and his chest should be rubbed with it, and he should use all that we mentioned in the section on fainting.
which results from vomiting. And as for someone who wants to relieve his arm and to let his blood flow again, it is necessary for someone whose bloodletting was for copious evacuation and whose strength is weak that the blood be let little by little according to his strength on consecutive days.
And as for someone who wants to relieve his arm and to let his blood flow again and whose body is strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting. And as for someone who wants to draw the blood from his body to the opposite side to which it inclined, it is necessary that he be relieved on the second or third day.
And as for someone whose blood is abundant, who has become feverish and sharp, and has developed a fever, it is necessary that blood be removed from him at one time and that a large amount be removed, and the incision should be widened until fainting occurs, after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting. And that you place your hand on his pulse when the blood flows, lest death occur instead of fainting, for that often occurs if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs. And it is not necessary, if you want to open the arm and let the blood flow again.
And if the mouth of the vein has closed and the exit of the blood is difficult, do not prick it forcefully or twist it strongly, for that is very bad. Rather, either leave it until you bleed it again, or remove with the blade of the lancet what has congealed of the blood in the mouth of the vein.
Or apply something of salt dissolved in water, or apply something of theriac or  shakhzaniya, and prick it gently until the blood comes out.  If the vein has swollen, leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If the necessity to let blood again arises, then either bleed him above that place, or bleed him in the other arm or in another vein.
As for phlebotomy of the brachial vein, it is bled instead of the basilic and cephalic veins if they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them.  Its method of bleeding is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then it is tied above it with a ligature, a little moderately tight.
Then bleed the vein slightly obliquely, neither across nor lengthwise, and let the opening be wide, and your bleeding of it should be a little above the wrist joint. If the exit of blood is difficult, return the hand to the vessel of hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches your need. If you are in the summer days, you may dispense with returning the hand to the hot water.  The practice of letting the blood flow into hot water is mostly done in winter.  And bleeding this vein is safest of all veins. Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it. As for bleeding the cephalic vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments.  Its method of bleeding is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no major vein or nerve beneath it. As for bleeding the cephalic vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments.  Its method of bleeding is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very perceptible to the touch. Then bleed it slightly obliquely; and even if you sever it entirely, it will not harm him at all. Be careful not to thrust your hand deeply with the lancet, for beneath it are the nerves of the fingers, and the place is bare of flesh. Then return the hand to the hot water.
And let the blood flow in it; for if you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will congeal in the mouth of the vein and prevent its flow. When you have removed the amount of blood needed, apply fat and salt to the vein lest it close quickly.
And similarly you should do with every narrow branch. As for the benefit of bleeding from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen; and you do the same in bleeding it as you did in the second, equally. As for bleeding the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower part of the body, such as uterine ailments.
and suppression of menstruation, and kidney diseases, and chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar diseases. Its method of bleeding is that he puts his leg in hot water and rubs it until the veins swell, then you tie above the ankle joint with a bandage; and the vein is located at the heel, on the surface toward the great toe.
And many branches extend from it on the front of the leg. Therefore, bleed it in the widest branch of it, or at the heel where it converges; this is best and safest.  If you bleed it on the front of the leg, then beware of the nerves beneath it on the front of the leg.
And make your incision as if you were intending to amputate it, and let the lancet be sharp. If the blood does not come out, then let him return his leg to hot water, and let the blood flow in it until it is emptied. If the phlebotomist misses the vein on the first attempt,
let him try again, bleeding a little higher; for the place is safe, nothing is to be feared from it if you beware of the nerve, as we said. And do the same with the saphenous vein of the other leg, equally. As for the sciatic vein, its place, as we said, is at the heel, on the outer side.
And the benefit of bleeding it is for hip pain, if that is from hot blood.  And its method of bleeding is that you put the patient in the bath and hurry, and you bind his leg from the hip up to above the heel by four fingers’ breadth with a thin, long bandage, for it does not appear except by that.
Then, when it appears, bleed it in any way you can; either by making an incision, which is best, or by amputating it, or by incising it; for its place is safe, and it is very hidden in most people. If you do not find it,
and it does not appear at all to the touch, then bleed some of its branches, which are those which appear on the back of the foot towards the little finger and the ring finger; and beware of the nerves, and let out the amount of blood you want.
Then loosen the binding, and put cotton on the place of bleeding, and bind the place; for it quickly heals.
Chapter ninety-six.
Concerning cupping and its method of use.
Cupping instruments may be made of horn, wood, brass, or glass, and cupping is of two kinds: one is cupping with scarification and the removal of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping without scarification is of two kinds.
And the cupping of the mastoid processes, which are the sides of the neck on both sides, and the cupping of the chin, which is under the lower jaw from the mouth, and the cupping of the shoulders, and the cupping of the coccyx at the base of the spine, and the cupping of the forearms, which are the middles of the arms, and the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and for that reason it does not weaken the body as bloodletting does, and cupping, whether with fire or without fire, should not be used in any disease that is due to plethora until the whole body is purged.
If cupping is needed because of illness or custom, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle of it, and at any time whatsoever.
That is because some people, when their blood increases to the point that they need to have it removed by cupping, feel heaviness and headache in their heads; and some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck; and some feel itching in their face and sides, and darkness and pain in their eyes; and some itch in the place of their cupping.
And some laugh a lot, and some find the taste of blood in their mouths, and their gums swell, and they spit blood, and some sleep a lot, and some see blood, redness, wounds, and injuries in their sleep, and things like that.  So when we see any of that...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours of the day have passed. As for the benefit of cupping the temples, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water that pours into the eyes.
But it should be after evacuating the whole body. And this cupping may be a substitute for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.  It is cautioned that it not be used by someone who is cold-brained or has a cold, for it will harm him greatly.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their heads should not use it, and those who use it habitually will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, you should instruct the barber-surgeon to lower his hand with the cupping glass a little lower, for fear of generating forgetfulness. As for cupping the nape of the neck, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic and cephalic veins.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory apparatus, cough, and fullness.  The cupping of the nape of the neck should be raised a little, for if it is lowered, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it benefits from pains occurring in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and toothache. It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic vein. You should instruct the barber-surgeon not to deepen his hand with the scarificator, lest he cut an artery and cause hemorrhage.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from canker sores in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth. It may take the place of phlebotomy of the labial veins. As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations that result from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the insides of the forearms, it benefits from what benefits phlebotomy of the three veins—the basilic, cephalic, and median—because it attracts blood from all those small veins in the flesh, and it attracts those small veins from other thicker veins, until the attraction reaches the three large veins.
You should instruct the barber-surgeon not to be excessive in scarification, because the area is sparsely fleshed, and underneath are nerves and arteries. As for the single cupping performed on the coccyx, it benefits from hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
You should instruct the barber-surgeon that the cupping glass should be large and made of copper, because the area needs strong suction, and a glass cupping glass may break and require much scarification. As for cupping the legs, it visibly reduces fullness.
Because it attracts blood from the whole body, it benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; it promotes menstruation; it benefits from boils and abscesses; and it takes the place of phlebotomy of the saphenous veins and the veins of the ankles, except that it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people.  The benefits of cupping the ankles are similar to the benefits of cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping glasses is that the cupping glass is first placed empty and moderate suction is applied. Do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors are drawn to the area evenly. Continue to repeat this until you see that the area has reddened and opened.
When the redness of blood appears, then scarify and gently resume suction. Then observe the condition of the bodies. If a person has thin flesh with porous skin, then you should scarify only once, lest the area ulcerate.
Instruct the barber-surgeon to widen the incision and deepen it slightly, and to regulate the suction gently and with a delicate movement. If the blood is thick, then it should be scarified twice: the first time to open a path for the thin, watery blood, and the second time to completely extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, repeat the scarification a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, we are satisfied with one scarification; if we want to remove a large amount of blood, we make more scarifications, especially if we judge that the blood is thick.
It is necessary to make a deep scarification, and the moderate limit in scarification is the depth of the skin only. What ointments should be used when applying cupping glasses, and what waters, and what the cupped person should avoid. As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and whose pores are tight,
it is necessary to anoint the places of the cupping glasses with opening, softening, and resolving ointments. If it is summertime, then such as rose ointment, violet ointment, sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is in winter, then such as daffodil ointment, iris ointment, chamomile ointment, or lily ointment and the like. If the waste matter is thick and cold, then let the ointment be marjoram ointment, or wormwood ointment, or mastic ointment, or rue ointment and the like.
If the cupped person has wide pores and soft flesh, then the ointment should be avoided.  These people should wash their cupping sites after cupping with rose water, or cold water, or fox grape water, or gourd water, or chickweed water, and the like. As for someone whose blood is very moist,
he should wash his cupping sites with vinegar, or with water of myrrh and sumac and the like. As for someone whose waste matter is thick, he should wash his cupping sites with old wine, or with marjoram water, or a decoction of rue, or chamomile and the like. Cupping should be avoided in the bathhouse and immediately after the bathhouse.
Rather, it should be done after leaving the bathhouse by an hour or two.  No one should sleep after cupping. What the cupped and bled person should manage before and after cupping.
First, it must be observed whether the cupped or bled person is choleric, and if sharpness and inflammation predominate in his blood, then he should take cooling things like pomegranate and dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, oxymel, and sherbet and the like.  And he should make his foods from the intestines and the flesh of sheep, stews and sour things and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, then he should be given honeyed drinks and quince drinks or seed oxymel, and he should take moderately fragrant wine, that which is between old and new. He should be ordered to eat little, and he should make his food from intestines and intestines and birds, and young pigeons, stews. And the drink on the day of cupping and bleeding should be more than the food.
And it may be necessary to give some people at certain times some of the  Faruq antidote, or musk medicine, or the silthā, before cupping and before bleeding, or after it, to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood. And it should not be given to those with fever.
As for cuppings which are without a condition, they are the cuppings which are placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, on the abdomen and navel, and the place of the kidneys and hip joint, for these organs do not tolerate a condition upon them, but rather it is desired by them either to draw blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing the cupping glass on the breasts in the case of epistaxis, or we use it to remove a cold humor which has accumulated in an organ, such as placing the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel, for it loosens the organ and warms it and removes the pain by resolving that humor.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if an obstruction or stone appears in them, for by the strength of its drawing it may open the obstruction or remove the stone from its place.  And likewise it acts if it is placed on the liver and spleen when a humor troubles them. And these cuppings may be used empty, by suction only.
And it may be used with fire, and it may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of phlegm; that is to say, fill the cupping glass, and let it be large, with hot water alone, or with water in which some herbs suitable for this have been cooked.
Then it is placed full on the place and held and removed and repeated several times according to need. And this is the image of the cupping glass which is used with fire:
Its opening should be the width of two open fingers, as we have pictured, and its depth about half a span, and in its side, about halfway up, there should be a small hole about the size that a needle will enter. It is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper, with a thick rim.
Smooth, level, and polished, lest it injure the member when placed upon it; and in its middle there should be a crossbar of copper or iron where the lighted candle is placed. And this cupping glass may be made larger than we have described, or smaller, according to the diseases.
And let the user be skilled, for the cupping glasses of boys and thin people are not the cupping glasses of men and those with full bodies. As for the method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the member, it is to bring a burning wick of tightly woven linen or a small candle of pitch and place it on the middle of the solid rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass.
So that the fire will rise upwards towards the bottom of the cupping glass, lest the body of the patient be burned. Then it is placed on the member, and the finger on the hole which we mentioned, so that when we have held the cupping glass as long as we need, the finger is removed.
And the vapor will come out through that hole, and the cupping glass will adhere to the place. Then bring the wick in the manner described, and repeat it if you need to. As for the cupping glass which is used in the treatment of sciatica with water, it has no solid rod nor hole.
Rather it is used by filling it with water and placing it on the member only. And this is its form:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to hold much water, the better it is.
Chapter ninety-seven.
On the suspension of the leech.
Leeches are used mostly in those members where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because of their smallness, like the lip and gum and so on, or because the member is devoid of flesh, like the finger and nose and so on.
And the method of using them is that you should choose leeches which are in fresh, pure water, free from impurities; then leave them for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body is first purged by phlebotomy or cupping, then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is placed upon it; and when it is full, it falls off. And if it is possible to suck the place with a cupping glass, it is more effective; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with much water, and rub and squeeze it.  If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and this is a trickle, then let a linen cloth be dipped in cold water.
And place it on top until the trickle stops. If the bleeding is profuse, sprinkle on it powdered vitriol or alum or the like of astringents until the bleeding stops, or apply halved peeled lupins to the place.
And leave it until the lupin adheres to the place, for then the blood will stop.  And it is necessary, if it is needed to reapply leeches, that those leeches not be used if others are available. And if the leeches refuse to attach, let the place be wiped with fresh blood.
Or pierce a needle into the place until some blood comes out, then apply it; for when it senses some blood, it will attach itself.  And if you want it to fall off, sprinkle on it some aloes or salt or ashes, for then it will fall off.
Chapter Three.
On setting (bones).
This chapter also treats of what is needed in the art of medicine, namely, the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never studied the books of the ancients in this field, nor read a single word of them.
For this reason, this art of science has become nonexistent in our country, and I have never encountered anyone at all who was skilled in it. And I have only derived what I have derived from my long reading of the books of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them, until I extracted knowledge of it from them; then I devoted myself to experience and practice throughout my life.
And I have depicted for you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience has followed it after I have presented it to you, and freed it from the branches of prolixity, and shortened it to the utmost, and clarified it to the utmost, and depicted for you in it many images of the instruments which are used in it.
For it is from the increase of explanation, as I did in the preceding two chapters. And there is no strength except in God, the Most High, the Most Great.
Chapter One.
In it are summaries and compendia concerning the matter of fractured bones, which it is necessary to present. And before we begin to mention the fractured and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary that we mention at the beginning of this chapter a summary of the discourse and sections. It necessitates you first to understand them and to stand upon their reality, you and whoever else is eager to learn this noble craft.
So I say that when a fracture or dislocation or wound or fall occurs to someone, it is necessary first to quickly bleed him or purge him, or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or the one to whom this has happened is a child or an old, aged man, or the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then restrict his food to cold vegetables and the meat of birds and gazelles, and forbid him drink and thick meats and overfilling with food, and every food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling is safe and the effusion of material to the location is not expected.
Then let the patient return to his first regimen, according to his custom.  When the broken bone begins to knit, then the patient should eat foods which provide abundant, thick, strong nourishment with viscosity, such as porridges, rice, heads, necks, and tripes of oxen. And eggs and soft fish and thick drinks and the like, for by this regimen the consolidation of the fracture will be quicker and better. And know that broken bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot ever unite and heal to their first nature.
Because of the dryness and hardness of their bones.  And it may unite and heal in bones that were extremely soft, like the bones of small children. But nature grows on the broken bone from all its sides something resembling glue, thick and viscous, which adheres to it and tightens it until it binds one part to another.
And it binds one part to another until it comes to the utmost strength and firmness, as it was at first, so that nothing hinders its actions.
For this reason it is necessary to give the patient foods which have solidity, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said. And know that fractures differ in their types according to the difference of the members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head, and a fracture of the bone of the chest is different from a fracture of the bone of the back.
And so also all the other members, each differing from the other. And we shall come to the mention of each kind of fracture, explained in its chapter and detailed from the others.  And the kind of fracture of the bone may also differ in itself.
For its fracture may be a splintering without any fragments being produced, or its fracture may be along the length of the bone, and its fracture may have fragments and excrescences, detached or not detached; and the fracture may be with a wound and rupture in the skin; and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each kind there is a special method in its setting, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And among the things by which a fracture of the bone is recognized are its crookedness and swelling and its being perceptible to the touch, and its roughness when you touch it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor roughness, nor do you feel any disturbance in the bone upon touching it, nor does the patient feel much pain, then there is no fracture, but it may be a sprain or a slight fracture or a slight crack; therefore you should not move it at all by pulling and probing.
Rather, apply to it from the medicines whose mention will come after a while, what suits the place, then bind it gently. And know that if the bone is broken and split into two without any fragments being produced, yet each part has departed from its fellow.
Then you should hasten at once to straighten and set it before a hot swelling occurs to it.  If a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then set it in whatever way you are able with gentleness and skill.
And know that its setting and straightening is easier than a bone in which fragments have occurred. Then it is bound as will be mentioned. If the bone has fragments, then it is necessary to pull the broken limb from both sides, whether hand or foot.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand. Let the placement of the limb be on a level surface in its natural shape, so that when the broken parts of the bone are extended, then return those fragments to their places in whatever way you are able with skill and gentleness.
And strive with all your might that you do not cause the patient pain or suffering by your action, and strive with all your might to join one of the bones to its fellow in the best manner. At that time it is necessary to touch and feel them with your hand; if you see something amiss, correct and straighten it to the best of your ability.
And beware of strong pulling and forceful probing, as many ignorant people do; and they often cause by their action a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb, as I have witnessed many times from their actions.
Then, after the setting, perfecting, and binding, keep that limb still and quiet, and warn the patient not to move it during his waking hours and sleep, and when he turns and is restless, and when he has a bowel movement, and in all his movements as much as he is able; and let him strive to maintain the position of the limb in a position where he feels safe from pain.
And that is because if, while the limb is in that position, he feels pain or ache, he should move it to another position where he does not feel pain, and he should strive to make that position level and straight.
Lest crookedness occur in the limb when it is set. As for the method of binding the broken limb, it is as I describe to you. Know that broken limbs differ in their smallness and largeness and forms; those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm and the like.
Then it is necessary that the bandages be soft and gentle; and those that are thick, like the thigh, back, and chest, then it is necessary that the bandages be broad and firm, because a broad bandage is needed for a large limb and binds it from every side with equal pressure, so that no defect intervenes.
Then, after you have finished the setting, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. With a soft pad on the place of the fracture, then begin to wrap the bandage on the fracture itself three or four times, according to what the limb requires, and pull the bandage slightly with your hand, then take it to the upper side of the fracture.
With less tightening than you did to the broken place, then you spread the bandage a little from the place of the fracture, and loosen the tightening little by little until you take from the correct position something suitable, then you take another bandage and wrap it also on the broken place.
Then take the bandage to the lower side of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the bandage be as we mentioned in the first upper wrap, then place between the bandages from the soft padding or rags what evens out the crookedness of the fracture.
If there is a crookedness, otherwise do not put anything in it, then wrap another bandage around it, then straighten the tight splints on these bandages immediately if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, then apply something that calms that inflammation and removes the swelling, and leave it for days, then tighten the splints on it then; and let the splints be from wide, carved, skillfully prepared halves of reeds, or let the splints be from sieve wood.
which are made from pine or date-palm stalks or  boxwood or similar materials, whatever is available, and let the width of each splint be in this same way and form.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb in its size, Image of the splint:
Then tighten the splints with another bandage in the same way as your first tightening, then tie from above with firm threads according to what we mentioned of the tightening, which is that your tightening should be greater at the place of the fracture. And the further it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and let them be of wet linen especially, for if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by the ignorant, who make their threads from twisted linen strips, it is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them becomes excessive, and thin threads are also unsuitable because you do not achieve with them the tightening you want, and there should not be less than a finger between splint and splint.
If the patient is harmed by the ends of the splints after tightening in the healthy places, place soft tow or fluffy wool beneath them so that nothing harms him from that, and as for if the fracture is with a wound and a rupture in the skin occurs, we will mention that separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day, and that if the limb is large, splints should not be applied to it except after five or seven days or more, depending on your assurance that hot swelling will not occur.
And this is a description of the bandages with which the ancients used to bind fractures, dislocations, and sprains; a description of a general, concise bandage which binds fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is something that is not dominated by heat or cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill, which is the best part of the flour that adheres to the walls of the mill during the movement of the millstone, and knead it as it is without sifting it with egg white, and make its kneading neither thick nor thin, then you apply it.
A description of another bandage which binds fractures, dislocations, and sprains:  Take of mastic, leadwort, acacia, orris root, gum tragacanth, and sugar, ten drachms of each; and of myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; and of the lote tree, twenty drachms; and of Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachms. Pound all together and mix with the water of the lote tree, or with egg white if the temperament of the patient is hot, then use this bandage, for it is well-composed, quickly mends broken bones, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
A description of another bandage which is also useful for fractures and sprains: Take gum tragacanth, mastic, and white lead, ten drachms of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; acacia, six drachms; Armenian clay, twenty drachms. Pound all together finely, sift, and knead with water or egg white and use.
A description of a bandage for joints and bones that have left their places, and it soothes the pain affecting them, and for bone splinters and fractures: Take purified wool and dip it in vinegar and cooked oil and place it on the affected area. This bandage does not have the power to set [bones].
But it is excellent in soothing hot swelling and relieving pain especially. A description of another bandage which sets a broken bone: Take the leaves of the black fig and the leaves of wild poppy and pound them together and bandage with them while moist. A description of another brief bandage used when a bone fracture is uniting.
And you want to resolve the rest of the swelling, take of the root of white lead, chamomile, violet flowers, and crushed acorns, one part of each; pound all together and knead it with egg white if the member is not inflamed; if it is inflamed, then knead it with coriander water or with water, and use it.
A description of another bandage stronger in resolving [swelling] than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs when the bone is uniting: Take of the root of white lead, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, marjoram, violet flowers, and chamomile, one part of each.
Pound all together and knead with vinegar water or with fresh water or with egg white, all according to the heat of the member and the stillness of its fever. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken member when it is set.
Except for the cerate made from wax and oil only, and he described it as being of medium thickness. As for Galen, he saw that things which contain gums with some heat, such as myrrh, aloes, and frankincense, and the like that we have mentioned, should be placed on the broken member when it is set.
A description of a bandage that is useful for weakness and pain: Take of  myrrh,  alum, human hair that is cut or bird feathers, white lead, and salt, equal parts; pound, sift, knead, and bandage with it.  As for the amount that the bandage should remain...
Then it is to be undone: you should observe; if neither pain nor itching occurs in the diseased part, nor does the broken bone move from its place, do not undo it for many days.  If, however, severe itching or troublesome pain or swelling occurs in the place.
Then hasten to undo it at once and do not delay that at all, and remove the bandage from it. Then take a soft rag or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the place with it until the itching and pain subside, then leave the member to rest for an hour.
Then apply wool moistened in vinegar and oil or rose oil and bind it on for a night, until you are safe from hot swelling and the swelling of the member subsides and its pain goes away. Then return it to gentle tightening and a light bandage, and do not tighten it as tightly as the first time.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and puffiness, and all the symptoms have completely disappeared, and you need a bandage and tightening, then prepare it as you did initially, equally. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the member...
...then do not undo it except after three, four, five, or seven days; and it may be left for twenty days; all this depends on what appears to you of the condition of the member, as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh is about to consolidate upon it.
Then also increase the tightening more than your first tightening, and also increase the thickness of the patient's diet to what was previously mentioned. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried out and become thinner than it should be, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you unbind it, and let that be every three days, and loosen the tightening a little. For by this action, nourishment flows to the member and it heals quickly. As for what ignorant bone-setters do, breaking the member again...
...if it is not set properly the first time, and is set crookedly, that is an error in their actions and a great deception. And if it were correct, the early scholars would have mentioned it in their books and acted upon it; and I have not seen any trace of it from any of them at all. And the correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
Concerning a fracture occurring in the head.
The types of fracture occurring in the head are numerous, its forms varied, and its causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and it may either sever the bone completely until it reaches the membrane beneath the bone. Just as the wedge does in wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a wedge fracture.  Alternatively, the sword may cut some of the bone and sever only its surface, without the cut penetrating to the end. This type of fracture is called an absolute extraction, and the wound of these two fractures may be either large or small.
And there are fractures which are comminuted or contused, caused by a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone or something similar. This fracture may be penetrating, nearly reaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone.
The wound of these two fractures is also either large or small. And there are fractures which are hidden in the bone, fine as a hair, a slight crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called hairline.  There is also a fracture caused by a fall or blow from a stone or the like, which drives a part of the bone inwards, causing a depression in the area, like what happens to a copper pot when struck, causing part of it to cave in.
This most often occurs in the soft bones of the head, such as the heads of children. All these types of fractures may have loose and non-loose fragments. We will describe the treatment of all of these in their proper place, and we will identify all these types of fractures by examining them.
And examining them with probes and removing the corrupted flesh from them. As for the hairline type, it is identified by examining the bone, wiping it and applying ink to it; the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of fractures, first look to the patient's symptoms.
If you see symptoms that clearly indicate danger, such as vomiting bile, distension, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, sunken and reddened eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is inevitable with these symptoms in most cases.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his recovery, then proceed with his treatment.  If the injured person comes to you immediately after the injury, and this is during the winter.
Then you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case.  And if it is in the summer, then you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone is corrupted and those symptoms we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain and there is crushing and bruising, then you should cut away the crushed and bruised part as I describe to you, which is that you shave the head of the injured patient.
And uncover the bone in whichever way is possible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient. If, when you uncover the bone, you find bleeding or a hot swelling, then treat it accordingly.
This is to pack the area with cloths soaked in a potion and rose oil, and clean the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then begin to drill out and extract the bone, which can be done in one of two ways.
As for the first way, it is to cut the bone with a fine saw with a narrow blade.  This is its image:
Then use another saw after this one, slightly wider. This is its image:
Then also use another saw, wider still than the second.  It is necessary that you have several saws of different sizes, some wider than others, and some shorter than others. They should be extremely sharp and made of Indian steel or good quality tempered steel.
Use gentleness in applying the saw, lest you shake the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to drill around it before using the saws, with the drills called non-penetrating drills.  They are called non-penetrating drills.
Because they do not go beyond the bone of the skull to what is beyond it, for the drill has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a small ring or circle, preventing it from penetrating and going beyond the thickness of the bone.
You should have many of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of the bone, so that for every skull you have a drill whose sharp end is appropriate in length and shortness to the thickness of that skull. This is an image of three types. of drills: large, medium, and small.
As for the method of drilling around the fractured bone, you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then you move the drill to another place, making the distance between each hole about the thickness of a gimlet or similar.
Then cut between each two holes with the saw, doing so with the utmost gentleness as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument that you have prepared for this.
such as forceps and fine hooks. You should be very careful that the drill or saw does not touch any of the peritoneum. If the bone is removed and freed from the peritoneum, if it was attached to it, and you remove it, then it should be cleansed.
And smooth the roughness of what remains of the bone with another instrument similar to the saw, except that it should be thinner and finer than the other saws. If any small bones or fragments remain, remove them gently with the instruments at hand.
Then treat the wound afterwards with sutures and ointments, which will be mentioned. As for the other method of treatment, it is a very easy method, far from risk, which Galen mentioned and highly praised.
