This is what he said: First, you should begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until, when you have exposed that part, you place under it the tip of this lenticular saw, and this is its image:
Its lenticular part should be smooth so that it does not cut anything, and its sharp part should be on its long sides, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests on the peritoneum and the face of the sharp saw on the bone. Then saw on one side only.
With a small hammer, until you cut through all the bone gently, as it turns, and you are safe from the membrane, so that nothing frightening happens to it at all, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were asleep.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some places, scrape it off with the tip of the lenticular saw itself and carefully free it, for it will be freed without injury or fear.
But if the bone fracture has not penetrated to the membrane, and what has been broken is the surface of the bone, and roughness and small fragments remain in it, then you should remove that roughness and extract those fragments with fine forceps, of which various sizes have been made, so that you can use in each place the most suitable and best ones, according to what the work leads you to.
And the fractured bone itself and its shape, and you should first use the widest of those forceps to scrape the bone, then use a finer one after that, and continue to do so successively until you come to use the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small, hair-like cracks and slight fractures, you should use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its healing; this is something which is not hidden from anyone who has the slightest skill in this craft and has understood what we have written and explained about large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you extract the bone, you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it over the opening of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. And dip it in a syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do this as gently as you can so as not to weigh down the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage on top, and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are certain the acute inflammation has subsided, then unwrap it and after that use medicines with drying properties, such as iris roots, crushed costus, crushed spikenard, zedoary, and the like; you should make powders from these medicines.
And sprinkle them dry on the wound; in general, use a medicine that cleanses and does not sting, and strive during your treatment of the wound to keep it clean, free from grease or dirt, and do not allow pus to collect in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the membrane of the brain, it spoils and putrefies it, causing great harm to the patient; and this may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If blackness appears on its surface, and the patient exhibits the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he will inevitably die; but if the blackness resulted from a medicine applied to it, and it was in the nature of that medicine to cause that blackness.
Then you should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well, and apply them to a cloth, then place it on the peritoneum and treat it with the appropriate types of treatment until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of a broken nose.
Know that only the upper two parts of the nose, or one of them, can be broken, because they are bone; as for the lower part, it is cartilaginous and does not break, but rather it is subject to bruising, crookedness, and flattening.
If one of its parts is broken, you should insert your little finger into the nostril and set the fracture from the inside with your index and thumb from the outside, until you restore the nose to its natural shape.  Let this be done gently, being careful not to cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, it should be set with the end of a slightly thick probe. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same.  And let him be treated on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after seven or ten days, when the hot swelling has subsided. Then insert a tent of linen cloth into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two tents if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the tents be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some early bone-setters mentioned moistening the tents with fat and changing them daily, but I do not see this as necessary; rather, the tents should be moistened with egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the tents until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens.  Goose quills, wrapped in soft cloth, may be inserted into the nose at the site of the tents; this will hold the nasal fracture more firmly and prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not necessary; you may do it if you wish, or you may make tents instead. If hot swelling appears in the nose during your work, apply a compress of linen or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or some diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling appears, you should dress it externally with fine semolina and finely ground myrrh, mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage over it. Do not bind the nose at all if the nasal bones are only slightly fractured.
Or if they are shattered, you should make an incision over them and remove the fragments with the appropriate instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with suitable ointments that promote healing. If a wound occurs inside the nose, you should treat it with tents and use lead tubes. until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the setting of lower jaw fractures.
If the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then if the fracture is only external and has not broken into two pieces and is not depressed inwards, its detection is easy.
If the fracture is in the right ramus, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is in the left ramus, insert the index finger of the right hand and gently elevate the fractured fragment from the inside to the outside.
With your other hand on the outside of the bone, you control and manipulate its alignment. If the jaw fracture is broken into two pieces, then traction should be applied from both sides to achieve alignment until you are able to set it. If it has affected the teeth, they will loosen.
Or they become separated, then tie those you expect to remain with a gold, silver, or silk thread. Then, apply a compress to the fractured jaw, then place a folded cloth over the compress, and over the cloth apply a tight, large splint or a piece of leather the length of the jaw. Then tie it from above as you see fit to bind it and support the setting so that it does not become dislodged. Instruct the patient to remain quiet and still, and give him soft liquids as food. If you suspect that anything has changed in terms of alignment, proceed to untie it on the third day.
Then tie it from above as you see fit to bind it and support the setting so that it does not become dislodged. Instruct the patient to remain quiet and still, and give him soft liquids as food. If you suspect that anything has changed in terms of alignment, proceed to untie it on the third day. Then repair the dressing with mill dust mixed with egg white or semolina flour after removing the compress.  Place a soft poultice over the dressing. As long as the dressing adheres and the bone's condition does not change, leave it.
Do not remove it until it heals and the fracture is consolidated. This fracture often consolidates in three weeks. If a hot inflammation occurs during that time, use what we have repeatedly mentioned to alleviate it until the swelling subsides.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, observe whether a bone splinter or splinters have become separated from the bone.  Gently remove these splinters with whatever instrument is suitable for their removal. If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then, when you have removed the splinters and none remain, suture the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for this purpose. And stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of a fractured clavicle.
The clavicle is most often fractured anteriorly at the acromial end, and the fracture may be one of three types: it may be fractured into two pieces without splintering, which is the easiest to treat.
Or the fracture may be comminuted, which is more difficult to treat; or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, the procedure is as follows: have two assistants, one to hold the arm adjacent to the fractured clavicle, and the other to extend the neck to the opposite side.
Then set the fracture with your fingers until it is properly aligned, without any prominence or angulation. If further extension is needed, a ball of rags or wool should be placed under the patient's armpit.
The size of the ball should suit your needs. Extend and elevate the clavicle, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has been driven inwards, the patient should lie on his back.
A moderately sized pillow is placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses his shoulder downwards until the inwardly displaced clavicle is elevated. Then set and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle is broken and mobile, you should incise over it and gently remove the fragment. If the fragment is embedded in the bone, try to cut it with one of the instruments prepared for this purpose. After placing under the clavicle the instrument that protects the pleura, which is made of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a bowl.  Its width should correspond to the size of the bone, large or small, and its length should also be according to what is convenient for the operation. Let it have two ends, as you see, one wider and the other narrower.
If the wound made when removing the bone fragment is wide and you are sure there is no hot inflammation, then close the edges of the wound with sutures. If the wound is small, or you fear hot inflammation, treat the wound with lint and bandages appropriate to the size of the incision.
If hot inflammation occurs, wet the lint in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting a bone without a wound or incision, apply a plaster made of mill dust and egg white. Place a soft pad over it, then place a ball under the armpit if necessary. Then take a bandage that is very long, about a cubit wide, then apply a folded compress over the pad and plaster, then take a splint of thin wood. Let its width be three fingers, and its length likewise. Then insert it into a cloth, then insert that cloth with the splint into the part of the bandage that lies over the fracture, then tighten the bandage over the fracture as you go around. And pass it over the neck and under the sound armpit and under the affected armpit, and pass it repeatedly on each side, however you see that the tightening firmly fixes the fracture, which is not hidden from you.
The whole point is that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should check on the patient every day; whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct that and tighten the bandage. Then have the patient sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm will be raised from his side, and the clavicle fracture will be raised with the elevation of the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck, and do not untie the bandage unless something happens in the area, such as itching or swelling, until twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. Return to the binding and leave it until the clavicle fracture heals and consolidates, and it becomes stronger and firmer; this happens in about twenty-eight days. And in some people, it may be in less than that.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of a fractured humerus.  The humerus rarely fractures in its wide part; rather, it fractures at its extremities.  When a part of it is fractured, or it is fractured in its middle, this is known by touch.  Therefore, the treatment varies depending on the nature of the fracture.
Set it and restore it to its natural shape in every way possible, then apply to the area mill dust with egg white and soft lint. Place over it a pad of folded cloth, and then apply a wide splint of wood.
A splint as wide as the entire shoulder or slightly wider. If, under the splint, there is a bump in any part of the shoulder, smooth that bump with soft lint until the splint lies evenly, then bind it tightly from above with a long bandage.
Bind it firmly and make sure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and reposition the splint if it has moved. Let the patient lie on his healthy side, and the shoulder will heal in twenty [days].
days or twenty-five days.  When this time is complete, remove the bandage, for you are safe. For it is among the bones which one does not fear will be shattered or dislocated. If a fragment of bone protrudes and is irritating under the skin, incise over it and remove it. And do what was mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle to soothe the hot swelling, if anything of that should occur.
Chapter Seven.
On the setting of a fracture of the chest.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens; but its extremities are more frequently fractured and shattered. Among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and there occurs severe pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing.
And sometimes blood is expelled, and the depression in the fractured bone becomes apparent and is not hidden from the touch.  Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back, and a pillow is placed between his shoulders; then his shoulders are compressed, and the ribs are brought together by the hands from both sides, and gentleness is employed in straightening it in every way possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be; then apply a bandage and a splint, and place over it a splint of a thin, light board of willow or elm, or similar, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then gently bind it to the fractured bone lest it move; and pass the bandage around to the back several times, and tighten it firmly; then examine the bandage frequently, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity demands its removal because of suppuration occurring at the site, or pain, or swelling, then hasten to remove it, take off the bandage, and treat what has occurred by its appropriate treatment; then reapply the bandage if you see fit to do so. And treat it in this way and keep it firmly bound until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
On the setting of ribs if they are fractured. Know that fractures of the ribs occur in the thick parts which are next to the back, and their extremities in front are subject to contusion because they are cartilaginous; and the knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense when examined with the fingers.
And its treatment is that you set the fracture with your fingers in the accessible manner until the shape is even as it should be; then you bandage and bind the fractured bone with a splint if it needs that.  If the fracture of the ribs is oblique inwards, then intense pain occurs to the patient.
And it pierces like the piercing which occurs to someone with a wound of the pleura, because the bone pierces the diaphragm, and also shortness of breath, coughing and the ejection of much blood occur to him. This is difficult to treat, and the ancients devised many methods for it.
And some of them said it is necessary to give the patient foods which produce flatulence and wind, so that the abdomen becomes distended and expands and the fracture is pushed outwards. And we dislike this lest it should confirm the occurrence of hot inflammation if it has not occurred; and if it has occurred then it increases and confirms it.
And some of them said a cupping glass should be placed on the place, then sucked strongly; and this is more like the correct method, except that it is feared that the cupping glass will draw impurities to the place in its weak state. And some of them said it is necessary to cover the place with wool which has been dipped in hot oil.
And make a bandage between the ribs until it is full, so that the binding is even when you wrap it round, then treat the patient with the treatment of a wound, with regard to food and medicine, if a severe matter which he cannot bear overwhelms the patient. And the bone pierced the diaphragm a harmful piercing, and we feared for the patient, so it is necessary that we make an incision at the place and uncover the broken rib, then we place under it the instrument which protects the peritoneum, which has been described previously, and we cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then we bring together the edges of the wound if it is large with stitching, and we treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot inflammation appears during this, then act quickly, moisten bandages in rose oil, and place on the place, and treat the patient with that which calms the inflammation internally also.
And he lies on the side on which sleep is easier for him until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On setting the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture affects them—and this rarely happens to them, and what most often happens to them is a bruise, and likewise the vertebrae of the back also—if this happens to someone and you want to know whether he will recover or not. Then look; and if you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and dead, and he is not able to move them, nor extend them, nor contract them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, nor does he find any pain in them, then know that he will not recover in most cases; he is dying.
And if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking in them, then know that the marrow of the bone is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment. If the vertebrae of the back are similarly affected and you want to know whether he will recover or not,
look at his legs; and if you see that they have become relaxed and what happened in the hands has happened in them, then if he lies on his back, wind and feces come out involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is dying; do not trouble yourself with his treatment. If nothing of that happens to him, the matter is less serious.  The treatment for what has happened is that you seek to alleviate the hot swelling.
By placing on the injured vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks, applying it three times a day, until the hot swelling subsides; then apply to the place one of the strengthening, drying compresses, and bind it tightly, and instruct him to remain still and quiet.
And he should not sleep except on the side where he feels no pain, until he recovers. If, at the time of the injury to the bone, a splinter or something has broken off from it, then it is necessary to incise the skin and remove that bone. Then bring together the edges of the wound if it is large, with sutures, then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, then you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the rectum.
And set the broken bone with the other hand, as much as you can manage and the setting allows, then apply the compress and splint if you need to, then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, then incise it and remove it, and treat the wound with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Hip bones rarely fracture; but if they do fracture, it is in such a way that their ends are shattered, they split lengthwise, and they incline inwards. The patient experiences pain in the affected area, and numbness and weakness in the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its treatment is that you pass your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the ends, then set that fracture as well as you can, so that it resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise, or if it has inclined inwards, lay the patient on his stomach so that you can set the fracture. When you have set it, apply a bandage, then apply a splint of wood or leather and bind it tightly.
Do not fear that the fracture will shift or that the splint will come loose. Fill the hollows of the hips with padding so that the binding is even. Instruct the patient to lie on his back or on his healthy side. If hot swelling occurs,
desist from setting and binding it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply what will alleviate it, as previously described, then return to setting and binding it properly. If there are fragments or shattered pieces of bone at its ends, they should not be removed.
They should not be touched, but smoothed from the outside as we have said, and the binding should be left in place until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the setting of a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder. If it is fractured, it is set in one of two ways. One is to take a smooth, curved stick of medium thickness, in this manner:
And you tie two bandages to its ends, then it is hung from a high place and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the stick until his armpit is stuck in the middle of the curve of the stick, then something heavy is hung from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with his hands together until he returns the fracture to what is appropriate. The other way is that the patient lies on his back and his hand is hung from his neck with a bandage.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and each stretches to his side. And if you want the stretching to be stronger, then tie below the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and each of the servants stretches them to his side.
If the fracture is close to the end of the shoulder, then the middle of the bandage should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.  Likewise, if the fracture is close to the elbow, then the bandage should be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then you set the fracture gently without violence until the fracture is set as it should be and unites well. Then you bandage it if no hot swelling appears. If hot swelling appears, then leave bandaging until the seventh day and place on it a moistened soft cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then bandage it. The method of bandaging is to place the dressing on the fracture, then place a bandage of new cloth on the dressing, then bind the arm to the humerus itself and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And you apply the cloth and bandage to the humerus and forearm so that the forearm acts as splints if nothing prevents you and nothing changes in the broken bone. If you fear that something will change, then use splints.
And that is to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint. And let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an increase of three fingers on each side.
Then you bind the splints with the binding that I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your binding on the place of the fracture should be tighter.
And the further from the fracture, the less tight the binding. If you see the placement of the splints and the binding as we said when you set the limb at the time, then do so.
And if you fear a hot swelling, leave off the binding and splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days, lest there be itching or swelling in the place, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive tightness; then correct all that as we have mentioned.
But if you are confident that none of that will happen, then do not loosen the bandage except after many days. And let the patient lie on his back, his hand on his stomach, and a cushion filled with wool should be placed under the armpit, moderately.
And examine at all times, night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be disturbed or the bandage loosened; then correct all this with your effort. And make the patient's food according to the order we presented, that it be mild at first, until the bone begins to strengthen.
Then it is necessary to increase his food. For it is the nature of the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days; then it is necessary to loosen [the bandage], and he should use the bath and be treated with ointments suitable for this.  If the fracture is severe and comminuted,
then do not loosen the bandage from him until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the ulna; one is small, which is that next to the thumb, and the other is large, which is situated below the small one from below.  So perhaps the larger ulna is fractured alone, or the smaller one alone, and perhaps they are fractured together.
And when the smaller upper ulna is fractured, then its setting is easy and its healing is quicker. And when the lower ulna is fractured, then its fracture is bad and its healing difficult. And the worst is if the two bones are fractured together.  If the bone which is fractured is the smaller upper ulna,
then it is necessary for the physician, when setting it, to make its extension easy gently until he straightens it. If the large ulna is fractured, then it is necessary to make its extension stronger. And if both ulnae are fractured, then it is necessary to make the extension much stronger.
And it is necessary that the shape of the hand, when it is set and extended, be extended on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be above, higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the rest of the fingers, and the patient should sit squarely on himself.
And let the cushion be opposite him in height, lest the patient should suffer difficulty.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends from above likewise. Then the physician straightens the bone until he restores it to the best shape he can. If there are fragments in the fracture of the bone, then they are removed.
Restore each fragment to its place to the best of your ability. If a fragment appears detached and it pierces the skin, and there is no hope of setting it, then incise upon it and remove it in the manner which we have mentioned previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then it has been given a separate chapter.
Then take its treatment from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your setting, then apply a cloth with kypros prepared with rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between thickness and thinness, and bind the cloth upon it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the kypros and apply the prepared bandage of mill dust with egg white. Then apply the splints; and let the splint which is placed upon the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger. And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most instances.
Whether the fracture was in one forearm bone or in both forearm bones together, then make your binding at the location of the fracture stronger and tighter. And whenever you turn the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding somewhat looser, as previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths which are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, and not very hard. And let the thread with which it is bound be of special linen, moderate between thinness and thickness, as we have described. And examine the limb and the binding after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected, such as an itching that appears in the limb, then the limb should be bathed with warm water until that itching subsides.  And the limb should be left unbound for a night so that it may rest, then the binding should be resumed.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted or something like that, then repair all of that with your best effort. And also look, if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive binding, then it should be loosened a little and left for some days until nourishment flows to it, then bind it.
If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs to the patient, then it should not be unbound except after twenty days or so.  Then suspend the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be straight. He should carefully avoid agitated movements and sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all that according to the conditions of temperaments and conditions of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the hand and fingers.
Fractures rarely occur in the metacarpus and phalanges of the fingers; rather, contusions frequently occur.  Therefore, when a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand…
The patient should sit cross-legged with a chair directly in front, level with him.
Then he should place his hand upon it, extended. Then the bonesmith should extend the broken bones and the physician should set them, until they are well-set. Then a bandage and a splint should be applied, unless a hot inflammation occurs. Then a further splint should be applied on top, according to the location.
And it has been inserted into a soft cloth.  If the fracture is towards the lower part, towards the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball from a cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then bind it tightly with a long cloth.
Let the splint be of soft leather, so that it conforms to the skin with the flexion of the whole hand, and bind it as necessary. If the fracture is towards the outside, then the splint should be placed above and another below in the hand, so that the hand is open and straight.
Then the binding should be applied as the hand turns, and the fingers should be interwoven with the bandage. If the fracture affects one of the finger joints, if it is the thumb, then it should be set properly and then bound with the hand.  And if you wish, you can make a small, upright splint to support the fracture.
And it should not move. If the fracture is to the other fingers, such as the middle, index, little, or ring finger, then they should be set and bound with the healthy adjacent finger, or all bound together, which is better, or a small upright splint should be placed on them.
As we said for the thumb, and examine during and after your setting for hot inflammation, and treat it appropriately whenever anything of that occurs, as has been repeatedly described.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the setting of a fractured thigh.
The thigh bone is frequently fractured and is easily apparent to the touch, because it moves forwards and backwards.  Its setting is by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, the patient lying on his stomach.
Then each bandage is extended to its side moderately. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone; but if it is near the head of the thigh, then it is necessary to tie a soft bandage, either of wool or the like.
At the head of the thigh towards the groin, so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both his hands until he restores it to its natural form.
And the bone unites well. Then it is necessary to apply the bandage and tightening, if no hot inflammation occurs in the limb. But if inflammation occurs, leave it for several days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for its tightening, it is necessary to wrap a firm, wide bandage twice or three times around the fracture, and what remains is left. Then the leg is twisted until the heel is at the head of the thigh, and a long thread is passed between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, and the ends of the thread are brought up from both sides.
Then you wrap around the leg and thigh what remains of the bandage. Then you place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and you place one splint on the bone of the leg. Then you fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft rags so that the tightening is even.
Then begin with strong tightening from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns.  And the further the bandage is from the site of the fracture, the less, softer, and looser your tightening should be.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie with it the splints that are above. Then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the heel of the foot, and with them also tighten the ends of the splints on the other side.
So that the bandage does not move from its place. Then leave the tightening as it is as long as no pain, swelling, or puffiness, etc., occurs in the limb. If any of that happens, quickly untie it and correct whatever occurred, as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a sharp fragment of bone, it should be straightened if possible. Otherwise, incise over it and remove it, and treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.  This fracture of the thigh may be tightened without the leg being added to it with splints.
As we mentioned in the humerus and forearm, except that its treatment in this way does not result in lameness for the patient. But if it is treated alone without the leg being added to it, the patient will necessarily always be lame.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more or less, all that according to the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
Fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but it is often bruised. If a fracture does occur, it is either a crack or a shattering into pieces; and this occurs with or without a wound.
You will ascertain all this by touch, and you will set it by aligning the separated parts with your fingers until they are joined and united, as far as careful and gentle manipulation allows. Then apply a bandage and a circular splint if necessary.
Then tighten the appropriate bandage above, and treat all the conditions we described in other fractures, such as hot swelling and so on, by counteracting each symptom with what is suitable for it, until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the treatment of fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones; one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula.  They are subject to the same types of fracture as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg is displaced in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, and this happens lower down, this is something that will not escape your notice.
So use extension, reduction, and splinting accordingly; however, if the leg fracture is severe with many fragments, the extension should be gentler and lighter, and you should take great care in its treatment.
In treating the leg, there is something additional to what is done for the arm: when you have set the bones and completed the procedure, take two splints of pine wood, such as are used for smoothing rooms, placed between the cracks in the boards, or made from palm leaves or similar material.
Choose those of moderate thickness, not too thin, and let their length be that of the leg from the knee downwards. Then wrap each one twice with a cloth lengthwise, and place one on each side of the leg.
Let them extend from the knee to the bottom of the foot, and then bind the splints in three places: at both ends and in the middle.  With this support the leg is prevented from tilting to either side, and is held firmly in place. A wooden gutter, the length of the leg, may also be used.
It is placed in this to keep it from moving, and this is especially necessary in fractures complicated by a wound. Then examine the leg daily and give it the utmost care in case of swelling, inflammation or other complications. If any of these occur, treat them appropriately until healing is complete.  A leg fracture heals in about thirty days.
Chapter Seventeen.
On fractures of the bones of the leg and toes.
As for the heel, it is never fractured; as for the bones of the leg, they may be fractured; and the toes are also seldom fractured, but are usually bruised.
If a leg bone is fractured and you see that some bones overlap, have the patient stand with their foot straight on the ground, then place your foot on the elevated portion of the bone and press down to realign it.
Until they return to their places, then apply a bandage and splint from above, placing a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot; then bind the foot firmly after wrapping it with cloths and whatever else is needed.
If three or four days pass, untie the bandage, and you will find the bones straight, whether they were broken or dislocated.  The procedure is as you see.  If any of the toes are broken, set and align them according to what I described for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken toe, the length of the toe and slightly wider, then place the board I described under the foot and bind it firmly. If two, three or more bones are broken,
make a splint for each toe of the appropriate size, layered in soft cloth, and bind the board to the bottom of the foot, with each end of the board extending beyond the sole of the foot for a good hold.
And you should not forget to treat all that we mentioned to you in other fractures with the symptoms we mentioned, and treat each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubic bone and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then gently bend her backward.  Let her be held from behind, then let the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and forms a ball inside her vulva.
Then the woman is shaken and her buttocks raised little by little.  The cotton will then emerge until it is like a ball at the entrance of the vulva and the fractured bone will return to its place. Then place a pad on her back; when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she may urinate.
And return it in the manner in which it was first inserted, then she returns to her original position. Do this for seven days or so, for it will heal.  And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube to its mouth, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva.
Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva; the fracture will then return to its place. Then pack it with cotton and leave it as described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, use this method for its treatment.
And treat it as described for the hip bone. The correct treatment for these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for someone with some experience and who understands this book well, will not be hidden from you, because the fracture itself will guide you in most cases.
Understand the correct way to set and bind it. As for the penis, if it is fractured, take a goose's windpipe and insert the penis into it, then apply a bandage of cloth, bind it, and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the treatment of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Whoever suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus or similar, should immediately be bled if the conditions for bloodletting, as we have previously mentioned, are met.
If the wound is bleeding, you should immediately stop it by applying powdered alum if nothing else is available, then proceed to set the fracture that same day and do not delay.
If no hot swelling occurs, then if hot swelling does occur, postpone setting it until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides, and do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, for it exposes it to bad consequences.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin and exposed, you should gently try to reduce and realign it with your hands with slight extension; and if you cannot reduce and realign it with your hands, reduce it with this instrument, which is an instrument made of iron, seven or eight fingers long. And its width is according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of them, according to his need for treatment in each type of fracture, and let them be round, with a little thickness so that they do not bend when pressed during use.
And let the end be sharp, with a curve at its end, and let the upper part be thicker, and from its middle to the lower part much thinner.  And this is its image:
And it is called in Greek *peron*, meaning a small lever.  Its sharp, curved end should be placed on the end of the protruding bone, and push it gently until, when the bone returns and is somewhat aligned, you finish aligning the ends of the fracture with each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, then the end of that bone should be cut until the instrument can grasp it. If you are not able to reset the bone as we described at all, then cut it with a similar cut to those we mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, however you can, then remove any roughness and thin scales remaining on the bone. If, after resetting it, the patient feels severe, harmful pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, then do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. When you have finished setting the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not apply any ointment or anything containing fat to the wound.
Lest putrefaction and corruption occur in it. Then, apply the splints when you have finished setting the bone, and leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, to the size of the wound.
And beware, with all caution, of tightening the wound with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this, causing their patients either death, or gangrene, or tetanus.  Let your tightening be gentle, loose, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels disturbing pain in the place, then you should not apply splints. Make him bandages from strong cloths in the place of the splints and bind it with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus is beginning to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you placed on it with the drink, then use the lint and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, such as the four-ingredient ointment and the like.  You should loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every day, evening and morning, until it heals and mends. You should position the limb so that the pus flows down easily. If many days pass and the wound does not heal, nor the pus cease, then know that there are small bone splinters.
Then you should probe the wound; whatever of those splinters is loose, extract and remove it. Whatever is not loose, and is piercing the limb and causing pain, then work at cutting and extracting it in every way you can.
If the wound shows signs of inflammation, gangrene, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should counteract each symptom with the appropriate treatment previously described in its chapter.
And what you should pay attention to and keep in mind is that if a large bone is broken and protrudes from a limb, such as the thigh bone, the humerus, and similar large members, do not attempt to pull it or remove it. For death often results from such an attempt. Rather, leave it until it putrefies; perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of the complication which occurs after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially those near the joints; it makes the limb unsightly and may prevent it from performing its natural function.
Observe; if the complication is fresh, use astringent drugs such as aloes, frankincense, myrrh, gum mastic, acacia, and the like. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent syrup, or with egg white, or with vinegar.
Apply it to the complication with a bandage and bind it tightly. Leave the bandage undisturbed for many days, then unbind it and reapply another, until the complication disappears, God willing. Or, bind a tight lead plate to it.
For lead has the property of removing all that is superfluous in the limbs. If the complication has become hardened and severe, and removal is necessary, incise it from above and cut away the protruding excess, or shave it off with some instrument until it is gone.  Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the treatment of a fracture if it has healed, but the limb remains thin and not as it was originally.
If a bone fracture has healed, and the limb remains thin and weak, this is due to many causes. One reason is excessive loosening and tightening of the bandages, or excessive tightening of the bandages such that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, or excessive and repeated manipulation, or excessive movement at the wrong time, or a deficiency of blood and weakness in the patient's body.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that his blood increases; use of baths and inducing joy and happiness in him and the like; then applying pitch to the limb so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it, or repeatedly massaging it with lukewarm water. until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of fractured bones which have been set crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
If, after a limb has healed, a crookedness or a protrusion of the fractured bone, or a complication appears, and the appearance of the limb is thereby disfigured, unless the limb is prevented from its natural function, then one should not accept the opinion of those who claim that the limb should be broken from the head, even though many ignorant physicians and bone-setters in our country used to do so.
And this action is utterly reprehensible, leading to great harm, the least of which is disability; but if the crookedness and complication are recent, then it should be softened with water in which emollient herbs have been cooked, such as mallow leaves and roots, vervain, and the like.
And it should be bandaged with emollient bandages, such as well-made diachylon, or the sap of mallow root should be taken and beaten with chicken fat and lard and bandaged with it, or ripe figs should be taken and pounded with pigeon droppings and similar things, among the medicines called resolvents.
And sometimes the complication will resolve with constant gentle rubbing by hand, and the movement of the limb in every direction at all times should be used; but if the crookedness has become established and hardened, and treatment with iron is required,
then it should be incised above and the connection of the bone released, and whatever remains of the complication or bone should be cut with fine cuts, and gentleness should be used with effort and care, then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its place; it impedes movement, disfigures the limb, and causes the patient severe pains and aches.  If a dislocation occurs, one should immediately attempt to reduce it.
One should not delay at all, for if he delays, the place will swell and become inflamed, making it difficult to reduce the dislocation. Therefore, one should not delay, nor move, nor stretch it while it is swollen, because this often causes the patient spasms and harmful pain.
But if this occurs, one should immediately bleed the patient, then wait until the swelling subsides somewhat. Then bathe the limb with warm water and oil, and gently reduce it, treating each limb as described in its place.
The chapters on dislocations are also arranged, as with fractures, from the top of the body to the bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Dislocation of both jaws rarely occurs.  Their dislocation happens in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced, relaxing a little, or they are completely dislocated, relaxing fully. towards the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot retain it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stumbles in speech.  But if the dislocation is slight, in most cases it returns of its own accord very easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, then it is necessary to hasten its reduction quickly and not delay it at all. This is done by having an attendant hold the head of the patient, and the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside his mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
or he inserts both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers from the outside, leveling them, and he orders the patient to relax his jaw and release it to go to every side, while the physician levels the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, then use fomentations with hot water and oil until their reduction is facilitated, and do not delay their reduction at all, as we said.  Then, when they have returned and are aligned, and the mouth of the patient is closed and they are not relaxed, then apply to them pledgets of cloths with a paste made from wax, oil, and rose water.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on his back with his head elevated between two pillows, lest he move it to the right or left, and let him not strain to chew anything, but let his food be soft gruel.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is set, then let him eat whatever he wishes, and let him use it gently and not force the opening of his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws is difficult, if they become dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then fevers and persistent headaches often result from this, and perhaps the patient's abdomen will become distended, and perhaps he will vomit pure bile. If you see this, then know that it is corrupted.
And many die from what has befallen them in ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
In the reduction of the clavicle and the tip of the shoulder.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection to the chest.  It may dislocate to the outside, and this is evident to the touch. Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms.
Then press the place with your hand strongly, so that it returns. Then apply a bandage and padding and bind it. As for its end which is towards the shoulder and connected to it, it rarely dislocates. If it should dislocate sometime, it should be reduced and set as we have mentioned and as may be possible for you.
Then apply a bandage, padding, and binding, and instruct the patient to maintain rest and stillness until it heals. With this same treatment, the tip of the shoulder is reduced if it also leaves its place.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one, that it dislocates towards the armpit downwards; the second, that it dislocates towards the chest; and it may dislocate above the shoulder, but this happens rarely.
It does not dislocate to the rear because of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate to the front because of the nerve; it most often dislocates and protrudes downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is thin, for in them it quickly goes out and quickly goes in.
But as for those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, I mean that it goes out with difficulty and goes in with difficulty.  Sometimes a blow or a fall may happen to some people, and the shoulder swells with a hot swelling, so they think that it has dislocated.  It is necessary to examine that until you ascertain its truth.
Then you proceed to its treatment. The dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit; if you compare between the dislocated shoulder and the sound shoulder, you will find a clear difference between them, and you will find the head of the humerus distorted and under the armpit, on palpation, the head of the humerus like an egg.
And the patient is not able to raise his hand to his ear, nor to move it in all movements.  Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find that clearly apparent to the touch, it is not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child. Its reduction is that a servant raises his hand upwards, then you place the thumbs of your hands under his armpit and raise the joint forcefully upwards to its place, and the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lowers it downwards, and it returns quickly. If it does not return by what we have mentioned, and the dislocation has been for many days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use things that relax and soften, such as cooking marshmallow root, fenugreek, and rue in water, and use them.
Then the patient lies on his back, and you place under his armpit a ball of wool which is moderate between softness and firmness, then the physician places his heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, then it returns to its place. And if you wish to reduce it in this way, it is that you bring a taller man than the patient and stand him on the side, and he puts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards his stomach. If the patient is light, then something else should be hung on him to weigh him down, then the jaw returns immediately. And it may also be reduced in another way.
And that is to fix in the ground a long piece of wood, the head of which is round in shape like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin, then it is placed under the patient's armpit after placing a soft cloth on the head of the wood, and the patient standing along the length of the wood.
Then you extend his hand downwards from the other side, and he also extends his body from the other side with force, then the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce it by all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: that you take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is about four fingers and its thickness about two fingers, it has a round head to facilitate its entry into the depth of the armpit in this manner:
Then a soft cloth is tied to the round head lest the wood hurt the patient, then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are extended on the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbeam.  The crossbeam is placed horizontally, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side, then the joint enters immediately. If it enters in any way possible, then a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder as it circles from above, then the ball is tightly bound under the armpit, and the bandage is wound over the bandage from above, and his hand is hung to his neck.
He is left without moving his hand for seven days, and the patient's food should be light so that the limb may strengthen, for this is quicker for its healing. Then, after seven or five days, it is unbound and exercised; if it is firm and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to dampness or some other reason, then cauterization with three cautery irons should be used, as described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this has been done and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint is not stable,
the bandage and binding have been repeated many times and it has not become stable, and it has fallen and relaxed and you cannot raise it upwards, then know that the nerve in the head of the humerus has been severed, or stretched, or relaxed, then know that the joint will never remain in place.
As for the dislocation which is towards the chest and breast and to the rear, its reduction is by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns, and all the binding and treatment is used until it heals. If, after healing, there is stiffness in the limb and slowness in movement,
let the patient use the bath many times until that stiffness softens and returns to its former nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
In the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow joint is difficult to dislocate and also difficult to reduce; it is dislocated in all directions, especially forwards or backwards.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, because it is under sight and touch, whichever way it is dislocated.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, that will be clearly apparent to you by the deformity of the joint; and he cannot bend the arm nor touch his shoulder with it. And you should hasten to reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot inflammation occurs.
For if a hot inflammation occurs, reduction is difficult, and perhaps it will never heal completely; especially if the dislocation is backward, for that is the worst of all types of dislocation and the most painful, and frequently death follows.
And its reduction, if it is of a kind that can be reduced, is that an attendant extends his hand with both hands and his arm extended, and the physician's hands above and below the elbow, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both his hands, or with the base of his palms, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is forward, it may be reduced by bending the hand sharply until the base of the hand strikes the corresponding shoulder; and if the dislocation does not return, use strong traction, that is to say that two attendants extend the arm.
And two attendants also hold the patient, lest he move during traction; then the arm is rotated to every side, after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around his hand. And when the physician touches the joint with his hands, he should anoint them with oil, so that it helps in moving the joint easily.
Then he pushes the joint strongly until it returns; and after its return, a bandage containing compression and desiccation, with egg white, should be applied tightly, and the arm is suspended from the patient's neck, and it is left for several days.
Then it is untied; and if the joint is firm in its place, untie the bandage from it and leave it. And if you see that the joint is not strengthened sufficiently, then reapply the bandage and leave it also for several days until it is strengthened, then untie it.  And if swelling occurs after the return of the joint and its unbinding,
and there is difficulty in movement, then use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and rubbing until it softens; or place on the joint a ram's testicle, then bind it and leave it on for a day and a night, then remove it and put him in the bath; and when he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then reapply the testicle once, twice, and three times, with the bath, until it softens. And if you wish to apply moist, light cow dung with fat and bind it on, do that several times, for it softens and returns to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints; however, one should hasten to reduce it at the time it dislocates, before the place becomes swollen or hot swelling appears.
To reduce it, place the patient's wrist on a board, and let an assistant extend the arm.  The physician should place his hand on the prominence of the joint and push until it returns; however, he should observe whether the dislocation has shifted towards the palm of the hand.
Let the patient place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction. If the dislocation is prominent towards the back of the hand, let him place the palm of his hand on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same prominence of the joint. If it returns immediately, well and good; otherwise, bind it with a bandage which soothes the swelling.
Leave it and do not manipulate it again, for it will not tolerate it, and you will not be able to reduce it after several days have passed, except that the joint will remain crooked, and this will not harm the patient except insofar as the hand will become weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has been severed or bruised, and there is no remedy for it except cauterization, which may or may not be effective. When the wrist has been reduced, apply the bandage we have described, and then bind it tightly and leave it for five days.
Then unbind it and exercise the hand. If its movement is difficult or there is any stiffness, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter Twenty-Nine
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may be dislocated in any direction. If a finger is dislocated towards the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns to place. Then bind the end of the finger and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight that day. If it is night, bind it in the same manner; continue to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bind it towards that same direction and do as you did before until it heals.  Do likewise if it is dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
If a vertebra of the vertebrae of the back or neck is dislocated, a complete dislocation, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment for it, for death quickly comes to the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's stool is passed involuntarily, he cannot restrain it, and often some of his limbs become relaxed, either his legs or his arms, or one of them. But if only one vertebra is displaced from its place, it often returns on its own.
And its displacement is in four directions; that which is displaced backwards is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should look: if the hump has occurred since childhood, there is no treatment for it and no cure at all.  But that which has occurred from a fall or a blow, and the like...
For the ancients mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which are of no benefit. I have summarized from that what suffices, a little for much of what they presented, by drawing closer to the meaning and explaining it, and I have depicted the instrument differently from what they explained and described.
I say that the hump which occurs in the front of the chest has no remedy and no cure, and so also that which is to the two sides. Only that which occurs in the back is treated, specifically with what I describe, which is to stretch the patient on his face.
on a level bench near a wall, and spread a wet mat beneath him so that his chest is not hurt, then place an upright wooden post fixed in a hole in the ground at the end of the bench towards his head, and another wooden post towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the wooden post, and let it not be fixed in the hole, and another servant holds the other in that manner. Then wrap around the chest of the patient and under his armpits with a soft, tight bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the wooden post at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another bandage above his hips and above his knees and at his ankles, then gather all the bandages and tie them to the other wooden post at his feet. Then each servant pulls his wooden post with the bandage, and the two wooden posts do not move from their fixed positions.
Except that they are not fixed, as we said. And the physician places his hands on the hump with force until it returns, or he places a board on it, then he leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits.
And dig in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, a place into which the end of the board is inserted, then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until he presses the hump.
And it returns to its place. And if you wish to make it with a screw that is twisted by hand, it is that you plant in the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the shop two wooden posts, their length being a cubit, and at his feet at the end of the shop also two wooden posts.
Let the distance between each post be a span. And a hole is made in each post through which the screw passes. And all the wood is firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all. And insert a round rod, which is the screw in which the bandage is twisted, into the holes of the two posts.
And at its end is a hole in which a rod a span long is fixed by twisting. And in the other two posts the same. Then tighten the bandage which you tightened on the chest of the patient in the screw which is at his head, and the bandages which you tightened on his legs in the screw which is at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw twisting his hand with the handle with which the screw is twisted, and the physician straightens the hump as we have explained. And this is a picture of the screw, the shop, and the patient:
Then, after the vertebra returns and the place is straightened, it is necessary to apply a drying compress with egg white, then lint, then place on top of the compress a splint of wood whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length is as much as the length of the hump.
And on some sound beads, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses light food until he recovers. If some protrusion remains in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary to use medicines that relax and soften, along with the use of the splint which we described for a long time.
And a lead plate may be used for that. And a protrusion may appear at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it may be thought to be a dislocation, and it may be an extra bone that has protruded.  Do not treat it with this treatment, for perhaps death may result.
Chapter Thirty-One.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and are not subject to what happens to the other joints of slight displacement and depression, and the hip joint dislocates in four ways.
That is because it dislocates inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards, and it most often dislocates inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards.  And the sign of its dislocation inwards is that if you compare the sound leg of the patient with the affected one, the sound leg will be longer.
And the knee will be more flexed than the sound one, and the patient is not able to bend his leg at the groin, and the area near the groin will be clearly swollen because the head of the femur has gone there.
And the sign of that which is dislocated outwards is that its symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms. And the sign of that which is dislocated forwards is that he extends his leg completely, except that he cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he wants to walk he cannot do so forwards.
And he retains his urine, and his groin swells, and when walking he puts his weight on his heel. And the sign of that which is dislocated backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly at the position of the loin. As for the reduction of the types of this dislocation, you should look; if the dislocation is old, has lasted a long time with its owner, and no one has attempted to reduce it, and it has remained in its state, then there is absolutely no treatment for it.
Then it should not be treated. As for that which is recently dislocated, and whatever its type may be of the four types of dislocation, then hasten to twist the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, perhaps...
it returned and did not need any other treatment. If it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong, capable assistant who extends his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around his leg above the knee, and another assistant who extends him from above.
so that he puts his hands under his armpits, then he tightens with a soft bandage on the root of the thigh, and another third assistant holds the end of the bandage, and the extension is either from the front, from the side of the groin to the side of the clavicle, or from behind to the side of the back.
And their extension is all at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended.  For this type of extension is a common type for the four types. If the dislocation returns as we said, otherwise, each type needs what I mention of special treatment.
As for its specific reduction, if the dislocation is inwards, then the patient should lie on his sound side, then a bandage is applied to the root of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the place under the groin.
Then the bandage is extended upwards from the groin to the upper body towards the clavicle, then another strong, capable assistant takes it with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a strong pull, then it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced. If it is difficult for you and it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then you should bind the patient's legs together with a strong, soft bandage on the heels and on the knees.
And the distance between each one and its partner is four fingers, and the affected leg is extended more than the other by two fingers. Then the patient is suspended by the head from a wooden beam in the house, and its distance from the ground is two cubits.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh, and another boy hangs onto the patient, and the other boy embracing the thigh pushes strongly, then the joint returns to its place quickly. As for its specific reduction if the dislocation is outwards.
Then the patient should lie on the bench as we described for the hunchback, and the bandage is tightened on his affected leg in particular and on his chest. Then two pieces of wood are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head, then an extra piece of wood is placed in the middle of the bench, very firmly secured.
A wet cloth is wrapped around it so that it does not harm the patient, so that the wood is between his thighs so that he does not be pulled downwards when stretched. Then each servant pulls towards his side and the physician adjusts the hip with his hands; if it responds to reduction.
Otherwise, place the board on him and press it down as we mentioned in the case of the hunchback, except that the patient should lie on his sound side. And if the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg should be extended once while he is in this same position on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected groin, then presses it with the other hand, and with that he makes the pressure extended downwards towards the knee. And if the dislocation is backwards, then the patient should not be extended downwards.
And it is raised from the ground, but rather it should be placed on something firm, as it should also be from the dislocation of his hip outwards, according to what we mentioned of his lying on the bench and he is on his face and the bandages are tight.
As we said before, and the pressing with the board should also be used on the place to which the joint has gone out.  Then, if the hip joint is completely reduced as it should be—and the sign of its reduction will not be hidden from you—which is that you extend the patient's legs.
Then if you see them even and the patient bends and extends the leg without difficulty, know that the member has returned as it should.  Then, join the thighs and apply the bandage, and bind it with a turban tightly so that the hip does not move in any direction.
And the patient must remain still for three or four days. Then untie the bandage, and compare the leg with the other. If you see them equal in size, know that the fracture has healed, so release the patient to walk. And if you see in it some relaxation…
then return and bandage it and tighten it according to your first tightening, and also leave it for three days, then untie it, and he should walk slowly on it for days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
In the treatment of a fracture of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards – that is, backwards; it does not dislocate forwards at all.  And the sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to bring his leg to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. And the treatment for all types of its dislocation is that you seat the patient sitting with his legs extended if he is strong enough for that, and a servant sits behind him to hold his waist and twist him slightly backwards, then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back against his face and place his leg between your legs, then you grasp his knee with your hands and interlock them with your fingers on his knee, then you press with your hands on the sides of his knee forcefully, and another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh softly and without force. Then bandage it and fasten the leg to the thigh, then bind them both together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. And he should only do a little walking for some days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it by what we described, then use strong traction with the bandages that I previously described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may be slightly dislocated, or it may be completely dislocated.  Its dislocation is either inward or outward.  The sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and prominent in the direction in which it was dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight displacement, it is easy to reduce it.  This is done by gently extending it with the hands and straightening it until it returns.  As for its treatment if it is completely dislocated, then it is necessary that you seat the patient sitting, and a strong servant holds him from behind his back at his waist.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from above, and with your left hand from below the foot at the place of the heel bone. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with the left twice, then extend it with the left and push the front of the foot with the right towards the leg without violence.
Do this twice as we described, then push the front of the foot to the leg on the third time while you pull on the heel. If it returns in one or two times in this manner and you see the foot straight, otherwise repeat the process, for it will return.
If it refuses to return by what we described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground, and plant a firmly fixed stake in the ground so that it falls between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it lest it harm the patient, then a servant should hold his thigh.
Then another servant extends the leg, either with his hands or with a rope that he ties around the neck of the leg. Then each servant extends in opposition to the pull of his companion, and the stake stands between the patient's thighs holding it so that his body does not slip downwards during extension, then the physician sets the joint with his hands.
And another servant holds the healthy leg below, for the joint returns quickly.  When the joint returns and you see that its return is correct, apply the bandage and splint and fasten it with tight bandages and bind the foot with a bandage below.  It is necessary to protect the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
Lest the bandage be too tight and harm him. Then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it, then release it on the third or fourth day.  The patient must abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be sure that the joint will not dislocate again and be ruined, and will not then accept treatment. If a hot swelling appears, then you should use what we previously described in another place for treatment and soothing until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
In the treatment of the dislocation of the toes of the foot.
You should set what has dislocated with a gentle pull without force; this is not difficult, but easy. If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the instep, then the patient should place his foot on a level surface of the ground.
Or on a board while he is standing as if walking; then you place your foot on what is dislocated from those joints and then press down on it with your foot strongly until it returns and you see that it is straight and no prominence appears in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board which takes the whole foot and has two ends, then bind it tightly and firmly for three days, then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are safe from recurrence, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation which occurs with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When something of that happens and you attempt its treatment and setting, it often results in death.
Therefore, no one should undertake the treatment of such a thing except one who is skillful in the art, long experienced, delicate, gentle, careful, not rash nor impetuous, and that he should use in the beginning only those medicines which calm hot swellings and leave the patient alone.
As for the rest, O Lord, except what you hoped for its safety from harm, with the mildness of the disease and some hope appeared to you in it, then return it from its place at the first, before the hot swelling occurs.
If the limb returns to what you intended, then use the treatment which calms hot swellings and treat the wound with whatever is suitable for it from drying ointments; if the dislocation is with a fracture and there are separated splinters in the bone, then remove them. And follow in that what we mentioned in the simple diseases, what preceded in their places.
And strive your best and refrain from entering the path of danger, as I advised you before; that is more lasting for your reputation and safer for your honor, God willing.
